The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
When the Negative Premise is Particular in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains why a particular negative premise yields no conclusion in disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a particular negative premise in a disjunctive syllogism yields no conclusion.

Core Idea
A particular negative premise disables the disjunctive syllogism; no conclusion can be drawn.

This lesson explores the rule that when one premise is a particular negation, the disjunctive syllogism fails to produce a conclusion. The author explains the reasoning for both inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) and exclusive (man'at al-jam') disjunctions, using the concepts of contradiction (ta'and) and entailment (talazum). Understanding this rule prevents invalid inferences in compound syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative premise as particular
The text explicitly states: 'فإن كانت السالبة جزئية لا ينتج' (if the negative is particular, nothing is produced). Recognize that the negativity is limited (juz'iyya), meaning it does not apply universally.
In disjunctive syllogisms, a particular negative is too weak to force a conclusion.
2
Analyze the inclusive disjunction case
For an inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو), the author considers two subcases: with contradiction (تعاند) and with entailment (تلازم). In both, the more specific term of the contradictory may not align with the other term, preventing a valid inference. Example: 'Always either this is a human or a horse, and it may not be either a horse or not a human.'
The specific example shows that the conclusion does not necessarily follow.
3
Analyze the exclusive disjunction case
For an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع), again with contradiction and entailment. The more general term of the contradictory may be true with the contradictory, preventing a valid inference. Example: 'Always either not not a human or an animal (inclusive), and it may not be either an animal or a human (exclusive).'
The asymmetry in generality blocks a definite conclusion.
4
Generalize the outcome
In all cases with a particular negative, no conclusion is produced. The syllogism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت إحديهما سالبة. فإن كانت السالبة جزئية لا ينتج. من أما إذا كانت مانعة الخلو فلِصِدْقِ القياس مع التعاند تارة ومع التلازم أخرى أما مع التعاند فلأن الأخص من نقيض الشيء يمكن أن لا يصدق مع نقيضه كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون هذا الشيء إنسانًا أو فرسا، وقد لا يكون إما أن يكون فرسًا أو لا إنسانًا. وأما مع التلازم فلأن الأخص من نقيض الشيء يمكن أن لا يصدق مع اللازم المساوي كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إما أن يكون فرسا أو ناطقا . وأما إذا كانت مانعة الجمع إما مع التعاند فلأن الأعم من نقيض الشيء يمكن أن يصدق مع نقيض ذلك الشيء كقولنا دائما؛ إما أن لا يكون لا إنسانًا أو حيوانًا مانعا من الخلو، وقد لا يكون إما أن يكون حيوانا أو إنسانًا مانعة من الجمع. وإما مع التلازم فلأن الأعم من نقيض الشيء يمكن أن يصدق مع التلازم المساوي لذلك الشيء كقولنا قد لا يكون إما أن يكون حيوانًا أو لا ناطقاً مانعا من الجمع مع التلازم بين الإنسان والناطق. وأما إذا كانت السالبة كلية فإن كانت مانعة الخلو كلية كانت مانعة الخلو أو جزئية تنتج سالبة جزئية مقدمها طرف مانعة الجمع كما في قولنا دائمًا أو قد يكون إما أن يكون (أب) أو (ج) د مانعا من الجمع، وليس البتة إما أن يكون (ج) د) «أب» د» «ج د» أو «هـز» فإنه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ هز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وذلك مع قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن (ج) د » فـ «أب» ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن «ج د» فـ هـز». ويلزمه قد يكون إما (ج) د» أو «هـ ز»؛ وهو خلف. وإن كانت السالبة مانعة الجمع ينتج سالبة جزئية مع نقيض الطرفين مقدمها نقيض طرف مانعة الخلو كلية كانت أو جزئية لأنه إذا صدق قولنا دائما أو قد يكون إما أن يكون «أب» أو «ج ده مانعا من الخلو، وليس البتة إما أن يكون جد» أو «هز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا لم يكن أب» لم يكن هز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وإنه م ، مع قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د لم يكن «أب» يفضي إلى الخلف. وإن كانت مانعة الخلو كلية تنتج متّصلة طرفها نقيض طرف مانعة الجمع وإلا لصدق نقیضه ؛ وذلك يفضي إلى الخلف. وعلى هذا إذا كانت السالبة كلية والجزئية اللازمة من الموجبتين وكيف ما كان المقدم من الطرفين إن كانت الكلية مانعة الخلو، ومن نقيضها إن كانت مانعة الجمع واللازمة؛ وإحديهما سالبة كلية من الطرفين أو من نقيضهما. وكيف ما كان المقدم من طرف الموجبة إن كانت الموجبة كلية ، ومن نقيضه إن كانت جزئية يرتد إلى المنفصلتين؛ والمتصلة من الطرفين بالطريق الذي عرفته.
And if one of the two premises is negative. If the negative is particular, nothing is produced. As for when it is an inclusive disjunction, due to the soundness of the syllogism sometimes with contradiction and sometimes with entailment. As for with contradiction, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing may not be true with its contradictory, as in our saying: 'Always either this thing is a human or a horse, and it may not be either a horse or not a human.' As for with entailment, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing may not be true with the equal necessary, as in our saying: 'It may not be either a horse or a speaker.' As for when it is an exclusive disjunction, either with contradiction because the more general of the contradictory of a thing may be true with the contradictory of that thing, as in our saying: 'Always either not not a human or an animal, inclusive, and it may not be either an animal or a human, exclusive.' And either with entailment because the more general of the contradictory of a thing may be true with the equal necessary of that thing, as in our saying: 'It may not be either an animal or not a speaker, exclusive, with entailment between human and speaker.' And if the negative is universal, then if it is an inclusive disjunction, whether it is universal inclusive or particular, it produces a particular negative whose antecedent is the term of the exclusive disjunction, as in our saying: 'Always or sometimes either (A-B) or (C-D) exclusive, and not at all either (C-D) or (H-Z),' then it produces: 'Sometimes if (A-B) then (H-Z),' otherwise its contradictory is true. And with our saying: 'Sometimes if not (C-D) then (A-B),' it produces: 'Sometimes if not (C-D) then (H-Z).' And it entails: 'Sometimes either (C-D) or (H-Z),' which is a contradiction. And if the negative is an exclusive disjunction, it produces a particular negative with the contradictories of the two terms, whose antecedent is the contradictory of the term of the inclusive disjunction, whether universal or particular, because if our saying is true: 'Always or sometimes either (A-B) or (C-D) inclusive, and not at all either (C-D) or (H-Z),' it produces: 'It may not be that if not (A-B) then not (H-Z),' otherwise its contradictory is true. And that, with our saying: 'Sometimes if (C-D) then not (A-B),' leads to a contradiction. And if the inclusive disjunction is universal, it produces a conditional whose consequent is the contradictory of the term of the exclusive disjunction, otherwise its contradictory is true, and that leads to a contradiction. And so if the negative is universal and the particular is necessary from the two affirmatives, and however the antecedent from the two terms is, if the universal is inclusive, and from its contradictory if it is exclusive and necessary, and one of them is a universal negative from the two terms or from their contradictories, and however the antecedent from the term of the affirmative is, if the affirmative is universal, and from its contradictory if it is particular, it reduces to the two disjunctives, and the conditional from the two terms in the way you know.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage covers the rules when one premise is negative. It begins by stating that if the negative is particular, no conclusion follows. Then it explains why for inclusive and exclusive disjunctions, providing examples using terms like 'human', 'horse', 'speaker'. The reasoning involves whether the terms are more specific or more general relative to contradictories and entailment. When the negative is universal, the passage details multiple subcases: if the negative is inclusive (whether universal or particular), it produces a particular negative conditional with the antecedent being one term, and gives an example with variables (A-B, C-D, H-Z). It shows how the conclusion follows and what would lead to a contradiction. Similarly for exclusive disjunction. Finally, it summarizes that the rules reduce to disjunctive premises, and the form of the conditional is known from earlier.

Key Takeaway
A particular negative premise in a disjunctive syllogism always fails to produce a conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following premises: 'Always either the liquid is pure or impure' (inclusive), and 'It is not at all the case that it is sometimes impure' (particular negative). According to the rule, can any conclusion be drawn? Explain why or why not.
?

When the Negative Premise is Universal in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains the production rules when one premise is a universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a disjunctive syllogism when one premise is a universal negative, given the type of disjunction.

Core Idea
A universal negative premise transforms the disjunctive syllogism into a conditional conclusion, depending on whether the disjunction is inclusive or exclusive.

When the negative premise is universal, the disjunctive syllogism becomes productive. This lesson details the rules for both inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) and exclusive (man'at al-jam') disjunctions, showing how the conclusion takes the form of a conditional proposition. The passage uses variables (أب, ج د, هز) to illustrate the patterns, and the author verifies the conclusions by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Confirm the negative premise is universal
The text focuses on cases where the negative is universal (salibah kulliyyah). Identify that the negation applies to all members of the disjunct.
This is the only case where a conclusion is produced.
2
Identify the disjunction type of the positive premise
Determine whether the positive premise is an inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) or an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع). The rules differ.
3
Apply the rule for inclusive disjunction
If the positive premise is inclusive, the conclusion is a particular negative conditional: 'Sometimes if (term from negative) then (consequent).' The author gives an example with variables: 'Always or sometimes either (A-B) or (C-D) exclusive, and not at all either (C-D) or (H-Z)' yields 'Sometimes if (A-B) then (H-Z)'. Follow the logic of contradiction to verify.
The conclusion is always particular (juz'iyya) even if the premises are universal.
4
Apply the rule for exclusive disjunction
If the positive premise is exclusive, the conclusion is a particular negative conditional with the antecedent being the contradictory of the term from the negative premise. The author shows that if 'Always or sometimes either (A-B) or (C-D) inclusive, and not at all either (C-D) or (H-Z)' then 'It may not be that if not (A-B) then not (H-Z)'.
Note the use of contradictories.
5
Verify by contradiction
The author uses the method of assuming the opposite (خلف) to confirm the conclusion. Understand that if the conclusion were false, it would lead to a contradiction with the premises.

Source Text

And if one of the two premises is negative. If the negative is particular, nothing is produced. As for when it is an inclusive disjunction, due to the soundness of the syllogism sometimes with contradiction and sometimes with entailment. As for with contradiction, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing may not be true with its contradictory, as in our saying: 'Always either this thing is a human or a horse, and it may not be either a horse or not a human.' As for with entailment, because the more specific of the contradictory of a thing may not be true with the equal necessary, as in our saying: 'It may not be either a horse or a speaker.' As for when it is an exclusive disjunction, either with contradiction because the more general of the contradictory of a thing may be true with the contradictory of that thing, as in our saying: 'Always either not not a human or an animal, inclusive, and it may not be either an animal or a human, exclusive.' And either with entailment because the more general of the contradictory of a thing may be true with the equal necessary of that thing, as in our saying: 'It may not be either an animal or not a speaker, exclusive, with entailment between human and speaker.' And if the negative is universal, then if it is an inclusive disjunction, whether it is universal inclusive or particular, it produces a particular negative whose antecedent is the term of the exclusive disjunction, as in our saying: 'Always or sometimes either (A-B) or (C-D) exclusive, and not at all either (C-D) or (H-Z),' then it produces: 'Sometimes if (A-B) then (H-Z),' otherwise its contradictory is true. And with our saying: 'Sometimes if not (C-D) then (A-B),' it produces: 'Sometimes if not (C-D) then (H-Z).' And it entails: 'Sometimes either (C-D) or (H-Z),' which is a contradiction. And if the negative is an exclusive disjunction, it produces a particular negative with the contradictories of the two terms, whose antecedent is the contradictory of the term of the inclusive disjunction, whether universal or particular, because if our saying is true: 'Always or sometimes either (A-B) or (C-D) inclusive, and not at all either (C-D) or (H-Z),' it produces: 'It may not be that if not (A-B) then not (H-Z),' otherwise its contradictory is true. And that, with our saying: 'Sometimes if (C-D) then not (A-B),' leads to a contradiction. And if the inclusive disjunction is universal, it produces a conditional whose consequent is the contradictory of the term of the exclusive disjunction, otherwise its contradictory is true, and that leads to a contradiction. And so if the negative is universal and the particular is necessary from the two affirmatives, and however the antecedent from the two terms is, if the universal is inclusive, and from its contradictory if it is exclusive and necessary, and one of them is a universal negative from the two terms or from their contradictories, and however the antecedent from the term of the affirmative is, if the affirmative is universal, and from its contradictory if it is particular, it reduces to the two disjunctives, and the conditional from the two terms in the way you know.

The Five Types of Incomplete Shared Part
Author lists the five divisions when the shared part is incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of incomplete shared part in conditional syllogisms based on the number of shared parts.

Core Idea
When premises share an incomplete part, the classification depends on how many parts are shared.

In the second main division of mixed syllogisms, the two premises do not share a complete term but rather an incomplete part. The author enumerates five possible patterns of such sharing. This classification is fundamental for analyzing compound conditional arguments in Islamic logic, especially those used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the main division
Recall that in mixed syllogisms, the premises may share a complete term (first division) or an incomplete part (second division). This lesson focuses on the latter.
The incomplete part could be a part of a conditional proposition, such as the antecedent or consequent.
2
Recognize the fivefold classification
The author explicitly states that there are five divisions (khamsati aqsam). Although only three are given in the surviving text, the complete list would include all five patterns.
The enumeration uses Arabic letters or numbers; the third is shown as [ت].
3
Analyze the first type
In the first type, one part of one premise shares only one part of the other premise. This means a single component from each premise is common, and no additional sharing occurs.
For example, if premise 1 has parts A and B, and premise 2 has parts C and D, then A shares with C only.
4
Analyze the second type
In the second type, one part of one premise shares each of the two parts of the other premise. That is, a single component from the first premise is common to both components of the second premise.
For example, part A of premise 1 is shared with both parts C and D of premise 2.
5
Analyze the third type
In the third type, one of the two parts of one premise shares one of the two parts of the other premise. This is a more specific pattern where exactly one part from each premise shares with one part from the other.
The text cuts off here; the remaining two types are not provided in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يكون الاشتراك بين المقدمتين في جزء غير تام، فلك على خمسة أقسام لأنه [1] إما أن يشارك جزء واحد من إحديهما جزءًا واحدًا من الأخرى فقط، [ب] أو يشارك جزء واحد منهما من إحديهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، [ت] أو يشارك أحد جزئي إحديهما أحد جزئي الأخرى
And as for the second division, which is that the sharing between the two premises is in an incomplete part, it has five divisions: [1] either one part of one premise shares only one part of the other, [b] or one part of one premise shares each of the two parts of the other, [t] or one of the two parts of one premise shares one of the two parts of the other.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage introduces the second main division of mixed syllogisms, which deals with cases where the shared part between the two premises is not a complete term but an incomplete part. The author enumerates five types (though only three are listed in the surviving text). The first type is when one part of one premise shares only one part of the other premise. The second is when one part of one premise shares both parts of the other premise. The third is when one part of one premise shares one part of the other premise. These correspond to different patterns of shared components in conditional propositions. The remaining two types are presumably given in the continuation.

Key Terms
اشتراك
جزء غير تام

Key Takeaway
The second division of mixed syllogisms involves five patterns of incomplete part sharing, with three examples given: one-to-one, one-to-two, and one-to-one (from one part of each).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Five Modes of Imperfect Sharing in Mixed Conditional Syllogisms
Enumeration of five subdivisions of imperfect premise sharing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish each of the five modes of imperfect partial sharing between two compound premises.

Core Idea
Five modes of partial premise sharing exhaust all combinatorial possibilities in mixed conditional syllogisms.

In the previous lesson we learned about perfect sharing (where the whole premise is shared). Now we turn to the second major category: sharing only a part of a premise. The author systematically enumerates five distinct modes of such partial sharing, which are essential for constructing valid mixed syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
First mode: one-to-one partial sharing
Find the case where a single part of the first premise shares exactly one part of the other premise, with no other sharing.
This is the simplest form of imperfect sharing.
2
Second mode: one part shares both parts of the other
Identify the case where one component of the first premise is identical to both components of the second premise.
Here the shared part is more extensive.
3
Third mode: two-part cross-sharing
Recognize the symmetrical case where each part of one premise shares one corresponding part of the other.
This is a one-to-one mapping but may involve two separate shared parts.
4
Fourth mode: full cross-sharing
Note the case where every part of each premise shares every part of the other premise (four shared relationships).
This is the most complete form of imperfect sharing.
5
Fifth mode: mixed sharing
Observe the asymmetrical case where one part of the first premise shares both parts of the second, while the other part shares only one part of the second.
This combines features of modes 2 and 1.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يكون الاشتراك بين المقدمتين في جزء غير تام، فلك على خمسة أقسام لأنه [1] إما أن يشارك جزء واحد من إحديهما جزءًا واحدًا من الأخرى فقط، [ب] أو يشارك جزء واحد منهما من إحديهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، [ت] أو يشارك أحد جزئي إحديهما أحد جزئي الأخرى، والجزء الآخر للجزء الآخر، [ث] أو يشارك كل واحد من جزأي كل واحد منهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، [ج] أو يشارك أحد حزئي إحديهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، والجزء الآخر من الأولى لأحد جزأي الأخرى فقط.
As for the second section, which is that the sharing between the two premises is in an imperfect part, then there are five subdivisions: [1] either one part of one premise shares only one part of the other, [b] or one part of one of the two premises shares both parts of the other, [t] or one of the two parts of one premise shares one of the two parts of the other, and the other part (shares) the other part, [th] or each of the two parts of each of the two premises shares each of the two parts of the other, [j] or one of the two parts of one premise shares both parts of the other, and the other part of the first (premise) shares only one of the two parts of the other.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification. The author identifies the second major section of mixed conditional syllogisms, where the sharing between the two premises is in an imperfect part (i.e., a part that is not the whole premise, such as a component of a disjunctive or conditional). He then enumerates five distinct modes of such sharing. The first mode: one part of one premise shares exactly one part of the other premise. The second: one part of one premise shares both parts of the other premise. The third: each part of one premise shares a corresponding part of the other premise (one-to-one). The fourth: every part of each premise shares every part of the other (full cross-sharing). The fifth: one part of the first premise shares both parts of the second, while the other part of the first shares only one part of the second. These five modes cover all possibilities of imperfect partial sharing between two complex premises. The student should memorize this enumeration and understand the combinatorial logic behind it.

The First Subdivision Produces a Three-Part Inclusive Disjunction
Author explains first subdivision yields three-part inclusive disjunction with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a three-part inclusive disjunction from given conditional premises using the pattern described in the source.

Core Idea
The first subdivision always yields an inclusive disjunction of three parts, never an exclusive one.

In this passage, the author explains how the first subdivision of compound syllogisms works. He shows that combining two conditional premises with shared and non-shared parts always produces an inclusive disjunction with three disjuncts. The key insight is that the conclusion follows necessarily from the structure of the premises, regardless of the specific terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of premises
Recognize that each premise is an eternal disjunction (dā'iman immā...) of the form 'Always either P or Q'. The two premises share a term or part of a term.
In the text, premises are labeled with variables like 'A is B or A is C'.
2
Extract shared and non-shared parts
In each example, locate which terms are common to both premises and which are unique. The shared parts combine to form part of the conclusion; the non-shared parts are included as separate disjuncts.
For the first example, the shared parts are 'A is B' and 'A is C' (both have 'A'). The non-shared are 'C is D' and 'H is Z'.
3
Form the three-part disjunction
The conclusion is an eternal disjunction of three parts: the shared part from the first premise, the shared part from the second premise, and one of the non-shared parts (either from the first or second premise, depending on structure). The pattern ensures that at least one is true.
In the first example, the conclusion is 'Always either all A is B or all A is D or all H is Z'.
4
Verify it is inclusive, not exclusive
The conclusion is maniat al-khulu (inclusive disjunction) meaning multiple disjuncts can be true simultaneously. The author notes it never produces maniat al-jam' (exclusive) because the result is more general and does not exclude combinations.
This is a key logical property of the first subdivision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the syllogism is formed in each of the four figures in each of these five sections, and the minor premise is distinguished from the major by considering the position of the middle term between the shared parts. [1] And the first section produces an inclusive disjunction (maniat al-khulu) composed of three parts, which is the result of combining the shared parts with the non-shared parts, due to the necessity of the impossibility of vacuity in reality of the sum of the two parts that are the premises of the syllogism, and of one of the non-shared parts, whether the part of each of the two premises shares a part, or the part of one of the two premises shares a part without the two parts of the first premise, or no part of one of them shares a part at all. As for the first example: 'Always either all A is B or all A is C, and always either all C is D or all H is Z' always yields 'Always either all A is B or all A is D or all H is Z'. And the second example: in our statement 'Always either all A is B or all A is C, and always either all C is D or H is Z' always yields 'Always either all A is B or all A is D or H is Z'. And the third example: 'Always either all A is B or all (C) D, and always either all D H or all H Z' always yields 'Always either all A is B or all C D or all H Z'. And likewise for the remaining moods of the first figure and other figures similarly, for it does not differ in any of them. And it does not produce an exclusive disjunction (maniat al-jam'), not this section nor any other, because it is possible that the result of composition, which is the necessary consequence of composition, is more general than the composition and does not conflict with any of the non-shared parts, such that the syllogism contains one composition; and also because no result of compositions should contain more than one composition.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the first subdivision of the second section, explaining its logical behavior. First, the author notes that syllogisms with imperfect sharing can be formed in any of the four figures, with the minor and major distinguished by the position of the middle term among the shared parts. He then states that the first subdivision always produces an inclusive disjunction (maniat al-khulu) of three parts. The result is formed by combining the shared part(s) with the non-shared parts from both premises, ensuring that in reality at least one of the three components is true. He provides three concrete examples using abstract variables (A, B, C, D, H, Z). In each example, the premises are two eternal disjunctions (dā'iman immā...), and the conclusion is also an eternal disjunction of three terms. The pattern is consistent across all figures and moods. Finally, the author notes that this section never yields an exclusive disjunction (maniat al-jam'), because the result is a necessary consequence that is more general and does not exclude any combination. The student should trace each example to see how the shared and non-shared parts combine.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو (maniat al-khulu): Inclusive disjunction: a compound proposition that asserts that at least one of its disjuncts is true, allowing for all to be true. [Note: In later logic, maniat al-khulu is consistently used for inclusive disjunction.]
مانعة الجمع (maniat al-jam'): Exclusive disjunction: a compound proposition that asserts that at least one disjunct is true but not more than one. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
نتيجة التأليف (natījat al-ta'līf): The conclusion that necessarily follows from the combination (ta'līf) of the premises' shared and non-shared parts. [Note: Specific to this text's analysis of compound syllogisms.]

The first subdivision of compound syllogisms always yields a three-part inclusive disjunction, never an exclusive one.

Five Divisions of Compound Syllogisms Based on Participation
Author enumerates five divisions of compound syllogisms based on participation of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five divisions of compound syllogisms based on the participation relation between premises and determine which relation applies to a given example.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms are classified by how their premise parts participate; each division works in any figure.

This lesson examines the classification of compound syllogisms (qiyas murakkab) into five types according to the relation of participation (musharaka) between the subject and predicate of each premise. The author describes three specific relations and states that each division can be validly formed in any of the four syllogistic figures. Understanding these divisions helps you parse complex arguments where multiple syllogisms are combined, a common technique in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the participants
Each premise has two parts: subject and predicate. The participation relation describes how the parts of the first premise relate to the parts of the second premise. Note that the author uses 'part' (جزء) to refer to these components.
2
Parse the first relation (A)
The text begins with 'الجزء الآخر للجزء الآخر' (the other part to the other part). This indicates a simple pairing: one part of the first premise corresponds to one part of the second premise. This is likely the first division.
The three relations given are A, B, and J as marked in the Arabic.
3
Parse the second relation (B)
The text says 'يشارك كل واحد من جزأي كل واحد منهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى' meaning each part of each premise participates with each part of the other. This is full participation – every part of the first premise relates to every part of the second.
4
Parse the third relation (J)
The text says 'يشارك أحد جزئي إحديهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، والجزء الآخر من الأولى لأحد جزأي الأخرى فقط'. This means one part of the first premise participates with both parts of the second, while the other part of the first participates with only one part of the second. This is a partial participation.
The author mentions five divisions but only describes three; the remaining two are likely the mirror of relation J.
5
Apply to all four figures
The author states that the syllogism occurs in each of the four figures within each division. So regardless of which figure (shakl) you use, as long as the participation relation matches one of the five divisions, the compound syllogism can be constructed.
Distinguish the minor and major premises by the position of the middle term between the participants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجزء الآخر للجزء الآخر، [ب] أو يشارك كل واحد من جزأي كل واحد منهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، [ج] أو يشارك أحد جزئي إحديهما كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، والجزء الآخر من الأولى لأحد جزأي الأخرى فقط. وينعقد القياس في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة في كل قسم من هذه الأقسام الخمسة، وتتميز الصغرى عن الكبرى باعتبار وضع الأوسط بين المتشاركين.
And the other part to the other part, [B] or each one of the two parts of each one of them participates with each one of the parts of the other, [J] or one of the two parts of one of them participates with each one of the parts of the other, and the other part of the first is for only one of the two parts of the other. And the syllogism occurs in each of the four figures in each of these five divisions, and the minor premise is distinguished from the major by considering the position of the middle between the participants.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage classifies compound syllogisms into five divisions based on how the parts of the two premises participate with each other. The author describes three specific relations: (1) each part of each premise participates with each part of the other, (2) one part of one premise participates with both parts of the other, (3) one part of one premise participates with both parts of the other while the other part of the first participates with only one part of the other. The complete list would include all five divisions, but only three are explicitly given here, indicating the others were mentioned earlier. The author then states that the syllogism can be valid in any of the four figures within each division, and the minor and major premises are distinguished by the position of the middle term between the participants. In Fiqh, one might consider: 'Every ruling is either wajib or haram' and 'Every contract is either valid or invalid'—the participation relations determine the form of the compound syllogism.

Compound Disjunctive Syllogisms: The Second Division
Author explains the second division with three subcases and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three subcases of the second division of compound disjunctive syllogisms and construct their three-part conclusions.

Core Idea
In compound disjunctive syllogisms, the conclusion can have three parts, depending on how the premises share terms.

Compound disjunctive syllogisms involve two premises, each a perpetual disjunction (e.g., 'always either...or...'). The second division focuses on cases where parts of the premises share terms. Based on the pattern of sharing, the conclusion is an affirmative disjunction with three parts. Understanding this division sharpens your ability to analyse complex disjunctive reasoning, which appears in Islamic jurisprudential and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the two disjunctive premises in the passage. Each premise is a perpetual disjunction: 'always either every A is B or every A is C'. Note the parts (juz') of each premise.
In the text, premises are introduced with 'دائما إما أن يكون...'.
2
Classify the sharing pattern
Determine which subcase applies: (1) one part of the first premise shares with each part of the second, (2) one part shares with only one part of the second, or (3) no parts share at all. The text gives an example for each.
Sharing means the same term appears in both premises, e.g., 'C' appears in both parts of the second premise in the first example.
3
Identify the two compositions (ta'lifan)
For each subcase, pair each part of the first premise with a matching part of the second. Each pairing forms a simple categorical syllogism (first or second figure). The conclusions of these two syllogisms, plus the non-shared part, form the three parts of the final disjunction.
In the first example, the compositions are: (1) 'every A is B' and 'every C is D'? Actually careful: The text says the two compositions produce conclusions like 'every A is D' and 'every A is E' plus the non-shared part 'every A is B'? Wait, let's read the example: The conclusion is 'every A is E or every A is D or every A is E' – that seems repetitive. Probably a typo in the OCR? But we follow the text. The guided path should help the student extract the compositions.
4
Form the three-part conclusion
Combine the two conclusions from the compositions and the non-shared part into an affirmative disjunction. The result is a disjunction with three parts, as shown in the examples.
In the third subcase, the compositions are from different figures, and the parts share no common term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من هذه الأقسام وهو أن يشارك أحد جزئي إحدى مقدمتيه كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى ينتج منفصلة موجبة مركبة من ثلاثة أجزاء أيضًا وهي نتيجتا التأليفين مع الجزء الغير المشارك لامتناع خلو الواقع عن الجزء الغير المشارك وعن مقدّمتي القياسين والأجزاء الثلاثة في النتيجة تشترك في جزء واحد إذا اشترك جزء كل واحدة من المقدمتين في جزء كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون كل «أب» وإما أن يكون كل «أج»، ودائما إما أن يكون كل «ج د» وإما أن يكون كل «ج هـ» ينتج دائما إما كل «أهـ» أو كل «أد» أو كل «أهـ» لما مر . وإن اشترك جزء إحدى مقدمتيه في دون الآخرين من الأخرى كان الاشتراك بين نتيجتي التأليفين من الأجزاء الثلاثة في جزء واحد كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون كل أب وإما أن يكون كل جد، ودائما إما أن يكون كل دهـا وإما أن يكون كل دز ينتج إما كل «أب» وإما كل «ج هـ» وإما كل «ج د» . وإن لم يشترك جزء واحد من المقدمتين في جزء فلا اشتراك بين الأجزاء الثلاثة كما في قولنا دائما إما أن يكون كل أب وإما أن يكون كل «ج د»، ودائمًا إما أن يكون كل ده أو كل زد ينتج دائما إما كل «أب» وإما كل ج هـ وإما بعض دز؛ ولا بد حينئذ من كون التأليفين من شكلين مختلفين.
The second of these divisions: that one part of one of its two premises shares with each one of the parts of the other [premise] produces an affirmative disjunctive composed of three parts as well, namely the two conclusions of the two compositions together with the non-shared part, because the actual situation cannot be empty of the non-shared part and of the premises of the two syllogisms. And the three parts in the conclusion share in one part if the part of each of the two premises shares in a part, as in our saying: always either every A is B or every A is C, and always either every C is D or every C is E, produces always either every A is E or every A is D or every A is E, due to what preceded. And if the part of one of its two premises shares with [the part] other than the two [others] of the other, then the sharing between the two conclusions of the two compositions of the three parts is in one part, as in our saying: always either every A is B or every C is D, and always either every D is H or every D is Z, produces either every A is B or every C is H or every C is D. And if not one part of the two premises shares in a part, then there is no sharing among the three parts, as in our saying: always either every A is B or every C is D, and always either every D is H or every Z is D, produces always either every A is B or every C is H or some D is Z; and then it is necessary that the two compositions are from two different figures.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage describes the second division of compound disjunctive syllogisms, where each premise is a perpetual disjunction (i.e., 'always either...or...'). The author considers three subcases based on how the parts (juz') of the premises share terms. In the first subcase, one part of the first premise shares with each part of the second premise; this yields a conclusion with three parts: the two conclusions of the two compositions (the syllogisms formed by pairing parts) plus the non-shared part. The example uses letters A, B, C, D, E as term variables. In the second subcase, one part of the first premise shares with one part of the second, but not the other; then the three-part conclusion shares one part. In the third subcase, no parts share at all, so the conclusion contains three parts with no common term, and the two compositions must be from different syllogistic figures (e.g., first and second figure).

Third Division: Crosswise Sharing and Two Conclusions
Author explains the third division with crosswise sharing and three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and identify the two conclusions of the third division of compound disjunctive syllogisms using crosswise sharing.

Core Idea
Crosswise sharing yields two conclusions: one from each composition pair.

We now explore the third division of compound disjunctive syllogisms, where the sharing between premises is crosswise: the first part of the first premise shares with one part of the second, and the remaining parts also share. This pattern produces not one but two distinct conclusions, each affirming a disjunction. Understanding this structure is key to handling complex multi-premise arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the crosswise sharing pattern
Identify that the first part of the first premise shares with one part of the second premise, and the other part of the first shares with the remaining part of the second. This is distinct from the earlier divisions where sharing was one-sided or full.
The passage calls this 'the third of the divisions'.
2
Understand the two possible conclusions
The first conclusion is an affirmative disjunction composed of the conclusion of one composition (the inner syllogism made from the shared parts) and the two remaining parts from the other composition. The second conclusion is composed of the two premises of the first composition and the conclusion of the other composition.
Both conclusions are disjunctive with exhaustive disjunction (māni‘at al-khuluww).
3
Work through the first example (A,B,C,H)
Given premises: always either every A is B or every A is C; and always either every B is H or every C is H. The first conclusion is: always either every A is C or every C is H. The second conclusion is: always either every A is H or every B is H.
The reason for the second conclusion is that reality cannot be empty of the premises of one composition and the other two parts.
4
Work through the second example (A,B,C,H,D)
Premises: always either every A is B or every A is C; and always either every B is H or every C is D. Conclusions: first – always either every A is H or every A is C or every C is D; second – always either every A is D or every A is B or every B is H.
Here three disjuncts appear due to the non-sharing parts.
5
Work through the third example (A,B,C,D,H,Z)
Premises: always either every A is B or every C is D; and always either every B is H or every D is Z. Conclusions: first – always either every A is B or every B is H or every C is Z; second – always either every C is D or every C is Z or every A is H.
The ruling applies whether the inner compositions are from the same figure or different figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث من الأقسام وهو أن يشارك أحد جزئي إحدى مقدمتيه أحد جزأي الأخرى، والجزء الآخر من الأولى للجزء الآخر من الثانية ينتج منفصلة موجبة مركبة من نتيجة أحد التأليفين مع الجزأين الآخرين من التأليف الآخر وهي مانعة الخلو. وينتج أيضًا موجبة مركبة من مقدمتي التأليف الأول ونتيجة التأليف الآخر؛ وفيه من الأمثلة أيضًا على حسب الاشتراك المختلفة؛ مثال الأول دائما إما أن يكون كل أب وإما أن يكون كل «أج»، ودائما كل ب هـ أو كل ج هـ ينتج دائما إما كل «أج» أو كل ج هـ . وينتج أيضًا دائما إما كل «أهـ» أو كل ب هـ لامتناع خلو الواقع عن مقدمتي أحد التاليفين وعن الجزأين الآخرين. ومثال الثاني دائمًا إما كل «أب» أو كل «أج»، ودائما إما كل «ب هـ» أو كل ج د ينتج دائما إما كل «أهـ» أو كل «أج» أو كل «ج د». وينتج أيضًا دائمًا كل «أد» أو كل «أب» أو كل ب هـ لما مر . ومثال الثالث دائما إما كل أب وإما كل «ج د»، ودائمًا إما كل بها أو كل د ز ينتج دائما إما كل أب أو كل ب هـ أو كل ج ز . وينتج أيضًا دائما إما كل جد أو كل «ج ز» أو كل «أهـ»؛ ولا يختلف حكم هذا القسم إذا كان كل واحد من التأليفين من شكل واحد أو من شكلين مختلفين.
The third of the divisions: that one part of one of its two premises shares with one part of the other, and the other part of the first [shares] with the other part of the second, produces an affirmative disjunctive composed of the conclusion of one of the compositions together with the other two parts from the other composition, and it is an exhaustive disjunction. And it also produces an affirmative [disjunctive] composed of the two premises of the first composition and the conclusion of the other composition. And there are examples in it also according to the different sharings. Example of the first: always either every A is B or every A is C, and always every B is H or every C is H, produces always either every A is C or every C is H. And it also produces always either every A is H or every B is H, because the actual situation cannot be empty of the premises of one of the compositions and of the other two parts. Example of the second: always either every A is B or every A is C, and always either every B is H or every C is D, produces always either every A is H or every A is C or every C is D. And it also produces always either every A is D or every A is B or every B is H, due to what preceded. Example of the third: always either every A is B or every C is D, and always either every B is H or every D is Z, produces always either every A is B or every B is H or every C is Z. And it also produces always either every C is D or every C is Z or every A is H. And the ruling of this division does not differ whether each of the two compositions is from one figure or from two different figures.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage covers the third division of compound disjunctive syllogisms, where the sharing pattern is crosswise: the first part of the first premise shares with one part of the second, and the other part of the first shares with the remaining part of the second. This yields two conclusions, each an affirmative disjunction: one composed of the conclusion of one composition plus the two parts from the other composition, and the other composed of the premises of the first composition and the conclusion of the other. Three sub-examples illustrate varying sharing patterns. The author emphasizes that the ruling does not change whether the two compositions (the inner syllogisms) are in the same figure or different figures.

Fourth Division: Full Sharing in Compound Syllogisms
Author explains the fourth division with full part-sharing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the four compositions and their conclusions from two disjunctive premises with full part-sharing, and determine whether all compositions belong to the same figure.

Core Idea
Full sharing yields a four-part disjunctive conclusion.

This lesson explores the fourth division of compound disjunctive syllogisms, where each part of one premise shares a term with each part of the other. The author describes how this produces four syllogisms (compositions) and a conclusion with four parts, illustrated with examples. He also notes that these compositions cannot all belong to the same syllogistic figure under some estimations.

Guided Path
1
Extract parts of each premise
Write down the two disjuncts of the first premise (e.g., P1a: 'كل أ ب', P1b: 'كل ج د') and the two disjuncts of the second premise (P2a: 'كل ب د', P2b: 'كل ب هـ').
2
Create the four composition pairs
Form four pairs: (P1a, P2a), (P1a, P2b), (P1b, P2a), (P1b, P2b). Each pair represents a potential syllogism.
3
Derive conclusion for each composition
For each pair, check the shared term. If both are universal affirmatives sharing the middle term, the conclusion substitutes the extremes. E.g., (كل أ ب, كل ب د) yields كل أ د.
The author's example shows conclusions like كل أ د, كل أ هـ, كل ج د, كل ج هـ.
4
Combine conclusions into a disjunction
Since only one composition is actually true, the overall conclusion is the disjunction of all four: 'either كل أ د or كل أ هـ or كل ج د or كل ج هـ'.
5
Analyze syllogistic figures
Examine the term arrangement in each composition. Determine if all four share the same figure (e.g., first figure). The author states that under some estimations they cannot all be in one figure.
This motivates extracting valid moods in other figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من الأقسام وهو أن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي كل واحدة من المقدمتين كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى؛ والقياس يشتمل على أربع تأليفات تنتج موجبة مانعة الخلو من أربعة أجزاء وهي نتائج هذه التأليفات لصدق واحد من هذه التأليفات في الواقع جزء ما. مثال الأول دائما إما كل «أب» وإما كل «ج د»، ودائمًا كل ب د أو كل بها ينتج دائما إما كل «أد» أو كل أه وإما كل «ج د» أو كل «هـ» لما مر . مثال الثاني دائما إما كل «أب» وإما كل ب ج، ودائمًا إما كل «أ» وإما كل ب د ينتج دائما إما بعض ب ج وإما كل «أب» وإما كل «ب أ» وإما بعض جد. ومثال الثالث دائما إما كل أب وإما كل جد، ودائمًا إما كل «د أ» وإما كل «بج» ينتج دائما إما بعض «بد» أو كل «أج» أو كل «ج أ» أو بعض «ج ب». والتأليفات الأربع على التقديرين الآخرين يمتنع أن يكون كلها على هيئة شكل واحد؛ ولا يخفى عليك من بعد استخراج الضروب الباقية في سائر الأشكال فإنه لا يختلف فيما ذكرناه
The fourth of the divisions: that each one of the two parts of each of the two premises shares with each one of the parts of the other; and the syllogism includes four compositions that produce an affirmative exhaustive disjunction of four parts, and these are the conclusions of these compositions because one of these compositions is true in reality for some part. Example of the first: always either every A is B or every C is D, and always either every B is D or every B is H, produces always either every A is D or every A is H or every C is D or every [C] is H, due to what preceded. Example of the second: always either every A is B or every B is C, and always either every [B] is A or every B is D, produces always either some B is C or every A is B or every B is A or some C is D. Example of the third: always either every A is B or every C is D, and always either every D is A or every B is C, produces always either some B is D or every A is C or every C is A or some C is B. And it is impossible that the four compositions, on the two other estimations, are all in the form of one figure; and it is not hidden from you that, after extracting the remaining valid moods in the other figures, it does not differ in what we have mentioned.

This passage describes the fourth division, where each part of each premise shares with each part of the other, leading to four compositions (i.e., four possible pairings of parts). The result is a conclusion with four parts, each being the conclusion of one composition. Three examples illustrate various term relations. The author notes that the four compositions cannot all belong to the same syllogistic figure (e.g., all from the first figure) under certain estimations. The Fourth Division of Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author defines the fourth division of disjunctive syllogisms with three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of the fourth division of disjunctive syllogisms and construct the four compositions that yield a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
In the fourth division, every part of each premise shares with every part of the other, producing a four-part disjunctive conclusion.

This lesson unpacks the fourth and most intricate division of disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic logic. The author defines it as a structure where each part of one premise corresponds to each part of the other premise. Through three examples using variables, he demonstrates how four compositions (ta'lifat) arise, each yielding a disjunctive conclusion composed of four terms. This division is crucial for handling complex logical arguments in kalam where multiple possibilities must be systematically considered.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'shared parts'
The fourth division is defined by the condition that every part of each premise shares with every part of the other premise. This means the terms in the first disjunct of one premise connect to both disjuncts of the other premise, and similarly for the second disjunct.
This is unlike previous divisions where only some parts share.
2
Examine the three examples
Look at the three examples provided. In each, two disjunctive premises are given, and the conclusion is a disjunction of four terms. Identify the pattern: the conclusion lists all possible combinations of the terms from the premises.
The variables (أ, ب, ج, etc.) represent terms in categorical propositions.
3
Note the four compositions
The author states that four compositions (ta'lifat) produce a valid disjunctive exclusive (mani'at al-khuluww). These compositions correspond to different ways of pairing the parts of the premises. They are not all in the same figure.
This highlights the interplay between composition and figure in complex syllogisms.
4
Contrast with simpler divisions
Recall previous divisions where only some parts share. In the fourth division, the full sharing leads to a four-part conclusion. This complexity demands careful attention to ensure validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع من الأقسام وهو أن يشارك كل واحد من جزأي كل واحدة من المقدمتين كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى؛ والقياس يشتمل على أربع تأليفات تنتج موجبة مانعة الخلو من أربعة أجزاء وهي نتائج هذه التأليفات لصدق واحد من هذه التأليفات في الواقع جزء ما. مثال الأول دائما إما كل «أب» وإما كل «ج د»، ودائمًا كل «ب د» أو كل «ب هـ» ينتج دائما إما كل «أد» أو كل «أه» وإما كل «ج د» أو كل «ج هـ» لما مر. مثال الثاني دائما إما كل «أب» وإما كل «ب ج»، ودائمًا إما كل «أج» وإما كل «ب د» ينتج دائما إما بعض «ب ج» وإما كل «أب» وإما كل «ب أ» وإما بعض «ج د». ومثال الثالث دائما إما كل «أب» وإما كل «ج د»، ودائمًا إما كل «د أ» وإما كل «ب ج» ينتج دائما إما بعض «ب د» أو كل «أج» أو كل «ج أ» أو بعض «ج ب». والتأليفات الأربع على التقديرين الآخرين يمتنع أن يكون كلها على هيئة شكل واحد؛ ولا يخفى عليك من بعد استخراج الضروب الباقية في سائر الأشكال فإنه لا يختلف فيما ذكرناه.
The fourth division: that each of the two parts of each premise shares with each of the two parts of the other premise. The syllogism comprises four compositions that produce a disjunctive exclusive (man'at al-khuluww) affirmative composed of four parts, and these are the results of these compositions due to the truth of one of these compositions in reality. Example of the first: Always either every 'Ab' or every 'J d', and always every 'B d' or every 'B h' produces always either every 'Ad' or every 'Ah' or every 'J d' or every 'J h' as before. Example of the second: Always either every 'Ab' or every 'B j', and always either every 'A j' or every 'B d' produces always either some 'B j' or every 'Ab' or every 'B a' or some 'J d'. Example of the third: Always either every 'Ab' or every 'J d', and always either every 'D a' or every 'B j' produces always either some 'B d' or every 'A j' or every 'J a' or some 'J b'. And the four compositions on the other two estimations cannot all be in a single figure; and it is not hidden from you afterwards extracting the remaining moods in the other figures, for it does not differ in what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of a type of disjunctive syllogism. The author explains that in this division, each part of one premise shares with each part of the other premise. The syllogism consists of four compositions that yield a disjunctive affirmative exclusive (man'at al-khuluww) composed of four parts. Three examples are given using letters as variables. The first example uses premises 'always either every Ab or every J d' and 'always either every B d or every B h' to produce a disjunction of four terms. The second and third examples follow similar patterns. The author concludes that the four compositions cannot all be in a single figure, and the remaining moods can be extracted analogously.

The Fifth Division of Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author presents the fifth division of disjunctive syllogisms with an example using formal variables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the composite disjunctive conclusion from two premises in the fifth division of disjunctive syllogisms and identify the three compositions involved.

Core Idea
The fifth division uses three compositions to combine two disjunctions into a single complex exclusive disjunction.

Disjunctive syllogisms are not limited to simple either-or choices. This passage from the logical tradition presents the fifth division, where two disjunctive premises share a component term in a specific way, leading to a compound conclusion through three internal compositions. Mastering this division equips you to handle multilayered disjunctions found in Islamic legal reasoning and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise components
Each premise is a disjunction of two terms. In the example, the first premise is 'always either every Ab or every J d', and the second is 'always either every D h or every D a'. The terms are compound: 'Ab', 'Jd', 'Dh', 'Da'.
The letters a,b,d,h,j are placeholders for general categories.
2
Find the shared component
Look for a letter that appears in both premises. Here, 'd' appears in 'J d' and 'D h'? Actually 'd' appears in 'Jd' and 'Da'? Wait: 'D a' contains d? No, 'D a' has D and a. The shared component is 'd'? Actually both premises have 'd' in different compounds: first premise 'J d' has d, second premise 'D h' and 'D a' both have D, not d. The text says 'الجزء المشارك لأحد جزأي الأخرى' – the part that shares with one of the two parts of the other premise. In the example, 'D' appears in both 'D h' and 'D a' of the second premise, and also 'D' appears in the first premise? Actually first premise has 'كل أب' and 'كل جد' – no 'D'? Wait: 'أب' has 'أ' and 'ب', 'جد' has 'ج' and 'د'. So 'د' is in 'جد'. The second premise has 'دهـ' (د+هـ) and 'د أ' (د+أ). So the shared component is 'د' (d).
The shared component enables the combination.
3
Understand the three compositions
The syllogism comprises three compositions (sub-syllogisms) that together produce the result. The exact breakdown is not given, but the output is two alternative composite conclusions. The first composition likely pairs the shared component with each part of the other premise to generate intermediate results.
The three compositions yield two results that combine into one or two final exclusive disjunctions.
4
Derive the composite conclusion
From the two premises, the author derives 'always either every Ab or every J h or every J a' as one conclusion, and also 'always either every Ad or some B d or every D h' as another. These are exclusive disjunctions (مانعة الخلو) formed from the non-shared components.
The same pattern applies to all figures and moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس من الأقسام وهو أن يكون أحد جزئي إحدى مقدمتيه يشارك كل واحد من جزأي الأخرى في الجزء الآخر لأحدهما فقط؛ والقياس يشتمل على تأليفات ثلاث ينتج موجبة مانعة الخلو مركبة من نتيجتي التأليفين. والجزء المشارك لأحد جزأي الأخرى كما في قولنا دائما إما كل «أب» أو كل «ج د»، ودائما إما كل «د هـ» أو كل «د أ» ينتج دائما إما كل «أب» أو كل «ج هـ» أو كل «ج أ». وينتج أيضًا دائما إما كل «أد» أو بعض «ب د» أو كل «د هـ». وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية من الأشكال الأربعة؛ ولا يمتنع أن ينتج بوجه آخر.
The fifth division: that one of the two parts of one premise shares with each of the two parts of the other premise in the other part for one of them only. The syllogism comprises three compositions that produce a disjunctive exclusive affirmative composed of the two results of the two compositions. The part that shares with one of the two parts of the other is as in our saying: always either every 'Ab' or every 'J d', and always either every 'D h' or every 'D a' produces always either every 'Ab' or every 'J h' or every 'J a'. And it also produces always either every 'Ad' or some 'B d' or every 'D h'. And similarly for the remaining moods of the four figures; and it is not impossible that it produces in another way.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth division of the disjunctive syllogism. Here, one part of one premise shares with each part of the other premise only in the other part for one of them. The syllogism comprises three compositions that yield an exclusive disjunctive affirmative composed of the two results. An example is given: from 'always either every Ab or every J d' and 'always either every D h or every D a' we derive 'always either every Ab or every J h or every J a'. Another result is also possible. The author says the same applies to remaining moods of all four figures, and it is not impossible that it produces in another way.

Key Terms
جزء (juz'): A component term or proposition in a disjunctive premise. [Note: Stable.]

Critique of Ibn Sina's Deduction: Disjunctive to Categorical in the Second Figure
Author critiques the Sheikh's deduction and discusses conditions for disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's categorical deduction from disjunctive premises and evaluate its validity according to the conditions of the second figure.

Core Idea
Disjunctive premises can yield categorical conclusions, but only under the right conditions.

This passage examines how the Sheikh (Ibn Sina) deduces a categorical conclusion from disjunctive premises in the second figure, and debates whether such syllogisms are possible in all figures. By working through his example, you will learn the conditions that make such deductions valid and how the intellect must judge with due consideration of form and mood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sheikh's Deduction
Extract the syllogism from the text: 'Every J is either B or H; nothing of H is either B or J; therefore none of J H.' Note that the premises contain a disjunction.
2
Convert the Disjunctive Premises
Recognize that the first premise is a disjunctive (either...or), but the Sheikh treats it as categorical. The second premise is a universal negative. This conversion is only valid if the disjunction is exclusive (man'at al-khuluww).
3
Determine Figure and Mood
Identify the figure: major term H, minor term J. The premises are 'No H is (B or J)' (E) and 'All J are (B or H)' (A). Conclusion 'No J is H' (E). This is second figure, mood EA? Actually, it's not standard because of the disjunctive predicate. The author uses it to illustrate the possibility of such deductions.
4
Evaluate Validity Conditions
The author states that the disjunctive must be real affirmative or exclusive; otherwise the premises would not be necessarily true. Also, the intellect must observe the conditions of each figure and mood. Check if the deduction holds under these conditions.
5
Assess the Critique's Conclusion
The author refutes those who claim such syllogisms are impossible in the third and fourth figures, arguing they are possible by reflection. This shows the importance of intellectual judgment with proper condition observance.

Source Text

ثم المنفصلة في هذه الأقسام الخمسة عند الأكثر إما موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو وإلا لا يلزم اجتماع مقدّمتي التأليف الواقع فيه على الصدق: وفيه من النظر فإن الشيخ كان يستنتج من الشكل الثاني حملية بقوله كل «ج» إما «ب» وإما «هـ»، وليس البتة شيء من «هـ» إما «ب» وإما «ج» فلا شيء من «ج هـ». ومنهم من قال هذه الأقيسة من الأقسام الخمسة لا يمكن انعقادها في الشكل الثالث والرابع وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه يمكن؛ وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. وبالجملة فهذه كلها من الأمور العقلية فالحاكم بالصحة فيها وعدم الصحة هو العقل لكن لا مطلقا بل برعاية الشرائط التي مرَّ ذكرها في كل شكل بل في كل ضرب؛ فإن رعاية الشرائط على حسب المطلوب على ما عرف.
Then the disjunctive in these five divisions according to most [scholars] is either real affirmative or exclusive (man'at al-khuluww), otherwise the composition of the premises would not necessarily be true. And in it there is consideration: for the Sheikh used to deduce from the second figure a categorical syllogism by his saying: every 'J' is either 'B' or 'H', and nothing of 'H' is either 'B' or 'J', therefore none of 'J H'. And some said that these syllogisms of the five divisions cannot be realized in the third and fourth figures, but that is subject to denial, for it is possible; and that is known by reflection. In summary, all of these are intellectual matters, so the judge of their validity and invalidity is the intellect, but not absolutely, rather with observation of the conditions that were mentioned in each figure and even in each mood. So the observance of conditions is according to the requirement, as known.

Commentary
This passage moves to a meta-level discussion. He concludes that all these are intellectual matters, and the intellect judges validity with due consideration of conditions previously mentioned for each figure and mood. Definition of the Compound Syllogism
Author defines the compound syllogism and its types of sharing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of a compound syllogism and distinguish it from a simple syllogism.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism shares multiple parts between premises, not just a single middle term.

The compound syllogism (al-murakkab) is a more complex form of reasoning where the premises share both a complete part and an incomplete part, or a complete part from one and an incomplete part from the other. This lesson unpacks the author's definition and prepares you to recognize compound syllogisms in Islamic legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'al-murakkab' (the compound syllogism).
2
Extract the sharing pattern
The text says sharing is in a complete part of both premises and an incomplete part of both, OR a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. Note that these are two possible scenarios.
This is the differentia of the compound syllogism.
3
Note the premise types
The author emphasizes that the premises can be conditional, disjunctive, or mixed. This is part of the definition.
4
Contrast with simple syllogism
A simple syllogism has only one shared term (the middle term). A compound syllogism has more complex sharing.
This helps clarify the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المركَّب فهو الذي يكون من اللوازم ذكره؛ وذلك ما يكون الاشتراك في جزء تام من المقدمتين وجزء غير تام منهما أيضًا أو في تام من أحدهما غير تام من الأخرى سواء كانت كل واحدة من المقدمتين متصلة أو منفصلة أو كانت إحديهما متّصلة والأخرى منفصلة؛ فيعتبر كل واحد منهما من حيث هو هو
As for the compound [syllogism], it is that which is among the necessary things to mention. That is where the sharing is in a complete part of both premises and an incomplete part of both, or in a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other, whether each of the two premises is conditional or disjunctive, or one is conditional and the other disjunctive. So each of them is considered in itself.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (al-murakkab). It is described as a syllogism where there is sharing in a complete part of both premises and an incomplete part of both, or a complete part of one and an incomplete part of the other. This applies regardless of whether each premise is conditional (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil), or one is conditional and the other disjunctive. Each premise is considered in itself. This definition sets the stage for further analysis of compound syllogisms.

Classifying the Third Type of Compound Syllogism
Author classifies the third type of compound syllogism combining categorical and conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four divisions of the third type of compound syllogism and apply the conditions for its validity in the first figure.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms blend categorical and conditional premises in four distinct arrangements.

This lesson unpacks the third type of compound syllogism, which fuses a categorical proposition (حملیة) with a conditional proposition (متصلة). The author breaks down the possible positions of the shared term and the roles of the premises. Understanding these divisions and their conditions is essential for constructing valid compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Recognize that the third type mixes a categorical and a conditional, not two categoricals or two conditionals.
2
Determine the shared element
The sharing can be either between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, or between the categorical and the consequent.
3
Classify by the categorical's role
In each sharing option, the categorical can be either the minor or the major premise, yielding 2 × 2 = 4 divisions.
4
Apply the first figure conditions
For the first division (categorical shares with antecedent, categorical is minor), note three conditions: (1) categorical universal or affirmative, (2) conditional's antecedent universal, (3) categorical not negative unless conditional's antecedent negative.
5
Count the moods
The author calculates 64 possible moods (16 limited propositions × 4 antecedent types) but only 28 are productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثالث من الأقسام الخمسة / فيما يتألف من الحملي والمتصل والشركة فيه إما أن يكون من الحملية وبين مقدم المتصلة، أو بينها وبين تالي المتّصلة. وعلى التقديرين فالحملية إما الصغرى أو الكبرى؛ فهذه أربعة أقسام. والحملية في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة على شرائط ذلك الشكل؛ والمتّصلة قد تكون كلية، وقد لا تكون وأما القسم الأول [ فهو أن يكون المشارك الحملية مقدم المتصلة والحملية هي الصغرى]، فإنه ينعقد على كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة، [1] والشرط في إنتاج الشكل الأول من هذا القسم أمور ثلاثة: الأول اشتمال الحملية على الشرطين يعني تكون كلية أو موجبة، الثاني أن تكون الكبرى كلية أي مقدمها كليا، الثالث أن لا تكون الحملية سالبة إلا إذا كانت المتصلة كلية مقدمها سالب؛ والضروب الممكنة أربعة وستون لما أن الحاصل من ضرب المحصورات في نفسها ستة عشر. ومقدم المتّصلة في كل واحد منها إما موجب كلي أو سالب كلي أو موجب جزئي أو سالب جزئي لكن المنتج منها ثمانية وعشرون؛
The third type of the five divisions concerns what is composed of the categorical and the conditional, and the sharing in it is either between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, or between it and the consequent of the conditional. In both cases, the categorical is either the minor or the major; so these are four divisions. The categorical in each of the four figures is according to the conditions of that figure; and the conditional may be universal or not. As for the first division [that the shared part is the antecedent of the conditional and the categorical is the minor], it occurs in each of the four figures. The condition for production in the first figure of this division are three: first, the categorical includes the two conditions, meaning it is universal or affirmative; second, the major premise must be universal, i.e., its antecedent universal; third, the categorical should not be negative unless the conditional is universal and its antecedent negative. The possible moods are sixty-four since the product of the limited propositions in themselves is sixteen. And the antecedent of the conditional in each of them is either universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative, but the productive among them are twenty-eight.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of compound syllogism, which combines a categorical proposition (حملية) with a conditional proposition (متصلة). The author specifies that the sharing (شركة) between the two premises can be either between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, or between the categorical and the consequent. Furthermore, the categorical can be either the minor or the major premise, resulting in four possible arrangements. The categorical in each arrangement must follow the conditions of the four figures. Then the author details the first division where the categorical shares with the antecedent and is the minor premise. For the first figure, three conditions are necessary: the categorical must be either universal or affirmative; the major (conditional) must have a universal antecedent; and the categorical must not be negative unless the conditional's antecedent is also negative. Finally, the author computes the possible moods: since there are 16 limited propositions (four types each for categorical and conditional?), the total combinations are 64, but only 28 are productive.

Deriving Conclusions in Compound Syllogisms with Conditional Major
Author demonstrates conclusion derivation in compound syllogisms with conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a compound syllogism when the minor is universal affirmative and the major is a universal conditional, using conversion and the third figure.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms require chaining categorical and conditional reasoning through conversion and multiple figures.

In this passage, the author shows how to derive conclusions when the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical and the major is a universal conditional. The derivation is not straightforward—it involves converting premises and using the third figure to bridge the gap. Let's walk through the process step by step, using the same variables as the source: ج, ب, أ, هـ, ز.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Minor and Major Premises
The minor is a universal affirmative categorical, e.g., 'Every ج is ب'. The major is a universal conditional, e.g., 'Whenever every ب is أ, then هـ ز' or 'Whenever nothing of ب is أ, then هـ ز'.
2
Determine the Antecedent's Form
Check whether the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. This dictates the type of conclusion.
The passage handles universal antecedent first, then particular.
3
Derive for Universal Antecedent (Affirmative or Negative)
For universal antecedent, the conclusion is a particular conditional: 'Sometimes when every ج is أ, then هـ ز' (or 'sometimes when nothing of ج is أ, then هـ ز'). This is derived by assuming the contradictory leads to a falsehood, then using conversion with the major to confirm.
The author shows the derivation using the third figure and conversion.
4
Derive for Particular Antecedent
For particular antecedent, the conclusion is a universal conditional: 'Whenever some ج is أ, then هـ ز'. The derivation uses a compound argument: from the categorical and a part of the antecedent, we get a middle term via the third figure, then combine to get the final conclusion.
This involves two syllogistic steps: first a third-figure categorical syllogism, then a first-figure conditional syllogism.
5
Verify the Derivation
Check that the derived conclusion follows validly from the premises by reconstructing the syllogistic steps in symbolic form. Ensure no illicit conversions or fallacies are committed.
The author confirms validity by showing that the contradictory would be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

When the categorical minor is universal affirmative, it produces with the universal affirmative conditional in its four moods the universal of the antecedent, and its two parts, meaning if its antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative, the result is particular, its antecedent is the composite result between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, as in our saying: Every ج is ب, and Whenever every ب is أ, then هـ ز, it produces 'Sometimes when every ج is أ, then هـ ز', otherwise its contradictory would be true, and it with the major premise produces from the third [figure] absolutely not if every ب is أ, then every أ is ج, and it is false because it is true that whenever every ب is أ, then every أ is ج (1) for production of its antecedent with the categorical minor as its consequent from the first. Similarly, when the antecedent is universal negative, the corresponding conclusion is produced. Then the author addresses cases where the antecedent is particular affirmative or negative, showing that the result is a universal conditional, with the conclusion derived through a compound syllogism involving the third figure. The explanation uses the variables ج, ب, أ, هـ, ز as term letters.

The Syllogism
