Mixed Syllogism: Categorical Universal Affirmative with Conditional Universal Affirmative (Universal Antecedent)
Author's first example of mixed syllogism with universal affirmative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a mixed syllogism where the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical and the major premise is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal antecedent.

Core Idea
The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent composites the categorical and the conditional's antecedent.

In this lesson, we explore how a categorical premise combines with a conditional premise to yield a new conditional conclusion. The author uses the example 'every J is B' and 'whenever every B is A then HZ' to show that the valid conclusion is 'it may be that whenever every J is A then HZ'. This demonstrates the principle of 'نتيجة التأليف' (composition) that is central to mixed syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the minor categorical premise (e.g., 'كل ج ب' – every J is B) and the major conditional premise (e.g., 'كلما كان كل ب أ فـ هـز' – whenever every B is A then HZ). Note that the conditional is universal affirmative with a universal antecedent.
The variable 'هز' is a placeholder for an arbitrary predicate.
2
Determine the middle term
The middle term is 'ب' (B), which appears in both premises: as the predicate of the categorical and as the subject of the conditional's antecedent.
In mixed syllogisms, the middle term is often the shared term between the categorical and the conditional's antecedent.
3
Form the composite antecedent
Composite (نتيجة التأليف) the subject of the categorical (ج) with the subject of the conditional's antecedent (أ) to create a new proposition 'كل ج أ' (every J is A). This becomes the antecedent of the conclusion.
The composite is a universal affirmative categorical, matching the universality of the original categorical.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the composite as antecedent and the original conditional's consequent (هز) as consequent, prefixed by 'قد يكون' (it may be that) to indicate possibility modality: 'قد يكون إذا كان كل ج أ فـ هز' (it may be that whenever every J is A then HZ).
The conclusion retains the conditional form of the major premise, not a categorical.
5
Verify by reductio
Assume the conclusion is false (i.e., its contradictory is true). Using the major conditional and the categorical, derive a contradiction via syllogistic conversions (first and third figures). This confirms validity.
The author uses 'خلف' (reductio) as proof method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلي أو موجب جزئي أو سالب جزئي لكن المنتج منها ثمانية وعشرون؛ إذ الصغرى الحملية إذا كانت موجبة كلية تنتج مع المتصلة الموجبة الكلية في ضروبها الأربعة كلي المقدم، وجزئيه يعني إن كان مقدمها موجبا كليا أو سالبا كليا فالنتيجة جزئية مقدمها نتيجة التأليف بين الحملية وبين مقدم المتّصلة كما في قولنا كل ج ب ، وكلَّما كان كل ب أ) فـ «هز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل (ج) أ» فـ هـز» وإلا لصدق النقيض وإنه مع كبرى Universal or particular affirmative or particular negative, but the product from them is twenty-eight; because the categorical minor premise if it is universal affirmative, it produces with the universal affirmative conditional in its four moods: if its antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative, then the conclusion is particular, its antecedent is the result of composition between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional, as in our saying: every J is B, and whenever every B is A, then HZ, it produces: it may be that whenever every J is A, then HZ; otherwise the contradictory would be true, and it with the major of the syllogism produces from the third: it is absolutely not that whenever every B is A, then every J is A, which is false because it is true: whenever every B is A, then every J is A, to produce its antecedent with the categorical minor its consequent from the first. Commentary
This passage is a systematic exposition of the valid conclusions produced when a universal affirmative categorical premise (صغرى حملية موجبة كلية) is combined with various types of conditional premises (متصلة). The author uses variables ج, ب, أ for terms and 'هز' (possibly a dummy conclusion) to illustrate. The text is a technical elaboration common in advanced logic commentaries. The author first considers the universal affirmative conditional (متصلة موجبة كلية) in its four moods: when its antecedent is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is a composition of the original categorical and the conditional's antecedent, and consequent is the conditional's consequent. The proof uses reduction to first and third figures and reductio ad absurdum. Then the author treats the particular affirmative conditional, then the negative conditional (سالبة كلية) in its four moods. Throughout, the method of 'aks (conversion) and khalf (reductio) are employed. This passage demonstrates the combinatorial power of syllogistic logic in Islamic tradition, akin to extending categorical syllogisms to mixed premises.

The Syllogism

Rules for Mixed Syllogisms with Categorical Minor and Conditional Major
Author gives rules for mixed syllogisms with categorical minor and conditional major

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conclusion form of a mixed syllogism composed of a universal affirmative categorical minor and a conditional major, and apply the rule with a given example.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent derives from the minor and the major's antecedent.

This passage details how a universal affirmative categorical minor premise interacts with a conditional major premise to produce a new conditional conclusion. The rule hinges on combining the minor premise with the antecedent of the major to form the new antecedent. Understanding this rule is essential for constructing valid mixed syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially in legal reasoning where conditional statements are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Locate the categorical premise that is a universal affirmative. In the passage, it is 'Every J is B' (كل ج ب).
The minor is always categorical in these mixed syllogisms.
2
Identify the major premise and its antecedent quantity and quality
The major is a conditional (muttasila). Determine whether its antecedent is universal affirmative, universal negative, or particular. In the example, the major is 'If some J is A, then H Z' with a particular affirmative antecedent.
The quality and quantity of the antecedent affect the conclusion.
3
Apply the rule for conclusion formation
The conclusion is a conditional: its antecedent is the result of combining the minor with the antecedent of the major (i.e., the conclusion of a categorical syllogism from those two), and its consequent is the consequent of the major. In the passage, the conclusion is 'It may be that if every B is A, then some J is A'.
The antecedent 'every B is A' comes from the minor 'every J is B' and the antecedent 'some J is A' via a categorical syllogism.
4
Verify by reductio (al-khalf)
The author demonstrates the conclusion using proof by contradiction: assume the opposite of the conclusion, derive a contradiction with the premises, thereby confirming the conclusion. This method is used to establish the logical necessity.
Reductio is a standard tool in Islamic logic for verifying syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتصلة إذا كان ليس بعض ( أ ) فـ هـ ز » لما مرَّ : فهذه أربعة أضرب. ولأن الصغرى الموجبة الكلية تنتج مع المتّصلة الموجبة الجزئية إذا كان مقدمها موجبا كليا أو سالبا كلّيّا موجبة جزئية مقدمها نتيجة التأليف كلية وجزئية، وتاليها تالي المتصلة لما مر من الخلف كقولنا كل ج ب»، وقد يكون إذا كان بعض ج أ» فـ هـز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك مع الكبرى ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل ب أ) فبعض (ج) أ»، وهو باطل لأنه كلما كان كل ب أ) فبعض (ج أ» لاستلزام مقدمها مع الحملية. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم الكبرى سالبا كليا كقولنا كل (ج) ب) ، وقد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من ب أ) فـ هز» ينتج قد يكون إذا ا كان ليس بعض (ج) أ) فـ و ز لما مر . ولا يخفى عليك لزوم هاتين الجزئيتين ومقدمهما كلي. ولأن الصغرى الموجبة الكلية الحملية ينتج مع السالبة الكلية في ضروبها الأربعة، وفي السالبة الجزئية في ضربها الكلي المقدم متصلة سالبة: وهي جزئية إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة كليا، وكلية إن كان جزئيا، ومقدمها نتيجة التأليف كيف كانت وتاليها تالي المتصلة؛ ويتبين بالخلف كما مر ، والأوسط مقدم المتّصلة. أما إذا كان مقدم المتصلة السالبة كليا كقولنا كل ج ب ، وليس البتة أو قد يكون إذا كان كل ب أ) فـ هـز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان كل ) أ فـ هـ ز » إذا كان كل (ج) (أ) فـ «هز» وإلا لكان نقيضه. ومع كبرى القياس ينتج من الشكل الثاني ليس البتة أو قد لا يكون إذا كان كل ب أ» فكل «ج أ» وإنه باطل لما صدق قولنا كلما كان كل «ب أ» فكل «ج أ»، وهذه الصادقة مع الكبرى ينتج من الثالث المطلوب. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم الكبرى سالبا كليا لكن مقدم النتيجة يكون سالبا كليًّا. ولا يخفى عليك إذا كان مقدمها جزئيا. وأما إذا كان مقدم السالبة الكلية جزئيا فالطريق أن تردَّ المتّصلة الكلية؛ الجزئية المقدم إلى المتصلة الكلية؛ الكلية المقدم، هذا من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين؛ وفيه النظر فإن الخلف الذي مرّ ذكره لا يدل إلا على النتيجة الجزئية. فأما على الكلية فلا يدل عليها وإن كان غيره من الدلالات التي مرَّ ذكرها يدل عليها كما مر .
The connected conditional: 'If not some (A) is H Z' as was mentioned: these are four types. Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a detailed set of rules for mixed syllogisms where the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical and the major premise is a connected conditional (mutasila). The author begins by stating that the conditional 'If not some (A) is H Z' has four types. Then he explains that with a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative conditional major (whether its antecedent is universal affirmative or negative), the conclusion is a particular affirmative whose antecedent is the conclusion of the compositional syllogism (i.e., the minor premise with the antecedent of the major) and whose consequent is the consequent of the major. This is demonstrated by reductio. He gives an example: 'Every J is B' and 'It may be that if some J is A, then H Z' would produce 'It may be that if every B is A, then some J is A', but that is false because the implication of the antecedent with the categorical actually forces 'Whenever every B is A, then some J is A'. Then he considers the case where the antecedent of the major is universal negative, producing a conclusion with a negative antecedent. He notes that both particular conclusions follow from universal major antecedents. Next, he discusses the universal negative conditional major: with the universal affirmative categorical minor, it yields a negative conditional conclusion, and the mood details depend on the quantity of the major's antecedent. The reduction by reductio is indicated. Then he addresses the case where the major's antecedent is universal negative, leading to a conclusion in the second figure that is false unless corrected by a third-figure inference. Finally, the author critiques a method adopted by some earlier scholars: when the antecedent of the negative conditional is particular, they suggest reducing it to a universal antecedent. He points out that the reductio proof only proves the particular conclusion, not the universal one, though other methods could prove the universal. This critique shows the author's rigor in distinguishing what each proof establishes.

The Limits of Reductio in Mixed Syllogisms: A Critique
Author critiques reduction method for conditional antecedents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why reductio ad absurdum in mixed syllogisms only proves particular conclusions, not universal ones, as critiqued by the author.

Core Idea
Reductio proves only particular conclusions; universal ones require other methods.

In the latter part of the passage, the author critiques a method adopted by earlier scholars: when the antecedent of the negative conditional major is particular, they reduce it to a universal antecedent. The author points out that this reduction via reductio only establishes a particular conclusion, not the desired universal one. This lesson examines that critique and its implications for logical rigor in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier method being critiqued
Some earlier logicians, when faced with a conditional major whose antecedent is particular (e.g., 'If some A is B then C'), would reduce it to a conditional with a universal antecedent by assuming the universal form and proving it via reductio.
This reduction is intended to simplify the syllogism.
2
State the author's objection
The author argues that the reductio proof only yields a particular conclusion, not a universal one. He says 'the reductio mentioned does not indicate except the particular conclusion'.
The author is careful to distinguish what a proof actually demonstrates.
3
Understand the logical reason
Reductio works by assuming the opposite of a particular claim and deriving a contradiction. But to prove a universal claim, one would need to show it holds for every instance, which a single reductio cannot do unless the domain is finite or the reductio covers all cases. The author's point is that the method used was insufficient.
This critique reflects a deep understanding of the limits of proof by contradiction.
4
Consider the author's alternative
The author acknowledges that other methods (other dalalat) could prove the universal conclusion, but the reductio itself does not. Thus, one should not mistakenly think reductio suffices for universality.
This shows the author's commitment to precision in logical methodology.

The connected conditional: 'If not some (A) is H Z' as was mentioned: these are four types. Because the universal affirmative minor premise produces with the particular affirmative conditional when its antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative, a particular affirmative whose antecedent is the conclusion of the composition [of the syllogism] universal and particular, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional, as was mentioned in the reductio, as our saying 'Every J is B', and 'It may be that if some J is A, then H Z' otherwise its contradictory would be true; and that with the major premise produces 'It may be that if every B is A, then some J is A', which is false because whenever every B is A, then some J is A, due to the implication of its antecedent with the categorical. Likewise when the antecedent of the major is universal negative, as our saying 'Every J is B', and 'It may be that if no B is A, then H Z' produces 'It may be that if not some J is A, then W Z' as was mentioned. And it is not hidden from you the entailment of these two particulars and their antecedent is universal. And because the universal affirmative categorical minor premise produces with the universal negative [conditional] in its four moods, and with the particular negative in its mood with universal antecedent, a negative conditional: it is particular if the antecedent of the conditional is universal, and universal if it is particular, and its antecedent is the conclusion of the composition however it is, and its consequent is the consequent of the conditional; and it is shown by reductio as was mentioned, and the middle term is the antecedent of the conditional. As for when the antecedent of the negative conditional is universal, as our saying 'Every J is B', and 'Absolutely not or it may be that if every B is A, then H Z' produces 'It may be that not if every A is H Z' if every J is A then H Z' otherwise its contradictory would hold. And with the major premise of the syllogism it produces in the second figure 'Absolutely not or it may be that if every B is A, then every J is A', and it is false because our saying 'Whenever every B is A, then every J is A' is true, and this true [statement] with the major premise produces from the third [figure] what is sought. Likewise if the antecedent of the major is universal negative, but the antecedent of the conclusion is universal negative. And it is not hidden to you when its antecedent is particular. And as for when the antecedent of the universal negative is particular, the way is to reduce the universal conditional; the particular antecedent to the universal conditional; the universal antecedent, this is among what a group of earlier scholars held; and there is scrutiny in it, for the reductio mentioned does not indicate except the particular conclusion. As for the universal, it does not indicate it, although other indications mentioned indicate it as was mentioned.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a detailed set of rules for mixed syllogisms where the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical and the major premise is a connected conditional (mutasila). The author begins by stating that the conditional 'If not some (A) is H Z' has four types. Then he explains that with a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative conditional major (whether its antecedent is universal affirmative or negative), the conclusion is a particular affirmative whose antecedent is the conclusion of the compositional syllogism (i.e., the minor premise with the antecedent of the major) and whose consequent is the consequent of the major. This is demonstrated by reductio. He gives an example: 'Every J is B' and 'It may be that if some J is A, then H Z' would produce 'It may be that if every B is A, then some J is A', but that is false because the implication of the antecedent with the categorical actually forces 'Whenever every B is A, then some J is A'. Then he considers the case where the antecedent of the major is universal negative, producing a conclusion with a negative antecedent. He notes that both particular conclusions follow from universal major antecedents. Next, he discusses the universal negative conditional major: with the universal affirmative categorical minor, it yields a negative conditional conclusion, and the mood details depend on the quantity of the major's antecedent. The reduction by reductio is indicated. Then he addresses the case where the major's antecedent is universal negative, leading to a conclusion in the second figure that is false unless corrected by a third-figure inference. Finally, the author critiques a method adopted by some earlier scholars: when the antecedent of the negative conditional is particular, they suggest reducing it to a universal antecedent. He points out that the reductio proof only proves the particular conclusion, not the universal one, though other methods could prove the universal. This critique shows the author's rigor in distinguishing what each proof establishes.

Rules for Mixed Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms: Particular Affirmative and Universal Negative Minors
Author gives rules for particular affirmative and universal negative categorical minors with universal conditional majors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion produced by a mixed categorical-conditional syllogism given the minor's quantity and quality and the major's type.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the minor's quantity and quality determine the conclusion's antecedent and quantity.

This passage from the Mantiq Kabir presents two more rules for mixed syllogisms where the minor is categorical and the major is conditional. We have already covered universal affirmative minors; now we learn about particular affirmative and universal negative minors. The author specifies exactly which combinations of minor and major yield valid conclusions and what those conclusions look like. Mastering these rules allows you to evaluate arguments that mix categorical and conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise's type
Determine the quantity (particular or universal) and quality (affirmative or negative) of the categorical minor. In this passage, two cases are given: particular affirmative and universal negative.
2
Check the major premise's type
The major is a conditional (muttasila). Note whether it is universal or particular, affirmative or negative, and whether its antecedent is universal or particular.
3
Apply the rule for particular affirmative minor
If the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal conditional (affirmative or negative), it produces a conclusion in all four moods of the conditional. If the major is particular conditional (affirmative or negative) with a universal antecedent, it produces the mentioned results, but the antecedent of the universal conclusion must be universal.
4
Apply the rule for universal negative minor
If the minor is universal negative and the major is two universal conditionals with negative antecedents, the conclusion is a particular affirmative whose antecedent is only particular affirmative, known by reflection (ta'mmul).
5
Practice with an example
Construct a mixed syllogism with a particular affirmative minor and a universal conditional major to see the conclusion.

Commentary
This passage provides two rules for mixed syllogisms with a categorical minor and a conditional major. When combined with a particular conditional (affirmative or negative) that has a universal antecedent, the conclusions are as previously mentioned, but note that the antecedent of the universal conclusion must be universal. Second, when the minor is a universal negative categorical, it combines with two universal conditionals that have a negative antecedent, producing a particular affirmative conclusion whose antecedent is only particular affirmative—known by reflection (bi al-ta'mmul). This is a concise statement of the productive moods for these minor premises.

Conditions for Productive Syllogisms in the Second Figure with Mixed Premises
Author states conditions and lists thirty-six productive moods with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two sets of conditions for productive moods in the second figure with conditional major premises and trace how a conclusion is derived in one example.

Core Idea
The second figure with mixed premises has two sets of conditions yielding thirty-six productive moods.

In this passage, the author extends the second figure of categorical syllogism to cases where the major premise is a conditional. He specifies two conditions for the syllogism to be productive and then illustrates how a particular mood works with an example. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology where conditionals are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The author states: first, the conditional must be universal or its antecedent universal; second, the categorical minor and the antecedent of the conditional must differ in quality. Alternatively, the conditional must be universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality.
2
Understand the thirty-six moods
The text claims that considering these conditions, there are thirty-six productive moods. This number comes from combining the two conditions with the four moods of the categorical minor (AAA, AII, EAE, EIO, etc.)? Actually, the text says the universal affirmative categorical minor produces with the universal affirmative conditional in its four moods.
The exact derivation of 36 is not fully detailed in this passage, but it is given as a result.
3
Trace the example derivation
The example uses a universal affirmative categorical minor 'Every J is B' and a universal affirmative conditional 'Whenever every A is B, then H Z'. From these, the conclusion 'It may be that if some J is A, then H Z' is derived. The author explains that this involves first-figure reasoning to produce 'Whenever every A-J, then every A-B', then using the converse of the antecedent.
This demonstrates a valid mood in the second figure.
4
Connect to conditions
Check that the example satisfies the conditions: the conditional is universal, the antecedent 'every A is B' is universal affirmative, and the categorical minor 'Every J is B' is also universal affirmative. Since they agree in quality (both affirmative), it falls under the second set of conditions.

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for productive mixed syllogisms in the second figure. The author states two sets of conditions: first, the conditional must be universal or its antecedent universal, and the categorical minor and the antecedent of the conditional must differ in quality; second, the conditional must be universal and its antecedent must agree with the categorical in quality. Conditions for the Second Figure: 36 Productive Moods
Author states the two conditions for the second figure and notes it yields 36 productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for productive moods in the second figure and derive the total number of moods.

Core Idea
Second figure validity hinges on two distinct condition sets, yielding 36 moods.

In mixed syllogisms combining categorical and conditional premises, the second figure has specific conditions to produce a conclusion. The author presents two alternative formulations that determine when a conclusion follows. Understanding these conditions is key to checking syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially for Kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
The passage states the condition for production (shart intajihi) is on two divisions (qismayn). Notice the two alternative sets of conditions.
The author uses 'aw' (or) to separate the two divisions.
2
Analyze the first division
First condition: either the conditional (al-muttasila) is universal (kulliyya) or its antecedent (muqaddam) is universal. Second condition: the categorical (al-hamliyya) and the antecedent differ in quality (ikhtilaf fi al-kayf) – one affirmative, one negative. Also, 'neither is superior in quantity' (wa la yakun ashraf minhuma fi al-kamm) likely means both are universal in quantity.
The phrase about quantity may be corrupt; focus on the quality condition.
3
Analyze the second division
Alternative condition: the conditional is universal (kulliyya) and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality (muwafiq li al-hamliyya fi al-kayf). This is a simpler condition: both have the same quality.
This division does not require differing qualities.
4
Calculate the total moods
Considering these two conditions, the productive moods are 36 (sitta wa thalathun). This number arises from combining the four categorical premise types (A, E, I, O) with the four conditional premise types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) under each condition set.
The exact combinatorial calculation is based on constraints of the second figure.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Example: 'If the world were eternal, it would have no beginning; but it has a beginning; therefore, it is not eternal.' This is a conditional-categorical syllogism in the second figure. Check: the conditional is universal affirmative? The antecedent 'the world is eternal' is universal affirmative? Actually need to assess: the conditional premise is 'If... then...' and the categorical premise is 'it has a beginning' (affirmative). here the antecedent of conditional is 'the world is eternal' (affirmative) and categorical is 'it has a beginning' (affirmative). They agree in quality? Yes, both affirmative. So the second condition (conditional universal, antecedent agrees with categorical in quality) is satisfied. The conclusion follows.
Ensure the syllogism actually fits the second figure formally.

وأما الشكل الثاني فشرط إنتاجه على قسمين: أحدهما كون المتصلة كلية أو كون مقدمها كليا، وثانيهما اختلاف الحملية ومقدم المنفصلة في الكيف؛ ولا يكون أشرف منهما في الكم، أو كون المتّصلة كلية وكون مقدمها موافقا للحملية في الكيف. وباعتبار هذين الشرطين كانت الضروب المنتجة ستة وثلاثون؛

This passage defines the conditions for valid syllogisms in the second figure when mixing categorical and conditional premises. The author lists two conditions: first, the conditional premise must be universal OR its antecedent must be universal; second, the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Alternatively, if the conditional is universal, its antecedent must match the categorical in quality. Based on these conditions, there are 36 productive moods. The commentary should clarify that the second figure typically requires difference in quality, but the author provides a refined condition.

Categorical Minor with Conditional Major: First Two Subcases
Author explains the first two productive subcases when minor is universal affirmative and major is universal affirmative, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a mixed syllogism when the categorical minor is universal affirmative and the conditional major is universal affirmative, distinguishing between universal and particular affirmative antecedents.

Core Idea
The antecedent's quantity in the conditional major determines the conclusion's antecedent quantity.

In this lesson, we examine two subcases of mixed syllogisms where the categorical minor is universal affirmative and the conditional major is universal affirmative. The key is whether the major's antecedent is universal or particular affirmative — this determines whether the conclusion's antecedent is particular or universal affirmative. We'll follow the author's step-by-step derivation using the first and third figures and the conversion of the antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
The categorical minor is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every J is B'). The conditional major is universal affirmative with either a universal affirmative antecedent (e.g., 'Whenever every A is B, then H Z') or a particular affirmative antecedent (e.g., 'Whenever some J is A, then H Z').
2
Subcase 1: Major antecedent universal affirmative
When the major's antecedent is universal affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent. The derivation uses a first-figure categorical syllogism combined with the conditional major, plus conversion of the antecedent ('aks al-muqaddam).
The conclusion is 'It may be that some J is A then H Z'.
3
Subcase 2: Major antecedent particular affirmative
When the major's antecedent is particular affirmative, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with a universal affirmative antecedent. This derivation uses a third-figure categorical syllogism and then the first figure.
The example is 'Every J is B' and 'Whenever some J is A then H Z'.
4
Compare the two subcases
The quantity of the major's antecedent (universal vs particular) reverses the quantity of the conclusion's antecedent: universal antecedent yields particular conclusion antecedent; particular antecedent yields universal conclusion antecedent.
This is due to the interplay of the categorical minor and the conditional major in different syllogistic figures.

Because if the categorical minor is universal affirmative, it produces with the universal conditional in its four moods. If its antecedent is universal affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative with a particular affirmative antecedent, because it holds that whenever every 'A' is 'J' then 'H Z' — to produce the antecedent of this conditional with the categorical minor as the major premise from the first figure that necessitates its consequent essentially, and with the conversion of the antecedent it produces the required, as in our saying: Every J is B, and whenever every A is B then H Z, yields: It may be that some J is A then H Z, because whenever every A is J and every J is B, then whenever every A is J and every J is B, then every A is B from the first [figure], yields: Whenever every A is J then every A is B, and whenever every A is B then H Z yields: Whenever every A is J then some J is A yields: It may be that some J is A then H Z, which is required. Deriving Conclusions from Negative Antecedents by Reductio
Author explains the third and fourth subcases when the antecedent is universal negative or particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a mixed syllogism with a categorical minor universal affirmative and a conditional major universal affirmative whose antecedent is negative, using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
A negative antecedent in the conditional major yields a positive conclusion via reductio, not direct conversion.

In the previous lesson, we examined subcases where the antecedent of the conditional major was positive. Now we turn to negative antecedents. The author explains two more subcases: when the antecedent is universal negative and when it is particular negative. The key method here is reductio ad absurdum (bil-khalf), which allows us to prove the conclusion even when direct deduction is not obvious. Let us walk through the third subcase with the example given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the categorical minor: 'Every J is B' (كل ج ب) and the conditional major: 'Whenever nothing of A is B, then H Z' (كلما كان لا شيء من أب فـ هز).
The minor is universal affirmative; the major is universal affirmative with a universal negative antecedent.
2
Recognize the antecedent type
Note that the antecedent of the conditional is 'nothing of A is B', which is a universal negative. The author states that when the antecedent is universal negative, the conclusion is particular affirmative with a universal negative antecedent.
The conclusion form is: 'It may be that when nothing of J is A, then H Z' (قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هز).
3
Apply reductio ad absurdum
Assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion: that it is not the case that 'when nothing of J is A, then H Z'. Then combine this assumption with the conditional major to derive a statement that contradicts the categorical minor, showing the assumption is false.
The author says 'otherwise its contradictory would be true, and with the major it leads to the absurd'.
4
State the conclusion
Since the assumption leads to a contradiction, the original conclusion is proved. Thus, the conclusion is validly derived by reductio.
The author also mentions that the fourth subcase (antecedent particular negative) follows similarly, but the conditional is more specific (weaker).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كان مقدمها سالبا كليا فالنتيجة موجبة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره كما في قولنا كل ج ب، وكلما كان لا شيء من أب فـ هز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هز وإلا لصدق نقيضه ; وإنه مع الكبرى يفضي إلى الخلف. وعلى هذا إذا كان مقدم المتصلة الموجبة الكلية سالبا جزئياً لكون المتصلة حينئذ أخص من الموجبة الكلية التي يكون مقدمها سالبا كليا، فهذه أربعة أضرب.
And if its antecedent is universal negative, the conclusion is particular affirmative with a universal negative antecedent by reductio and otherwise, as in our statement: Every J is B, and whenever nothing of A is B then H Z yields: It may be that when nothing of J is A then H Z; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and with the major it leads to the absurd. And similarly, if the antecedent of the universal affirmative conditional is particular negative, because the conditional then is more specific than the universal affirmative whose antecedent is universal negative, these are four moods.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This lesson covers the third and fourth subcases when the categorical minor is universal affirmative and the conditional major is universal affirmative. Third subcase: if the major's antecedent is universal negative, the conclusion is particular affirmative with a universal negative antecedent, derived by reductio ad absurdum. The example: 'Every J is B, and whenever no A is B then H Z' yields 'It may be that when no J is A then H Z'; otherwise a contradiction arises. Fourth subcase: if the antecedent is particular negative, the conditional is more specific (i.e., weaker) than when the antecedent is universal negative; the author states that this also yields a conclusion, but does not give an explicit example. These four subcases complete the analysis for the universal affirmative conditional major.

Productive Moods with Universal Negative Conditional Major – First Two Examples
Author presents first two negative moods from universal negative conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to produce the correct negative conclusion for a syllogism where the conditional major is universal negative, using the first two moods.

Core Idea
A universal negative conditional major transforms affirmative moods into valid negative moods.

In previous lessons, we explored productive moods when the conditional major is universal affirmative. Now the author turns to the case where the conditional major is universal negative, expressed as 'absolutely not if...then...'. This yields four additional moods, each with a negative conclusion analogous to its affirmative counterpart. We will examine the first two examples in detail, understanding the logical transformations that guarantee their validity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure
Identify the two premises: a categorical minor (e.g., 'Every J is B') and a universal negative conditional major (e.g., 'absolutely not if every A is B then HZ'). The conclusion is a conditional that negates the counterpart affirmative conclusion.
2
Identify the affirmative counterpart
Each negative mood corresponds to an affirmative mood from the previously enumerated set. The negative conclusion is the negation of that affirmative conclusion.
The counterparts (نظائر) are the four affirmative moods.
3
Deduce the first mood's conclusion
From 'Every J is B' and 'absolutely not if every A is B then HZ', derive 'It may not be that if some J is A then HZ'. The proof: 'whenever every A is J then every A is B' (from the minor by conversion), then with the major we get 'if every A is J then HZ', and by conversion of the antecedent we obtain the conclusion.
The conversion steps rely on first-figure reasoning.
4
Deduce the second mood's conclusion
From 'Every J is B' and 'absolutely not if some A is B then HZ', derive 'Absolutely not if some J is A then HZ'. The proof: 'whenever some A is J then some J is A' (conversion), then with the major we get the conclusion via the third figure.
The conversion here is simple conversion of the particular affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحصل من انضمامها مع السالبة الكلية في ضروبها الأربعة أربعة أخرى ونتيجة كل واحد منها ما يكون للنظائر من الأربعة الأولى إلا أنها تكون سالبة، مثال الضرب الأول كل ج ب، وليس البتة إذا كان كل أب فـ هز ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان بعض ج أ فـ هز لأنه كلما كان كل أج فكل أب لما مر ; وإنه مع الكبرى ينتج إذا كان كل أج فـ هز وإنه مع عكس المقدم ينتج المطلوب من الثالث ومثال الضرب الثاني كل ج ب، وليس البتة إذا كان بعض أب فـ هز ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان بعض ج أ فـ هز لأنه إذا كان كلما كان بعض أ فبعض ج أ لما مر ; وإنه مع الكبرى ينتج المطلوب من الثالث.
And from joining it with the universal negative [conditional] in its four moods, four others result, and the conclusion of each of them is what belongs to the counterparts of the first four except that they are negative. Example of the first mood: Every J is B, and absolutely not if every A is B then H Z yields: It may not be that if some J is A then H Z, because whenever every A is J then every A is B as previously; and with the major it yields if every A is J then H Z, and with conversion of the antecedent it yields the required from the third. Example of the second mood: Every J is B, and absolutely not if some A is B then H Z yields: Absolutely not if some J is A then H Z, because if whenever some A is J then some J is A as previously; and with the major it yields the required from the third.

Commentary
This lesson discusses the additional moods when the conditional major is universal negative (instead of universal affirmative). Four more moods result, analogous to the previous four but with negative conclusions. The first example: with a universal negative conditional major (i.e., 'absolutely not if every A is B then H Z') and a universal affirmative categorical minor ('Every J is B'), the conclusion is 'It may not be that if some J is A then H Z.' The justification uses the first figure and conversion. The second example: when the conditional is 'absolutely not if some A is B then H Z', the conclusion is 'Absolutely not if some J is A then H Z.' The reasoning again involves the third figure.

Four Moods of Mixed Syllogisms with a Universal Affirmative Conditional Major
Author enumerates four moods when categorical minor combines with universal affirmative conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the four moods of a mixed syllogism where the major premise is a universal affirmative conditional and the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical, determining the conclusion based on the antecedent's quantity and quality.

Core Idea
The antecedent of the conditional major alone determines the mood and conclusion.

In this passage, the author expands on a specific type of mixed syllogism: one where the conditional major premise is universal affirmative and the categorical minor is universal affirmative. By varying the antecedent's quantity and quality (universal/particular, affirmative/negative), exactly four moods arise. Each mood requires a distinct proof method—direct, via the third figure, or by reduction to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed minor premise
The minor premise is always a universal affirmative categorical proposition: 'Every j is b' (كل ج ب). This remains constant across all four moods.
In the passage, 'j' is the minor term and 'b' is the middle term.
2
Examine the major premise's antecedent
The major premise is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Whenever P then hz' (كلما كان كذا فـ هـ ز). The antecedent P can be universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative.
The consequent 'hz' remains the same throughout.
3
Enumerate the four antecedents
The passage covers all four possibilities: universal affirmative (كل أ ب), particular affirmative (بعض أ ب), universal negative (لا شيء من أ ب), and particular negative (ليس بعض أ ب).
The fourth is derived from the third as a special case.
4
Derive the conclusion for each case
For each antecedent, the author provides a conclusion in the form of a conditional. For universal affirmative antecedent, the conclusion is direct; for particular affirmative, it uses the third figure; for universal negative, it uses reduction; for particular negative, it follows from the third.
The conclusion's antecedent is a relation between j and a.
5
Verify the proof method
Check that each conclusion follows logically. For negative antecedents, the author uses reduction to absurdity (khalaf): assume the negation of the conclusion and derive a contradiction with the premises.
Reduction is a standard method in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«أب»، وكلَّما كان كل «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج كلَّما كان كل «أج» فبعض «ج أ» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان بعض (ج) (أ) فـ «هـ ز»، وهو المطلوب. وأما إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة الموجبة الكلية موجبا جزئيا فالنتيجة موجبة كلية مقدمها موجب كلي وموجب جزئي لاستلزام مقدم النتيجة مع الحملية مقدم الكبرى من الشكل الثالث. وإنه مع الكبرى للمطلوب من الأول كقولنا كل (ج) ب»، وكلَّما كان بعض (ج) أ» فـ «هـ ز». وأما إذا كان مقدمها سالبا كليا فالنتيجة موجبة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره كما في قولنا كل ج ب»، وكلَّما كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من ج أ» فـ «هـ ز» وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وإنه مع الكبرى يفضي إلى الخلف. وعلى هذا إذا كان مقدم المتصلة الموجبة الكلية سالبا جزئياً لكون المتصلة حينئذ أخص من الموجبة الكلية التي يكون مقدمها سالبا كليا، فهذه أربعة أضرب.
«ab», and whenever every ‘ab’ is ‘h z’, it produces that whenever every ‘aj’ is some ‘j a’, it may produce that if some (j) (a) then ‘h z’, which is the desired. And as for the case when the antecedent of the universal affirmative conditional is particular affirmative, the result is a universal affirmative whose antecedent is universal affirmative and particular affirmative, because the antecedent of the result together with the categorical yields the major premise from the third figure. And it, together with the major, yields the desired from the first, as in our saying: every (j) is b, and whenever some (j) is a then ‘h z’. And as for the case when its antecedent is universal negative, the result is a particular affirmative whose antecedent is universal negative by reduction and otherwise, as in our saying: every j is b, and whenever nothing of ‘ab’ is ‘h z’, it produces that it may be that nothing of j a is ‘h z’, otherwise its contradictory would be true; and it, together with the major, leads to reduction. And thus if the antecedent of the universal affirmative conditional is particular negative, because the conditional then is more specific than the universal affirmative whose antecedent is universal negative. So these are four types.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) enumerating the four types of mixed syllogisms where the major premise is a universal affirmative conditional (muttasilatun mūjibatun kulliyyatun) and the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical (ḥamliyya). The author, following the logical tradition, shows how the conclusion varies depending on whether the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional is universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. For each case, he gives a proof using conversion (ʿaks) and reduction (khalaf). The first case (antecedent universal affirmative) directly yields the conclusion that whenever some of the minor term (j) is the middle term (a), then the consequent (h z) follows. The second case (antecedent particular affirmative) requires the third figure to derive the universal affirmative conclusion. The third case (antecedent universal negative) uses reduction to absurdity (khalaf) to establish the conclusion. The fourth case (antecedent particular negative) is treated as a special case more specific than the third. This systematic enumeration is typical of advanced logical commentaries and is essential for mastering mixed syllogisms in the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya muttasila) consisting of an antecedent and consequent joined by implication (in kāna...fa...). In this passage, it serves as the major premise in a mixed syllogism. [Note: Stable term across the tradition; occasionally contrasted with munfasila (disjunctive).]
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional proposition. Here, it is the part before 'fa' and its quantity (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) determines the syllogistic outcome. [Note: No significant shift; also used in argumentation for the minor premise in categorical syllogisms, but here specifically for the conditional part.]
حملية (ḥamliyya): A categorical proposition (qadiyya ḥamliyya) with a subject and predicate. Here it refers to the minor premise in the mixed syllogism. [Note: Standard term; stable.]
خلف (khalaf): Reduction to absurdity (reductio ad absurdum): a proof method that assumes the opposite of the desired conclusion and shows it leads to contradiction. Used here to establish conclusions when the antecedent is negative. [Note: Common logical term; also used in dialectic and theology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A particular negative conditional
D) No conclusion can be drawn

Key Takeaway
The mixed syllogism with a universal affirmative conditional major splits into four moods based on the antecedent's quantity and quality, each with its own proof method.

Four Moods with Universal Negative Conditional Major
Enumerates four moods from universal negative conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conclusion for each of the four moods when the minor premise is a universal negative categorical and the major is a universal negative conditional.

Core Idea
When both premises are universal negative, the conclusions mirror earlier moods but yield negative results.

In this passage, the author systematically lays out four valid moods that arise when a universal negative categorical minor premise is combined with a universal negative conditional major premise. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing sound conditional syllogisms in Islamic logical works, where such combinations appear frequently in theological and jurisprudential reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is always a universal negative categorical: 'No J is B' (لا شيء من ج ب). Recognize this form in the text.
The variables ج, ب represent terms; treat them as placeholders.
2
Identify the major premise
The major is a universal negative conditional: 'Absolutely not: if [antecedent] then [consequent]'. Note the four antecedents: no A is B, not some A is B, all A is B, some A is B.
The conditional is 'ليس البتة إذا...' meaning 'it is absolutely not the case that if...'.
3
Extract the conclusion for each mood
For each antecedent, the author gives a specific conclusion. Write them down: 1) 'It may not be that if some J is A then H', 2) 'Absolutely not if some J is A then H', 3) 'It may not be that if no J is A then H', 4) 'mentioned result' (presumably similar pattern).
The conclusions are conditional propositions, not categorical.
4
Confirm the pattern
Notice that all conclusions are negative (either 'absolutely not' or 'may not be'), and they correspond to the results from an earlier set of four moods but with negation.
The author references 'proofs mentioned in those four' for justification.

وتحصل أربعة أخرى من انضمام الصغرى السالبة مع المتصلة السالبة الكلية في ضروبها الأربعة. ونتيجة كل ضرب منها نتيجة نظيره من الأربعة الأولى لكن نتائج هذه الأربعة سوالب بالبراهين المذكورة في تلك الأربعة. فالضرب الأول هو لا شيء من «ج ب»، وليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من «أب» فـ هـز ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان بعض (ج) أ) فـ هـز» لما مر . والضرب الثاني لا شيء من (ج ب»، وليس البتة إذا كان ليس بعض «أب» فـ «هز» ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان بعض «ج (أ) فـ هـز» لأنه كلما كان بعض «ج أ» فليس بعض «أب». وإنه مع الكبرى ينتج المطلوب من الأول. والضرب الثالث لا شيء من ج ب، وليس البتة إذا كان كل «أب» فـ هـز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان لا شيء من (ج أ» فـ هـز» بالخلف كما مر ؛ ثم المقدمة الصادقة مع الكبرى تنتج المطلوب من الثالث / والضرب الرابع لا شيء من ج ب»، وليس البتة إذا كان بعض أب» فـ هـز» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة. ولأن الصغرى السالبة الكلية ينتج مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين في ضرب واحد وهو الذي يكون مقدم المتّصلة فيه موجباً كلياً ونتيجتها متصلة جزئية كما في قولنا لا شيء من ج ب»، وقد يكون إذا كان كل «أب» فـ هـز» ينتج مع المتصلة الموجبة قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من (ج) أ) فـ «هز» ومع السالبة قد لا يكون إذا كان لا شيء من (ج) أ» فـ هـ ز » لأنه كلما كان كل «أب» فلا شيء من «ج أ» لصدق الصغرى؛ وإنه مع المتصلة الموجبة ينتج المطلوب من الثالث. وكذلك مع السالبة المتصلة.
And another four [moods] are obtained from combining the universal negative minor with the universal negative conditional in its four moods. And the result of each mood is the result of its counterpart from the first four, but the results of these four are negatives by the proofs mentioned in those four. So the first mood is: No thing from 'j b' is, and absolutely not if no thing from 'a b' is, then ‹haz›, produces: it is possibly not the case that if some 'j a' then ‹haz›, as previously. And the second mood: No thing from 'j b' is, and absolutely not if not some 'a b' is, then ‹haz›, produces: absolutely not if some 'j a' then ‹haz›, because whenever some 'j a' is, then not some 'a b' is. And it with the major produces the desired from the first. And the third mood: No thing from 'j b' is, and absolutely not if all 'a b' is, then ‹haz›, produces: it is possibly not the case that if no thing from 'j a' is, then ‹haz›, by reduction ad absurdum as previously; then the true premise with the major produces the desired from the third. And the fourth mood: No thing from 'j b' is, and absolutely not if some 'a b' is, then ‹haz›, produces the mentioned result. And because the universal negative minor produces with each one of the two particular [conditionals] in one mood, which is that the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative and its result a particular conditional, as in our saying: No thing from 'j b' is, and it may be that if all 'a b' is, then ‹haz›, produces with the affirmative conditional: it may be that if no thing from 'j a' is, then ‹haz›, and with the negative: it may not be that if no thing from 'j a' is, then ‹haz›, because whenever all 'a b' is, then no thing from 'j a' is due to the truth of the minor; and it with the affirmative conditional produces the desired from the third. And similarly with the negative conditional.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that we obtain another four moods by combining a universal negative categorical minor premise (الصغرى السالبة الكلية) with a universal negative conditional major premise (المتصلة السالبة الكلية) across its four moods. The author notes that the result of each of these four moods corresponds to the result of its counterpart from an earlier set of four moods, but the conclusions here are all negative. He then details each mood. The first mood has the minor 'No J is B' and the conditional 'Absolutely not: if no A is B then H' (i.e., universal negative conditional), yielding the conclusion 'It may not be that if some J is A then H'. The second mood has the same minor and the conditional 'Absolutely not: if not some A is B then H', yielding 'Absolutely not: if some J is A then H'. The third mood uses the conditional 'Absolutely not: if all A is B then H', yielding 'It may not be that if no J is A then H', proven by reduction ad absurdum. The fourth mood uses 'Absolutely not: if some A is B then H', yielding the mentioned result. Then the author adds that the universal negative minor also combines with each of the two particular conditionals (affirmative and negative) in a single mood each, where the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative and the conclusion is a particular conditional. He gives an example: 'No J is B, and it may be that if all A is B then H' with the affirmative conditional produces 'It may be that if no J is A then H', and with the negative conditional produces 'It may not be that if no J is A then H'. The reasoning relies on the truth of the minor premise. This entire section systematically enumerates the valid moods when the minor premise is a universal negative categorical proposition.

Checkpoint
In the second mood described, what is the conclusion when the minor is 'No J is B' and the major is 'Absolutely not: if not some A is B then H'?
A) It is absolutely not that if some J is A then H.
B) It may not be that if some J is A then H.
C) It may be that if no J is A then H.
D) It is absolutely not that if all J is A then H.

Key Takeaway
When the minor is universal negative and the major is universal negative conditional, the conclusion is a negative conditional that mirrors the structure of earlier moods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the first mood: minor 'No J is B', major 'Absolutely not: if no A is B then H'. Substitute J = 'human', B = 'eternal', A = 'created', H = 'perishable'. State the conclusion in Arabic using the pattern from the text.
?
For the third mood, use reduction ad absurdum: assume the opposite of the conclusion and derive a contradiction with the premises. Write out the proof step by step using variables.

Particular Conditional Majors with Universal Negative Minor
Describes the single valid mood for each particular conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid conclusion when a universal negative categorical minor is combined with a particular conditional major (affirmative or negative).

Core Idea
Only one mood each for particular conditionals: the antecedent must be universal affirmative and the conclusion is a particular conditional.

After detailing the four moods with universal negative conditionals, the author turns to particular conditionals. He explains that each particular conditional (affirmative or negative) yields exactly one valid mood when paired with a universal negative minor. The key condition is that the antecedent of the conditional must be universal affirmative. This lesson works through that logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the minor premise
The minor is again the universal negative categorical: 'No J is B'. This remains constant.
2
Identify the particular conditional major
The major is a particular conditional: 'It may be that if ... then H' (affirmative) or 'It may not be that if ... then H' (negative). Note that the antecedent here is 'all A is B' (كل أب), not the other possibilities.
The author says 'which is that the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative'.
3
Derive the conclusion for the affirmative case
With the affirmative conditional 'It may be that if all A is B then H', the conclusion is 'It may be that if no J is A then H'. The reasoning relies on the truth of the minor: from 'No J is B' and 'All A is B' we infer 'No J is A' (by contraposition or immediate inference).
The author states: 'produces with the affirmative conditional: it may be that if no thing from j a is then h'.
4
Derive the conclusion for the negative case
With the negative conditional 'It may not be that if all A is B then H', the conclusion is 'It may not be that if no J is A then H'. The same reasoning applies.
The author adds 'and with the negative: it may not be that if no thing from j a is then h'.

Sixteen Moods from Particular Minors with Conditionals
Enumerates moods from particular minors with conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the sixteen moods produced from particular affirmative and negative categorical minors paired with universal and particular conditional majors, and identify the quality of the conclusion in each case.

Core Idea
A particular minor with a conditional major yields sixteen moods; conclusions usually agree in quality with the major.

In mixed syllogisms combining a categorical minor and a conditional major, the quantity of the minor (universal vs. particular) affects the possible moods. Here, the author systematically enumerates all sixteen moods when the minor is particular, explaining how the conclusion's form depends on the antecedent of the conditional. This enumeration is essential for mastering compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of particular minor
The minor is either particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة) or particular negative (جزئية سالبة). Each yields distinct moods.
2
Pair each minor with conditional majors
For each minor, consider four types of conditional major: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. That gives eight pairings per minor.
The major is always a conditional (متصل).
3
Determine the possible moods for each pairing
For each pairing, the antecedent of the conditional can take different forms (e.g., universal negative, particular affirmative, etc.). The number of moods per pairing is specified: 3 for universal majors, 1 for particular majors.
The author lists the specific antecedent forms for each minor.
4
Count the total moods
Particular affirmative minor yields 8 moods (3+3+1+1). Particular negative minor also yields 8, totaling 16.
The text says: 'فهذه ثمانية أضرب' and 'فهذه ثمانية أضرب أخرى'.
5
Identify the four exceptional moods
In four moods, the conclusion is a universal conditional rather than a particular conditional. These occur when the antecedent of the universal conditional is particular, agreeing with the categorical minor in quality.
The result in these four is universal conditional, antecedent universal affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية ينتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروب ثلاثة وهي التي يكون مقدم المتّصلة فيها سالبا كليا وموجباً جزئياً أو سالبا جزئيا، ومع كل واحد من الجزئيتين في ضرب واحد وهو الذي يكون مقدم المتّصلة فيه سالبا كليّاً، فهذه ثمانية أضرب، ولا الصغرى السالبة الجزئية تنتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروب ثلاثة وهي التي يكون مقدمها موجباً كلياً أو موجباً جزئياً أو سالبا جزئيا. ومع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين في ضرب واحد وهو الذي يكون مقدم المتصلة موجباً كلياً، فهذه ثمانية أضرب أخرى. والنتيجة في كل ضرب من هذه الضروب الستة عشر متّصلة جزئية مقدمها سالب جزئي؛ وإنها موافقة للمتصلة في الكيف إلا في الضروب التي يكون مقدم المتّصلة الكلية فيها جزئيا موافقا للحملية في الكيف وهي أربعة أضرب ضربان من الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع المتصلة الكلية موجبة وسالبة إذا كان مقدمها سالبًا جزئيا؛ إذ النتيجة في هذه الأربعة كلية موافقة للمتصلة في الكيف، ومقدمها موجب كلي. والبيان في النتائج الجزئية من الضروب الاثني عشر بالخلف وغيره من الثالث؛ والأوسط فيه مقدم المتّصلة. وأما في النتائج الحملية من الأربعة فلاستلزام مقدم النتيجة مع الحملية مقدم الكبرى المتصلة من الثالث المستلزم بالذات لتاليها إن كانت المتصلة موجبة كلية، وغير المستلزم للتالي إن كانت سالبة كلية؛ فإذن النتيجة دائما موافقة للمتصلة في الكيف.
And because the particular affirmative minor produces with each one of the two universal conditionals in three moods, which are those in which the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative; and with each one of the two particular [conditionals] in one mood, which is that the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative; thus these are eight moods. And the particular negative minor produces with each one of the two universal conditionals in three moods, which are those in which its antecedent is universal affirmative, particular affirmative, or particular negative; and with each one of the two particular [conditionals] in one mood, which is that the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative; thus these are another eight moods. And the result in each mood of these sixteen moods is a particular conditional whose antecedent is a particular negative; and it agrees with the conditional in quality except in the moods where the antecedent of the universal conditional is particular, agreeing with the categorical in quality; these are four moods: two from the particular affirmative minor with the universal conditional affirmative and negative when its antecedent is particular negative; for the result in these four is universal, agreeing with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is universal affirmative. The explanation for the particular results from the twelve moods is by reduction ad absurdum and other [methods] from the third [figure]; and the middle term there is the antecedent of the conditional. As for the categorical results from the four [moods], it is because the antecedent of the result together with the categorical [minor] entails the antecedent of the major conditional from the third [figure], which necessarily entails its consequent if the conditional is universal affirmative, and does not entail the consequent if it is universal negative; so therefore the result always agrees with the conditional in quality.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage explains the moods that result when the minor premise is a particular categorical proposition (either affirmative or negative) and the major is a conditional proposition. The author first considers the particular affirmative minor (الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية). With a universal conditional major (either affirmative or negative), it produces three moods: those where the antecedent of the conditional is either universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. With a particular conditional major (affirmative or negative), it produces one mood where the antecedent is universal negative. This yields eight moods total for the particular affirmative minor. Next, the particular negative minor (الصغرى السالبة الجزئية) is discussed. With a universal conditional, it produces three moods: antecedent is universal affirmative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. With a particular conditional, it produces one mood where the antecedent is universal affirmative. This also yields eight moods, for a total of sixteen. The author then generalizes about the conclusions. In most of these sixteen moods, the conclusion is a particular conditional with a particular negative antecedent, and it matches the quality of the major conditional. However, there are four exceptional moods where the antecedent of the universal conditional is particular, and these produce a universal conditional conclusion whose antecedent is universal affirmative, matching the quality of the major. The author indicates the proof method: the twelve moods with particular conclusions are proven by reduction ad absurdum or other techniques in the third figure, with the antecedent of the major conditional serving as the middle term. The four moods with categorical (i.e., universal conditional) conclusions rely on the entailment relationship between the antecedent of the result and the antecedent of the major conditional. The passage ends by affirming that the conclusion always agrees with the major conditional in quality (affirmative or negative).

Key Takeaway

?

Conditions for the Third Figure and Its Thirty-Four Moods
Author states conditions for third figure mixed syllogisms and enumerates 34 valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the third figure and identify how the 34 productive moods are enumerated.

Core Idea
The third figure's productivity hinges on two conditions.

The third figure of mixed syllogisms combines a categorical premise with a conditional premise. The author specifies two conditions for such syllogisms to yield a valid conclusion, and then enumerates the resulting thirty-four productive moods. This lesson will guide you through these conditions and the breakdown of the moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find in the Arabic the two parts of the condition for productivity.
The first condition concerns universality, the second concerns quality and quantity.
2
Understand the condition on universality
One of the two premises must be universal, or the antecedent of the conditional must be universal.
3
Understand the condition on quality and quantity
If the categorical premise is negative, then the conditional must be universal and its antecedent must not be 'superior' (ashraf) in quantity and quality to the categorical.
Superior means more specific or stronger in quantifier/quality.
4
Enumerate the moods from universal affirmative categorical
Count the 16 moods from combining a universal affirmative categorical with each of the four quantified conditionals in their four moods.
This yields 4x4 = 16 moods.
5
Enumerate the remaining moods
Count the 12 moods from particular affirmative categorical with universal conditionals (8 from 2x4) and particular conditionals (4 from 2x2), leading to a subtotal of 28. The author likely continues with other categorical types to reach 34.
The text cuts off, but the logical pattern suggests 6 more moods from negative categoricals or other combinations.

[ت] وأما الشكل الثالث فشرط إنتاجه على قسمين: أحدهما أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية أو المقدم كليا، وثانيهما أن تكون المتصلة كلية إذا كانت الحملية سالبة، ولا يكون مقدمها أشرف من الحملية في الكم والكيف. والمنتج باعتبار هذين الشرطين أربعة وثلاثون ضربًا: ستة عشر من الحملية الموجبة الكلية مع كل واحد من المحصورات من المتصلة في ضروبها الأربعة، واثني عشر من الحملية الموجبة الجزئية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين / في ضروبها الأربعة، ومع الجزئيتين في ضربيهما اللذين مقدم المتصلة [٢١٢]
[T] As for the third figure, the condition for its productivity is twofold: one is that one of the two premises is universal or the antecedent is universal, and the second is that the conditional is universal if the categorical is negative, and its antecedent is not superior to the categorical in quantity and quality. And the productive [moods] according to these two conditions are thirty-four moods: sixteen from the universal affirmative categorical with each one of the quantified [conditionals] from the conditional in its four moods, and twelve from the particular affirmative categorical with each one of the two universal conditionals in its four moods, and with the two particular [conditionals] in their two moods whose conditional antecedent [٢١٢]

Commentary
This passage introduces the third figure (الشكل الثالث) of mixed syllogisms (categorical and conditional). The author states that the condition for its productivity has two parts. First, one of the two premises must be universal, or alternatively the antecedent of the conditional must be universal. Second, if the categorical premise is negative, then the conditional major must be universal, and the antecedent of the conditional must not be 'superior' (أشرف) to the categorical in quantity and quality—likely meaning it cannot be more specific or stronger. Given these conditions, there are 34 productive moods. These consist of: 16 moods from the universal affirmative categorical minor with each of the four quantified conditionals (presumably universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) in their four moods; 12 moods from the particular affirmative categorical minor with each of the two universal conditionals (affirmative and negative) in their four moods, and with the two particular conditionals in their two moods (making 4+4+2? Actually 12 = 8 from universal conditionals (2x4) + 4 from particular conditionals (2x2)? The text says 'اثني عشر من الحملية الموجبة الجزئية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربعة، ومع الجزئيتين في ضربيهما' meaning 2 universals x 4 moods = 8, plus 2 particulars x 2 moods = 4, total 12. The text is cut off, but the enumeration likely continues to account for the remaining 6 moods to reach 34. This passage sets up the detailed breakdown of the third figure's valid moods.

The Syllogism

Third Figure of Mixed Syllogisms: Conditions and Moods
Enumeration of conditions and 34 moods for third figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the conditions for productive moods in the third figure of mixed syllogisms and construct a valid conclusion using the given example.

Core Idea
The third figure mixes categorical and conditional premises: 34 moods, with conclusions derived via the first figure.

The third figure of mixed syllogisms involves one categorical premise and one conditional premise. The author lays down precise conditions for when such a combination yields a valid conclusion, and then catalogs all 34 productive moods. Understanding these patterns is essential for assessing arguments in Islamic theology (kalam) where categorical and conditional statements frequently combine, e.g., when proving the necessity of a creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for productiveness
First, at least one premise (either the categorical or the antecedent of the conditional) must be universal. Second, if the categorical is negative, the conditional must be universal, and its antecedent must not exceed the categorical in quantity or quality (i.e., be equal or less).
These conditions ensure that the middle term is properly positioned in the first figure after transformation.
2
Break down the enumeration of 34 moods
The author counts: 16 from universal affirmative categorical with each quantified conditional (4 moods); 12 from particular affirmative categorical with universal conditionals (4 each) plus two with particular conditionals when antecedents are universal; 4 from universal negative minor with universal conditionals when antecedents are negative; 2 from particular negative minor with universal conditionals when antecedents are particular negative.
The total is 16+12+4+2 = 34. Each mood corresponds to a specific combination of quantity and quality.
3
Understand the rule for the conclusion when the conditional is universal
If the conditional is universal, the conclusion is universal, agrees in quality with the conditional, and its antecedent is universal and matches the conditional's antecedent in quality. This is shown through a first-figure intermediate syllogism.
The reasoning: from the antecedent of the conclusion and the categorical minor, we derive the conditional's antecedent via first figure, then apply the conditional.
4
Work through the example with variables
Given 'Every B is J' and 'Whenever every B is A then H Z', the conclusion is 'Whenever every J is A then H Z'. To verify: assume 'every J is A', then 'every B is J' gives 'every B is A' (Barbara), then from the conditional we get 'H Z'. Similarly for negative cases.
The author also shows the negative case: 'Every B is J' and 'It is absolutely not the case that if every B is A then H Z' yields 'It is absolutely not the case that if every J is A then H Z'.
5
Identify the exception when the minor is negative
If the categorical minor is negative (universal or particular), the conclusion becomes particular (not universal) agreeing in quality, and its antecedent is affirmative particular. Example: 'Nothing from B is J' and 'Whenever nothing from B is J then H Z' yields 'It may be that if some A then H Z'.
There are six such moods (details omitted here but enumerated in the passage).
