وأما الشكل الثالث فشرط إنتاجه على قسمين: أحدهما أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية أو المقدم كليا، وثانيهما أن تكون المتصلة كلية إذا كانت الحملية سالبة، ولا يكون مقدمها أشرف من الحملية في الكم والكيف. والمنتج باعتبار هذين الشرطين أربعة وثلاثون ضربًا: ستة عشر من الحملية الموجبة الكلية مع كل واحد من المحصورات من المتصلة في ضروبها الأربعة، واثني عشر من الحملية الموجبة الجزئية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربعة، ومع الجزئيتين في ضربيهما اللذين مقدم المتصلة موجب كلي أو سالب كلي؛ أربعة من الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين إذا كان مقدمها سالبًا كلياً أو سالبا جزئياً، واثنان من الصغرى السالبة الجزئية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين إذا كان مقدمها سالبا جزئيا. وأما النتيجة فإن كانت المتصلة كلية فهي كلية موافقة للمتصلة في الكيف ومقدمها كلي دائمًا موافق لمقدم المتصلة في الكيف لاستلزام مقدم النتيجة مع الصغرى الحملية من الشكل الأول مقدم الكبرى المستلزم بالذات لتاليها إن كانت الكبرى موجبة كلية، وغير المستلزم له إن كانت سالبة كلية إلا في صورة واحدة يعرف من بعد. فقولنا كل ب ج، وكلما كل ب أ فـ هـ ز ينتج كلما كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز لأنه كلما كان كل ج أ فكل ب ج وكل ج أ، وكلما كان كل ب ج وكل ج أ فكل ب أ ينتج كلما كان كل ج أ فكل ب أ، وكلما كان كل ب أ فـ هـ ز. وكذلك قولنا كل ب ج، وليس البتة إذا كان كل ب أ فـ هـ ز ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان ج أ فـ هـ ز لأنه إذا كان كل ج أ فكل ب أ لما مر. وهو مع الكبرى ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم المتصلة سالبا كليا لكن يكون مقدم النتيجة سالباً كلياً. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب. وأما المستثنى من كون النتيجة كلية فهو أن تكون الصغرى الحملية إحدى السالبتين؛ وذلك ستة أضرب تكون النتيجة فيها جزئية موافقة للمتّصلة في الكيف، ومقدمها موجب جزئي بالبيان الذي مر ذكره في الصغرى السالبة الكلية في الشكل الأول كقولنا لا شيء من ب ج، وكلما كان لا شيء من ب ج فـ هـ ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان بعض أ فـ هـ ز. وكذلك إذا كانت المتصلة سالبة كلية لكن النتيجة سالبة جزئية. وأما إذا كان مقدم المتصلة سالبا جزئيا فإنه ينتج النتيجة المذكورة لما مر.
As for the third figure, its condition for production is in two parts: one: that one of the two premises be universal or the antecedent universal; and the second: that the conditional be universal if the categorical is negative, and its antecedent is not superior to the categorical in quantity and quality. The productive [moods] according to these two conditions are thirty-four moods: sixteen from the categorical affirmative universal with each of the quantified [conditionals] from the conditional in its four moods; twelve from the categorical affirmative particular with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods, and with the two particular [conditionals] in their two moods in which the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative or universal negative; four from the minor universal negative with each of the two universal conditionals if its antecedent is universal negative or particular negative; and two from the minor particular negative with each of the two universal conditionals if its antecedent is particular negative. As for the conclusion, if the conditional is universal, then it is universal agreeing with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is always universal agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality, because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical minor from the first figure necessitates the antecedent of the major [premise] which essentially implies its consequent if the major is universal affirmative, and does not imply it if it is universal negative, except in one form which will be known later. So our statement: 'Every B is J, and whenever every B is A then H Z' produces: 'Whenever every J is A then H Z' because whenever every J is A, then every B is J and every J is A, and whenever every B is J and every J is A, then every B is A, producing: 'Whenever every J is A then every B is A', and whenever every B is A then H Z. Similarly our statement: 'Every B is J, and it is absolutely not the case that if every B is A then H Z' produces: 'It is absolutely not the case that if J is A then H Z' because if every J is A then every B is A, as before. And this together with the major produces: 'It is absolutely not the case that if every J is A then H Z'. Likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, then the antecedent of the conclusion is universal negative. And so for the rest of the moods. As for the exception to the conclusion being universal, it is that the categorical minor be one of the two negatives; that is six moods in which the conclusion is particular agreeing with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is affirmative particular, by the explanation that passed regarding the universal negative minor in the first figure, like our statement: 'Nothing from B is J, and whenever nothing from B is J then H Z' produces: 'It may be that if some A then H Z'. And likewise if the conditional is universal negative, but the conclusion is particular negative. And if the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, then it produces the mentioned conclusion for what has passed.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for productive moods in the third figure of mixed syllogisms (where one premise is categorical and the other is conditional). The author begins by stating two conditions: first, one of the two premises must be universal (either the categorical or the antecedent of the conditional), and second, the conditional must be universal when the categorical is negative, and its antecedent must not exceed the categorical in quantity or quality (i.e., must be less or equal). Given these conditions, thirty-four moods are productive. The author enumerates them: sixteen from a universal affirmative categorical with each of the four quantified forms of the conditional (which itself has four moods: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative); twelve from a particular affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals (each having four moods) and with the two particular conditionals in their two moods where the antecedent is either universal affirmative or universal negative; four from a universal negative minor with each of the two universal conditionals when the antecedent is universal negative or particular negative; and two from a particular negative minor with each of the two universal conditionals when the antecedent is particular negative. The conclusion, if the conditional is universal, is universal and agrees with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is always universal agreeing with the conditional's antecedent in quality. This is because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical minor in the first figure yields the antecedent of the major premise, which essentially implies its consequent if the major is affirmative, and does not imply it if negative, except in one case to be mentioned later. The author provides an example with variables: 'Every B is J' (categorical minor) and 'Whenever every B is A then H Z' (conditional major). The conclusion is 'Whenever every J is A then H Z'. The reasoning is that if every J is A, then (since every B is J, and every J is A) every B is A, and from the conditional, H Z follows. Similarly, if the conditional is negative ('It is absolutely not the case that if every B is A then H Z'), the conclusion is negative. The passage then discusses an exception: when the categorical minor is negative (universal or particular), the conclusion becomes particular instead of universal, with six moods. For example, from 'Nothing from B is J' and 'Whenever nothing from B is J then H Z', the conclusion is 'It may be that if some A then H Z'. This mirrors the behavior of negative minors in the first figure. Thus, the author systematically catalogs all productive combinations and their conclusions.

First Figure Composite Syllogisms
Author enumerates productive moods and conclusions for first figure composite syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a first-figure composite syllogism (categorical minor + conditional major) and identify which moods yield universal or particular conclusions.

Core Idea
Composite syllogisms in the first figure combine a categorical minor and a conditional major; the conclusion's form depends on the mood.

In this lesson, we explore how the first figure of the syllogism handles premises of different types: a categorical (hamliyya) minor and a conditional (muttasila) major. The author of the matn works through the possible moods and the resulting conclusions, including exceptions when the minor is negative. Mastering these rules is essential for constructing sound arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh where conditional reasoning is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise is always categorical (hamliyya), and the major is always a conditional (muttasila). Note their quantities (universal/particular) and qualities (affirmative/negative).
2
Determine the figure and mood
The syllogism is in the first figure, meaning the middle term is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor. Use the standard first-figure rules as a baseline.
3
Apply the conclusion rule for universal conditionals
If the conditional major is universal, the conclusion is a universal conditional agreeing in quality with the major, and its antecedent always agrees in quality and quantity with the antecedent of the major. This holds except when the categorical minor is negative.
The author calls this 'the exception from the conclusion being universal'.
4
Handle the exception for negative minors
When the categorical minor is universal negative or particular negative, the conclusion becomes particular (not universal). The conclusion remains agreeing in quality with the major, but its antecedent becomes particular affirmative.
This is a critical nuance; many beginners miss it.
5
Verify with reduction (khalf)
The author demonstrates validity using reductio ad absurdum (khalf) and sometimes by a third-figure reduction. Practice applying khalf to confirm the conclusion follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Universal affirmative or universal negative; four from the universal negative minor with each of the two universal conditionals if its antecedent is universal negative or particular negative, and two from the particular negative minor with each of the two universal conditionals if its antecedent is particular negative. As for the conclusion: if the conditional is universal, it is universal agreeing with the conditional in quality and its antecedent is always universal agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality, because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical minor from the first figure entails the antecedent of the major which essentially entails its consequent if the major is universal affirmative, and does not entail it if it is universal negative except in one form which will be known later. Our statement: 'Every B is C, and whenever every B is A, then H is Z' yields 'whenever every C is A, then H is Z' because whenever every C is A, then every B is C and every C is A, and whenever every B is C and every C is A, then every B is A yields 'whenever every C is A, then every B is A', and whenever every B is A, then H is Z. Similarly, our statement: 'Every B is C, and it is absolutely not the case that whenever every B is A then H is Z' yields 'it is absolutely not the case that whenever every C is A then H is Z' because if every C is A then every B is A as before, and that with the major yields 'it is absolutely not the case that whenever every C is A then H is Z'. Also, if the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, then the antecedent of the conclusion is universal negative. And so on for the other moods. As for the exception from the conclusion being universal: it is that the categorical minor is one of the two negatives; that is six moods in which the conclusion is particular agreeing with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is particular affirmative by the demonstration mentioned earlier for the universal negative minor in the first figure, as our statement: 'Nothing of B is C, and whenever nothing of B is C then H is Z' yields 'it may be that when some A is C then H is Z'. Also, if the conditional is universal negative, the conclusion is particular negative. And if the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, it yields the mentioned conclusion as before. This is the well-known view among some of the earlier scholars; and there is discussion in it because when the minor is particular affirmative, and the major is universal affirmative or universal negative with its antecedent being one of the two universals, the conclusion is a particular conditional agreeing with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is particular agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality likewise. The demonstration by reduction and otherwise is as passed from the third. The middle term in it is the antecedent of the major, as in our statement: 'Some B is C, and whenever every B is A then H is Z' yields 'it may be that when some C is A then H is Z'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that with the major leads to a reduction. And so on for if the antecedent is universal negative. And if the conditional is universal negative, as in our statement: 'Some B is C, and it is absolutely not the case that whenever every B is A then H is Z' yields 'it may not be that when some C is A then H is Z' by reduction and otherwise as passed from the third. Also if its antecedent is universal negative. This is when the conditional is universal. As for when it is particular affirmative, the conclusion is particular agreeing with the conditional in quality, and its antecedent is particular agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality by reduction and otherwise from the third. The middle term is the antecedent of the major as passed, as in our statement: 'Every B is C, and it may be that when some C is A then H is Z' yields 'it may be that when some C is A then H is Z'. Then this true proposition, namely our statement 'whenever every B is A then some C is A', with the major yields the desired result from the third as known, and so on for the other moods.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion on the rules for productive moods in the first figure when the major premise is a conditional (mutasila) and the minor is a categorical (hamliyya). The author begins by enumerating the number of such moods: four from the universal negative categorical minor combined with the two universal conditionals when the antecedent is universal or particular negative; and two from the particular negative categorical minor with the two universal conditionals when the antecedent is particular negative. Then the author explains the general rule for the conclusion: if the conditional major is universal, the conclusion is a universal conditional that agrees in quality with the major, and its antecedent is always universal and agrees in quality with the major's antecedent. This is because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical minor (in the first figure) entails the antecedent of the major, which essentially entails its consequent if the major is universal affirmative, or does not entail it if the major is universal negative (except one case). For example, 'Every B is C, and whenever every B is A, then H is Z' yields 'Whenever every C is A, then H is Z'. The author provides a syllogistic demonstration. Similarly, when the major is a universal negative conditional, the conclusion is a universal negative conditional. This holds for all moods. An exception is when the categorical minor is one of the two negatives (i.e., universal negative or particular negative); then there are six moods where the conclusion is particular. For instance, 'Nothing of B is C, and whenever nothing of B is C then H is Z' yields 'It may be that when some A is C then H is Z'. The author then presents a well-known view among earlier scholars regarding the case when the categorical minor is particular affirmative. In this case, the conclusion is a particular conditional, and the demonstration uses reduction (khalf) from the third figure. The middle term in these composite syllogisms is the antecedent of the major premise. Multiple examples illustrate how the reduction works. The passage concludes by noting that a true proposition 'Whenever every B is A then some C is A' combined with the major yields the desired result from the third figure, and so on for other moods.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Fourth-Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Three conditions for fourth-figure mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three conditions for a valid fourth-figure mixed syllogism and apply them to check the validity of a given syllogism.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's mixed syllogisms are productive only under specific restrictions on quantity and quality of premises.

The fourth figure is the most complex of the syllogistic figures, especially when combining categorical and conditional premises. The author sets out three conditions that must be satisfied for such a syllogism to yield a valid conclusion. Understanding these conditions will help you avoid invalid argument forms in advanced logical reasoning used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the passage and list the three distinct conditions the author gives for a productive syllogism in the fourth figure.
The conditions concern the categorical premise (minor) and the conditional premise (major), as well as their relationship.
2
Examine the first condition
The first condition is that the categorical premise must be either affirmative or universal. That means it cannot be particular negative, and it must at least be universal or affirmative.
'Universal' here means it is a universal proposition (all S is P or no S is P); 'affirmative' means it is affirmative (all S is P or some S is P). So the categorical must be one of: universal affirmative, universal negative, or particular affirmative.
3
Examine the second condition
The conditional premise (a muttasila) should not be particular unless the categorical is universal affirmative, or the antecedent of the conditional is universal and opposite in quality to the categorical. This restricts when a particular conditional can be used.
If the conditional is particular (e.g., 'It may be that if A then B'), it is only allowed if the categorical is universal affirmative, or the antecedent of the conditional is universal and has the opposite quality (affirmative/negative) of the categorical.
4
Examine the third condition
The antecedent of the conditional cannot be universal affirmative while the categorical is particular. Also, the antecedent cannot be particular negative while the conditional is particular. This prevents certain invalid combinations.
This condition rules out specific quantifier/quality mismatches that would break the syllogism.
5
Apply the conditions to a sample
Take a sample fourth-figure mixed syllogism and check each condition. For example: minor categorical 'All humans are rational' (universal affirmative), major conditional 'If some rational beings are mortal then all humans are mortal' (conditional with particular antecedent). Does this violate any condition?
The conditional has a particular antecedent ('some rational beings'), so the second condition requires that the categorical be universal affirmative or the antecedent be universal and opposite quality. Since the categorical is universal affirmative, the condition is satisfied. The third condition does not apply because the antecedent is particular, not universal affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

With these conditions, the author counts thirty-two productive moods, beginning with the universal affirmative categorical minor combining with each of the two universal conditionals. The text cuts off before completing the enumeration.

Conditions for Productivity of the Fourth Syllogistic Figure
Author states conditions and enumerates 32 productive moods of fourth figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for the fourth figure's productivity and verify whether a given mixed syllogism satisfies them.

Core Idea
The fourth figure mixes categorical and conditional premises, and its validity depends on precise quantifier and quality constraints.

The fourth figure of the syllogism combines a categorical minor premise with a conditional major premise. Unlike earlier figures, its productivity requires specific restrictions on the quantity and quality of its premises and their interaction. This passage from an advanced logic manual lays out three conditions that guarantee a necessary conclusion, and then enumerates the thirty-two productive moods that result. Understanding these conditions is essential for anyone who needs to evaluate or construct mixed categorical-conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the first few lines of the Arabic where the author lists the three divisions (أقسام). Note them: (1) the categorical must be affirmative or universal; (2) the conditional cannot be particular unless the categorical is affirmative universal or its antecedent is universal and differs in quality; (3) the antecedent cannot be universal affirmative with a particular categorical, nor particular negative with a particular conditional.
These conditions govern the interplay between the categorical and conditional premises.
2
Understand the role of each premise
The categorical (حملیة) is the minor premise; the conditional (متصلہ) is the major. The middle term is the antecedent of the conditional, which also appears in the categorical.
This is a mixed syllogism, not a pure categorical one.
3
Apply the conditions to an example
Take the author's first example: 'Every B is J, and whenever nothing of A is B, then H is Z.' Check that the categorical 'Every B is J' is universal affirmative (condition 1 satisfied). The conditional is universal (condition 2: its antecedent is universal negative 'nothing of A is B' which is universal negative, so the categorical must be affirmative universal, which it is). Condition 3 is not violated. Therefore, the syllogism is productive, yielding 'Whenever nothing of J is A, then H is Z.'
The conclusion is also a conditional.
4
Recognize the role of reduction (al-khalf)
The author reduces fourth figure syllogisms to first and third figures via proof by contradiction (الخلف). This confirms productivity. Observe the example's reduction: 'Whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of A is B' from third figure, then with the major gives the desired from the first figure.
Reduction ensures that the moods are valid by more fundamental figures.
5
Enumerate the productive moods
The author states there are thirty-two productive moods. They arise from the four combinations of universal affirmative minor with each of the two universal conditionals (affirmative and negative) in four moods each, plus the particular conditionals in three moods each, etc. Trace the reasoning for one case to see how the count builds.
This enumeration is not memorized but shows the richness of the system.

As for the fourth figure, the condition for its productivity is on three divisions: one of them is that the categorical includes being affirmative or universal; second, that the conditional is not particular unless the categorical is affirmative universal or its antecedent is universal differing from the categorical in quality; third, that the antecedent is not universal affirmative while the categorical is particular, nor particular negative while the conditional is particular. And on the basis of these conditions, the productive [moods] are thirty-two types; because the universal affirmative minor produces with each one of the two universals of the conditional in its four moods, a conditional whose antecedent is universal agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality to produce the antecedent of the conclusion with the categorical minor, the major of this figure, which entails its consequent essentially if the major is affirmative, and does not entail its consequent if it is negative except in one mood — that in which the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative; for then the conclusion is particular, its antecedent particular agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality, by reduction to the contrary and other methods of the third [figure], like our saying: 'Every B is J, and whenever nothing of A is B, then H is Z' produces 'Whenever nothing of J is A, then H is Z' because whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of J is A, and every B is J, and whenever nothing of J is A and every B is J, then nothing of A is B from a third of this figure produces 'Whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of A is B'. And that with the major produces the desired from the first also. Similarly, if the conditional is universal negative, but the conclusion is our saying 'It is never the case that if nothing of J is A then H is Z', because whenever nothing of J is A then nothing of A is B for what has passed. And that with the major produces the desired from the first. As for when the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, that is like our saying: 'Every B is J, and whenever every A is B, then H is Z' produces 'It may be that if some J is A, then H is Z'; otherwise its contradictory would be true. And that with the major of the syllogism in the second figure, if the contradictory is its major, produces 'It is never the case that whenever every A is B, then some J is A', which is false. And because the universal affirmative minor produces each one of the two particular conditionals in three moods, which are the moods in which the antecedent of the conditional is not the particular negative, a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quality by reduction to the contrary and other methods of the third, and the middle term in it is the antecedent of the major, as in our saying: 'Every B is J, and it may be that if every A is B, then H is Z' produces 'It may be that if some J is A, then H is Z' for what has passed.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the fourth syllogistic figure (al-shakl al-rabi'), which combines a categorical minor premise with a conditional major premise. The author first specifies three conditions (شرائط) for productivity of this figure: First, the categorical premise (الحملية) must be either affirmative or universal (i.e., not negative particular). Second, the conditional premise (المتصلة) cannot be particular unless the categorical is affirmative universal, or the conditional's antecedent is universal and differs in quality from the categorical. Third, the antecedent of the conditional cannot be universal affirmative while the categorical is particular, nor can the conditional be particular with a particular negative antecedent. On the basis of these conditions, the author states that there are thirty-two productive moods (ضربا). He then explains that a universal affirmative minor premise produces with each of the two universal conditionals (in their four moods) a conditional conclusion with a universal antecedent that agrees in quality with the conditional's antecedent. This holds for affirmative and negative conditionals, except when the antecedent is universal affirmative, in which case the conclusion is particular. The author provides examples using letters (ب, ج, أ, هـ, ز) and reduces the syllogisms to earlier figures (first and third) via proof by contradiction (خلف) or other methods. He also discusses particular conditionals and shows that a universal affirmative minor produces with each particular conditional in three moods (excluding the one where the antecedent is particular negative). The commentary follows the author's order: first stating the three conditions, then enumerating the moods, then explaining each type with examples and reductions. This passage is a technical expansion of a dense matn on the fourth figure's mixed syllogisms.

Valid Moods When the Conditional Premise is the Major
Author enumerates valid moods of mixed syllogisms with conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid moods of a mixed syllogism with a conditional major and a categorical minor in the first figure, and apply reductio ad absurdum to test validity.

Core Idea
Only specific combinations of quality and quantity yield a valid conclusion in mixed syllogisms.

Mixed syllogisms combine a conditional and a categorical premise. In this lesson, we explore the first division where the conditional is the major premise. The author systematically lists the valid moods based on the antecedent's quality and quantity, using reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) to derive conclusions. You will learn to recognize these moods and verify them with logical notation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Premises and the Figure
Locate the conditional major (muttaṣila) and the categorical minor (ḥamliyya). Note that the categorical shares the antecedent of the conditional. The middle term is the antecedent of the major, placing this in the first figure.
2
Parse the Example with Variables
Examine the given example: 'كل ب ج، وكلما كان لا شيء من أ ب فـ هـ ز'. Identify 'ب' as the middle term connecting the categorical premise (كل ب ج) and the conditional antecedent (لا شيء من أ ب). The conclusion is a conditional: 'كلما كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هـ ز'.
3
Apply Reductio ad Absurdum
Follow the author's use of khalaf: assume the negation of the conclusion, combine with the categorical premise, and derive a contradiction using the third figure. The text shows how 'nothing of J is A' leads to the required conclusion via the minor premise.
Reductio is essential when direct proof fails, especially for particular conclusions.
4
Extend to Additional Moods
Vary the quantity and quality of the categorical minor (e.g., particular affirmative, universal negative) and note how the conclusion changes. The author enumerates six, eight, and further additional moods, always specifying the resulting conditional's quality and quantity.
The conclusion always follows the quality of the conditional premise.

التي يكون مقدم المتصلة فيها غير الموجب الكلي، ومع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين في ضرب واحد وهو الذي يكون مقدم المتصلة فيه سالبا كليا ينتج متصلة جزئية مقدمها جزئي موافق لمقدم المتّصلة في الكيف بالخلف وغيره من الثالث إلا إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة الكلية موجباً جزئياً؛ فإن النتيجة حينئذ تكون كلية موافقة للكبرى في الكيف، ومقدمها موجب كلي لاستلزام مقدم النتيجة مع الحملية مقدم الكبرى من هذا الشكل وإنتاجه مع الكبرى الموجبة والسالبة المطلوب من الأول: فهذه ثمانية أضرب أخرى. ولأن الصغرى السالبة الكلية تنتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربع. أما إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبا جزئياً فمتّصلة كلية مقدمها موجب كلي لاستلزام مقدمها مع الحملية مقدم الكبرى من رابع هذا الشكل؛ وإنتاجه مع الكبرى المطلوب الأول من الأول. وإن كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبًا كليًّا لمتصلة جزئية مقدمها موجب جزئي بالبيان الذي مرَّ ذكره في الصغرى السالبة الكلية في الشكل الأول. وأما إذا كان مقدم الكبرى موجباً كلياً أو موجباً جزئياً فمتصلة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره من الثالث لكن إذا كان المقدم موجبا جزئيا لا بد من الرد إلى المتّصلة الكلية الكلية المقدم ليصح الخلف وغيره : فهذه ثمانية أضرب أخرى. ولأن الصغرى السالبة تنتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبًا كليًّا متصلة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره من الثالث. والنتيجة تابعة المتّصلة في الكيف أبدا، فاعتبر بما عرفت. والقسم الثاني فهو أن يكون المشارك الحملية مقدم المتصلة والحملية هي الكبرى أما الشكل الأول منه فشرط إنتاجه على قسمين: أحدهما وهو أن
Its four moods: a conditional with its antecedent universal agreeing in quality with the antecedent of the conditional to produce the antecedent of the conclusion with the categorical minor, and the antecedent of the major from this figure is essentially entailing its consequent if the major is affirmative, and not entailing its consequent if it is negative except in one mood which is when the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative; for the conclusion then is particular, its antecedent particular agreeing in quality with the antecedent of the conditional by reductio and other methods from the third [figure], as our saying: every B is J, and whenever nothing of A is B, then H is Z; it produces: whenever nothing of J is A, then H is Z; because whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of J is A, and every B is J, and whenever nothing of J is A and every B is J, then nothing of A is B from the third of this figure; it produces: whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of A is B. And with the major it produces the required from the first also. Likewise if the conditional is universal negative but the conclusion is our saying: absolutely not if nothing of J is A then H is Z; because whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of A is B as before. And with the major it produces the required from the first. As for if the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, then that is like our saying: every B is J, and whenever every A is B, then H is Z; it produces: it may be that if some J is A, then H is Z; otherwise its contradictory would be true. And with the major of the syllogism in the second figure, if the contradictory is its major, it produces: absolutely not if every A is B, then some J is A, which is false. And because the universal affirmative minor produces each of the two particular conditionals in three moods — these are the moods in which the antecedent of the conditional is other than the particular negative, a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular agreeing in quality with the antecedent of the conditional by reductio and other methods from the third, and the middle term is the antecedent of the major, as in our saying: every B is J, and it may be that if every A is B, then H is Z; it produces: it may be that if some J is A, then H is Z as before. And likewise for the remaining moods: thus there are six additional moods — because the particular affirmative minor with each of the two universal conditionals in three moods — these are the moods in which the antecedent of the conditional is other than the universal affirmative — and with each of the two particular conditionals in one mood which is when the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, it produces a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular agreeing in quality with the antecedent of the conditional by reductio and other methods from the third, unless the antecedent of the universal conditional is particular affirmative; for then the conclusion is universal agreeing with the major in quality, and its antecedent is universal affirmative because the antecedent of the conclusion with the categorical entails the antecedent of the major from this figure, and its production with the affirmative and negative major of the required from the first: thus there are eight additional moods. And because the universal negative minor produces with each of the two universal conditionals in its four moods. As for if the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, then a universal conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative because its antecedent with the categorical entails the antecedent of the major from the fourth of this figure; and its production with the major of the required from the first. And if the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, then a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative by the explanation that was mentioned concerning the universal negative minor in the first figure. And if the antecedent of the major is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, then a particular conditional whose antecedent is universal negative by reductio and other methods from the third, but if the antecedent is particular affirmative, it is necessary to reduce to the universal conditional with universal antecedent so that reductio and other methods are valid: thus there are eight additional moods. And because the negative minor produces with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative, a particular conditional whose antecedent is universal negative by reductio and other methods from the third. And the conclusion always follows the conditional in quality, so consider what you have known. The second division is that the shared categorical is the antecedent of the conditional, and the categorical is the major. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and validation of the moods of mixed syllogisms where one premise is a conditional (muttaṣila) and the other is a categorical (ḥamliyya). The author systematically enumerates all valid combinations based on the figure, the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the antecedents, and the position of the conditional as major or minor premise. The discussion begins with the case where the conditional is the major premise, and the categorical (minor) is the premise that shares the antecedent or consequent. The author first describes the four primary moods that produce a conditional conclusion directly, noting exceptions where the conclusion is particular. He then uses logical examples with variables (such as 'كل ب ج' for 'every B is J') to illustrate how reductio ad absurdum (خلف) and other methods from the third figure (which seems to refer to a particular mood table) are applied. The text then extends to six additional moods when the minor is particular affirmative, eight more when the minor is universal negative, and another eight for other combinations, always specifying the quality and quantity of the resulting conditional conclusion. Finally, the second division is introduced, where the categorical is the major and the conditional is the minor, but the passage breaks off at the condition for the first figure of this division. Throughout, the author maintains strict logical rigor, ensuring that every mood's validity is justified by the entailments between terms. This passage is typical of advanced logic texts (sharḥ) that expand on the basic syllogistic schemes given in matn works. Students are expected to master the enumeration and reasoning patterns to confidently determine the validity of any mixed syllogism encountered in theological or legal argumentation, such as determining the implication of a conditional ruling in fiqh.

In a mixed syllogism with a conditional major, the conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent agrees in quality with the antecedent of the major.

Valid Moods When the Conditional Premise is the Minor (First Figure)
Author begins the second division on mixed syllogisms with categorical major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a valid mixed syllogism in the first figure when the categorical is the major and the conditional is the minor.

Core Idea
The validity of the mixed syllogism when the conditional is minor depends on the relation between the categorical and the conditional's antecedent.

The second division of mixed syllogisms places the categorical as the major premise and the conditional as the minor. This lesson focuses on the first figure of this division, where the condition for production is given in two kinds. You will learn the basic rule and see how the shared term (the antecedent of the conditional) operates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Setup
In this division, the categorical premise (ḥamliyya) is the major, and the conditional (muttaṣila) is the minor. The shared term is the antecedent of the conditional, which also appears in the categorical premise (as subject or predicate).
2
Identify the First Figure Condition
The passage states: 'the condition of its production is of two kinds.' The first figure requires a specific entailment between the categorical and the antecedent of the conditional. What is this condition? The text is incomplete, but based on the context, it likely involves the categorical premise entailing or being entailed by the antecedent.
The second kind is not provided in the text, so we focus on the first kind.
3
Apply the Condition to an Example
Construct a simple example: Suppose the categorical major is 'Every B is J' and the conditional minor is 'If every A is B, then H is Z'. For a valid deduction, we need that the categorical major relates to the antecedent 'Every A is B' such that the conclusion follows. The condition might involve the categorical major entailing the antecedent or being entailed by it.
The exact condition is only partially given; the student should recognize that the text introduces this division and outlines the first figure condition.

Basics of Mixed Syllogism: Particular Affirmative Minor
Author enumerates additional productive moods for mixed syllogisms with categorical minor and conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a particular affirmative categorical minor yields a productive mood with a universal conditional major and determine the form of the resulting conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
Not every combination of categorical minor and conditional major yields a valid conclusion; the antecedent's quality determines the outcome.

In this passage, the author begins enumerating the additional productive moods for mixed syllogisms. We will focus on the first set: when the minor is a particular affirmative categorical and the major is a universal conditional. Understanding these conditions is crucial for mastering advanced syllogistic theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major forms
The minor is a particular affirmative categorical (صغرى موجبة جزئية). The major is a universal conditional (متصل كلي) with an antecedent and consequent. Note that the conditional's antecedent can be affirmative or negative, universal or particular.
2
Recognize the three moods
The author says there are three moods from this combination. However, one mood is an exception. The three moods correspond to the possible antecedent qualities: universal affirmative, universal negative, and particular affirmative. The exception occurs when the antecedent is particular affirmative.
The exception is mentioned later: when the antecedent is particular affirmative, the conclusion becomes universal.
3
Apply the exception rule
If the antecedent of the conditional major is particular affirmative, the resulting conclusion is a universal conditional, not a particular one. Otherwise, the conclusion is particular conditional, matching the quality of the major's antecedent.
This exception is explicitly stated: 'إلا إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة الكلية موجباً جزئياً؛ فإن النتيجة حينئذ تكون كلية'.
4
Determine the conclusion's form
The conclusion is always a conditional proposition. Its antecedent matches the antecedent of the major in quality (affirmative or negative) and quantity (universal or particular), except the exception noted. The consequent of the conclusion is the same as that of the major.
The author provides examples using variables (ج، ب، أ) in the original text.

وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب الباقية : فهذه ستة أضرب أخرى لما أن الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين من ضروب ثلاثة وهي الضروب التي يكون مقدم المتصلة فيها غير الموجب الكلي، ومع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين في ضرب واحد وهو الذي يكون مقدم المتصلة فيه سالبا كليا ينتج متصلة جزئية مقدمها جزئي موافق لمقدم المتّصلة في الكيف بالخلف وغيره من الثالث إلا إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة الكلية موجباً جزئياً؛ فإن النتيجة حينئذ تكون كلية موافقة للكبرى في الكيف، ومقدمها موجب كلي لاستلزام مقدم النتنيجة مع الحملية مقدم الكبرى من هذا الشكل وإنتاجه مع الكبرى الموجبة والسالبة المطلوب من الأول: فهذه ثمانية أضرب أخرى. ولأن الصغرى السالبة الكلية تنتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربع. أما إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبا جزئياً فمتّصلة كلية مقدمها موجب كلي لاستلزام مقدمها مع الحملية مقدم الكبرى من رابع هذا الشكل؛ وإنتاجه مع الكبرى المطلوب الأول من الأول. وإن كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبًا كليًّا لمتصلة جزئية مقدمها موجب جزئي بالبيان الذي مرَّ ذكره في الصغرى السالبة الكلية في الشكل الأول. وأما إذا كان مقدم الكبرى موجباً كلياً أو موجباً جزئياً فمتصلة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره من الثالث لكن إذا كان المقدم موجبا جزئيا لا بد من الرد إلى المتّصلة الكلية؛ الكلية المقدم ليصح الخلف وغيره : فهذه ثمانية أضرب أخرى. ولأن الصغرى السالبة تنتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبًا كليًّا متصلة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره من الثالث. والنتيجة تابعة المتّصلة في الكيف أبدا، فاعتبر بما عرفت.
And according to this for the remaining moods: for these are six other moods, because the particular affirmative minor with each of the two universal conditionals is from three moods, namely the moods in which the antecedent of the conditional is not the universal affirmative, and with each of the two particulars is one mood, that is the one in which the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, producing a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular matching the antecedent of the conditional in quality by reductio and others from the third, except when the antecedent of the universal conditional is particular affirmative; for then the conclusion is universal matching the major in quality, and its antecedent is universal affirmative because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical entails the antecedent of the major from this figure and its production with the affirmative and negative major is the desired from the first: so these are eight other moods. And because the universal negative minor produces with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods. As for when the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, then a universal conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative because its antecedent together with the categorical entails the antecedent of the major from the fourth of this figure; and its production with the major is the first desired from the first. And if the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, then a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative by the demonstration mentioned earlier for the universal negative minor in the first figure. And as for when the antecedent of the major is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, then a particular conditional whose antecedent is universal negative by reductio and others from the third, but if the antecedent is particular affirmative, it is necessary to reduce to the universal conditional; the universal antecedent in order for the reductio and others to be sound: so these are eight other moods. And because the negative minor produces with each of the two particular conditionals when its antecedent is universal affirmative, a particular conditional whose antecedent is universal negative by reductio and others from the third. And the conclusion always follows the conditional in quality, so consider what you have known.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the productive moods in a mixed syllogism where the minor premise is categorical and the major premise is conditional. The author proceeds systematically by considering the quantity and quality of the minor and the antecedent of the conditional. First, he states that when the minor is particular affirmative and the major is one of the two universal conditionals (universal affirmative or universal negative), there are three moods, but then he notes that one of them yields a particular conclusion except when the antecedent is particular affirmative, which yields a universal conclusion. This gives eight moods from the combination with the universal negative minor? Actually, the text is intricate, but the key is that the author is counting all possible productive combinations. The example uses variables that would correspond to terms in Islamic logic, such as 'ج' (jim), 'ب' (ba), 'أ' (alif). The conclusion always matches the conditional in quality. This enumeration is typical of advanced commentaries on logic, expanding on the basic matn of Isaghuji or similar texts.

Universal Negative Minor with Universal Conditionals
Author enumerates additional productive moods for mixed syllogisms with categorical minor and conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four productive moods that arise when the categorical minor is universal negative and the major is a universal conditional, and specify the form of the conclusion for each case.

Core Idea
A universal negative minor combines with a universal conditional major in four distinct moods, each with a specific conclusion form.

The author continues his enumeration by considering the case where the minor is universal negative categorical and the major is universal conditional. This combination yields four additional moods, depending on the quality of the antecedent of the conditional. This lesson will break down each mood and its resulting conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major forms
The minor is a universal negative categorical (صغرى سالبة كلية). The major is a universal conditional (متصل كلي) with either an affirmative or negative antecedent, and the antecedent can be universal or particular.
2
Recognize the four moods
The author states that the universal negative minor produces with each of the two universal conditionals in four moods. These correspond to the four possible antecedents: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
The text says: 'في ضروبها الأربع' (in its four moods).
3
Analyze the first two moods (antecedent particular negative and universal negative)
When the antecedent is particular negative, the conclusion is a universal conditional with a universal affirmative antecedent. When the antecedent is universal negative, the conclusion is a particular conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent.
This is derived from the translation: 'أما إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبا جزئياً فمتّصلة كلية مقدمها موجب كلي' and 'وإن كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبًا كليًّا لمتصلة جزئية مقدمها موجب جزئي'.
4
Analyze the remaining two moods (antecedent universal affirmative and particular affirmative)
When the antecedent is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, the conclusion is a particular conditional with a universal negative antecedent, derived by reductio (khalaf) and other methods from the third figure.
The passage says: 'إذا كان مقدم الكبرى موجباً كلياً أو موجباً جزئياً فمتصلة جزئية مقدمها سالب كلي بالخلف وغيره من الثالث'.
5
Note the special condition for particular affirmative antecedent
If the antecedent is particular affirmative, it is necessary to first reduce it to a universal conditional (with universal antecedent) for the reductio to be sound. This is an additional step.
The text: 'لكن إذا كان المقدم موجبا جزئيا لا بد من الرد إلى المتّصلة الكلية'.

Negative Minor with Particular Conditionals and Reductio
Author enumerates additional productive moods for mixed syllogisms with categorical minor and conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion form when the categorical minor is negative and the major is a particular conditional, and explain the role of reductio (khalaf) in deriving the conclusion.

Core Idea
When the minor is negative (whether universal or particular) and the major is a particular conditional, the conclusion is always derived via reductio and follows the conditional in quality.

In the final part of the passage, the author addresses the case where the minor is negative (either universal or particular? Actually, he says 'الصغرى السالبة' which could be any negative minor) and the major is a particular conditional. He specifies that when the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, the conclusion is a particular conditional with a universal negative antecedent, obtained by reductio. This lesson focuses on that specific combination and the use of reductio.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor is a negative categorical (صغرى سالبة) – this could be universal or particular negative, but the text seems to treat it generally. The major is a particular conditional (متصل جزئي) with a universal affirmative antecedent.
The text: 'ولأن الصغرى السالبة تنتج مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجبًا كليًّا'.
2
Determine the conclusion form
The conclusion is a particular conditional whose antecedent is universal negative. This is derived 'by reductio and others from the third' (بالخلف وغيره من الثالث).
3
Understand the role of reductio
Reductio (khalaf) is a proof method that assumes the opposite of the desired conclusion and shows that it leads to a contradiction. Here, it is used to derive the conditional conclusion from the premises.
The author also mentions 'others from the third', referring to other syllogistic forms in the third figure.
4
Note the quality dependence
The conclusion 'always follows the conditional in quality' (النتيجة تابعة المتّصلة في الكيف أبدا), meaning the conclusion's quality (affirmative or negative) matches that of the major premise.
This is a general rule stated at the end of the passage.

Conditions for Productivity in Mixed Syllogism (Categorical Major)
Author presents conditions for productiveness in the second division with categorical major and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions for a productive mixed syllogism with a categorical major and a conditional minor, and trace the reduction to the second figure.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism's productivity requires matching categorical and conditional antecedent in quantity and quality.

This passage specifies the conditions under which a mixed syllogism, where the categorical premise is the major and the conditional is the minor, yields a conclusion. You will learn two key conditions and see how they produce twenty-six productive moods, illustrated with an example involving logical variables and a reduction to the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the conditions: (1) The categorical must be universal or particular affirmative, matching the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and quality, while the conditional is universal. (2) The conditional must be universal or its antecedent affirmative.
These conditions ensure the premises can be combined to yield a valid conclusion.
2
Understand the enumeration of moods
The author claims twenty-six productive moods result from these conditions. Break them down: eight from universal affirmative categorical with two universal conditionals (four moods each), plus others from particular categoricals.
This enumeration reflects the combinatorial possibilities satisfying the conditions.
3
Parse the example premises
The example uses variables: conditional minor 'Whenever it is the case or absolutely not, if nothing from ج is ب, then هـ is ز', and categorical major 'Every ب is أ' (affirmative) or 'Nothing from ب is أ' (negative).
The categorical major can be affirmative or negative, affecting the conclusion.
4
Trace the reduction to the second figure
Follow the reduction: from the categorical major and the antecedent of the conclusion, the author derives a proposition in the second figure, which then combines with the original minor to produce the final conditional.
Reduction shows how mixed syllogisms rely on categorical logic.
5
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional: 'Whenever nothing from ج is أ, then هـ is ز' for affirmative major, or its negative equivalent. Note how the quality of the categorical major affects the antecedent of the conclusion.
The conclusion follows the conditional in quality (tabi'a al-muttasila fi al-kayf).

And the second division is when the shared element is the categorical (premise) with the antecedent of the conditional, and the categorical is the major. As for the first figure of it, its condition for production is two kinds: one of which is that the categorical is universal or particular affirmative matching the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and quality while the conditional is universal. The second is that the conditional is universal or its antecedent is affirmative. Considering these two conditions, the productive moods are twenty-six: eight from the universal affirmative categorical major with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods, and four from each of the two particulars in the two moods whose antecedent of the conditional in one is universal affirmative and in the other particular affirmative, and two more moods from the particular affirmative categorical major with each of the two universal conditionals when the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative. As for the conclusion, if the conditional is universal and its antecedent is negative, then it is universal and its antecedent matches the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and opposes the categorical in quality, and that is eight moods: four from the universal affirmative categorical major with the two universal conditionals when its antecedent... And an example of it from the universal negative categorical major with them if the antecedent is negative, because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical major entails the antecedent of the minor from the second figure. And its production with the minor is the desired from the first figure, like our saying: 'Whenever it is the case or absolutely not, if nothing from ج is ب, then هـ is ز', and 'Every ب is أ' produces 'Whenever nothing from ج is أ, then هـ is ز' if the minor is affirmative. And 'absolutely not if nothing from ج is أ, then هـ is ز' if it is negative, because whenever nothing from ج is أ, then nothing from ج is أ, so every ب is أ, and whenever nothing from ج is أ and every ب is أ, then nothing from ج is ب, which produces from the second figure 'whenever nothing from ج is أ, then nothing from ج is ب', and that with the minor produces the desired from the first. And likewise if the antecedent is particular negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever it is the case or absolutely not, if nothing from ج is ب, then هـ is ز', and 'Nothing from ب is أ' produces 'Whenever everything from ج is أ, then هـ is ز' if the minor is affirmative, and 'absolutely not if everything from ج is أ'

Commentary
This passage describes the second division of mixed syllogisms, where the categorical premise is the major and the conditional is the minor. The author states two conditions for the first figure to be productive: either the categorical is universal or particular affirmative matching the antecedent in quantity and quality while the conditional is universal, or the conditional is universal or its antecedent is affirmative. From these, twenty-six productive moods are enumerated, broken down into groups. The author then provides an example using logical variables: 'Whenever it is the case or absolutely not, if nothing from ج is ب, then هـ is ز' as the conditional major, and 'Every ب is أ' or 'Nothing from ب is أ' as the categorical minor. The conclusion is a conditional such as 'Whenever nothing from ج is أ, then هـ is ز' or its negative. The reasoning involves reducing the combined premises to the second figure to derive the desired conclusion. In Islamic logic, this type of reasoning is used in juridical analogies (qiyas) and theological arguments, though the example here is abstract.

Productive Moods in the Second Figure of Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates the 26 and 18 productive moods in the second figure of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for productive moods in the second figure of mixed conditional syllogisms and enumerate the 26 and 18 productive moods.

Core Idea
The second figure of conditional syllogisms has 26 productive moods when the conditional is universal with a negative antecedent, and 18 otherwise.

This passage from a classical logic manual delves into the conditions that make a mood productive in the second figure of conditional syllogisms. Here, the major premise is categorical and the minor is conditional. You will learn to recognize the alignment required between the categorical and the conditional's antecedent, and how the number of valid moods changes based on the universality and negativity of the conditional. This knowledge is essential for constructing valid arguments that combine categorical and conditional propositions, a common pattern in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for productivity
The first condition requires the categorical premise to be universal or particular affirmative and to match the conditional's antecedent in quantity and quality. The second condition requires the conditional to be universal or its antecedent to be affirmative. Jot these down.
2
Count the productive moods under the first condition
When the conditional is universal and has a negative antecedent, there are 26 productive moods. Understand how the author arrives at this number: 8 from universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals (4 moods each) etc.
Refer to the text's breakdown.
3
Analyze the example with logical variables
The author gives an example using letters ج, ب, أ, هـ, ز. This shows a concrete inference that reduces to the first figure. Trace the steps: from 'If no J is B then H is Z' and 'All B is A' to 'If no J is A then H is Z'.
This illustrates how a second-figure mood is converted.
4
Understand the 18 moods for non-universal conditionals
When the conditional is not universal and negative in the antecedent, the conclusion is a particular conditional, yielding 18 moods. Note the change in conclusion type.
The conditions shift, reducing the count.

تكون الحملية كلية أو موجبة جزئية موافقة لمقدم المتصلة في الكم والكيف مع كون المتصلة كلية. وثانيهما أن تكون المتصلة كلية أو يكون مقدمها موجباً. وباعتبار هذين الشرطين كانت الضروب المنتجة ستة وعشرين؛ ثمانية من الكبرى الحملية الموجبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربعة وأربعة منها كل واحدة من الجزئيتين في الضربين اللذين مقدم المتصلة في أحدهما موجب كلي وفي الآخر موجب جزئي، وضربان آخران من الكبرى الحملية الموجبة الجزئية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين إذا كان مقدم المتصلة موجباً جزئياً. أما النتيجة فالمتصلة إذا كانت كلية، ومقدمها سالب فهي كلية ومقدمها موافق لمقدم المتصلة في الكم ومخالف الحملية في الكيف وذلك ثمانية أضرب؛ أربعة من الكبرى الحملية الموجبة الكلية مع المتصلتين الكليتين إذا كان مقدمها. ومثالها من الكبرى الحملية السالبة الكلية معهما إذا كان المقدم سالبا لاستلزام مقدم النتيجة مع الكبرى الحملية مقدم الصغرى من الشكل الثاني. وإنتاجه مع الصغرى المطلوب من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من ج ب فـ هـ ز، وكل ب أ ينتج كلما كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هـ ز إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة. وليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هـ ز إذا كانت سالبة لأنه كلما كان لا شيء من ج أ فلا شيء من ج أ فكل ب أ، وكلما كان لا شيء من ج أ وكل ب أ فلا شيء من ج ب ينتج من الثاني كلما كان لا شيء من ج أ فلا شيء من ج ب وهو مع الصغرى ينتج المطلوب من الأول. وكذلك إذا كان المقدم سالبا جزئيا كما في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من ج ب فـ هـ ز، ولا شيء من ب أ ينتج كلما كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز إن كانت الصغرى موجبة، وليس البتة إذا كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز إذا كانت سالبة لأنه كلما كان كل ج أ فلا شيء من ج ب لما مر؛ وإنه مع الصغرى ينتج المطلوب. وعلى هذا إذا كان المقدم سالبًا جزئيًا. وأما إذا لم تكن المتصلة كلية سالبة المقدم فالنتيجة متصلة جزئية مقدمها موافق لمقدم المتصلة في الكم والحملية في الكيف وذلك ثمانية عشر ضربًا.
The categorical premise is universal or particular affirmative, matching the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and quality while the conditional is universal. The second: that the conditional is universal or its antecedent is affirmative. Considering these two conditions, the productive moods are twenty-six: eight from the universal affirmative categorical major with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods, and four from each of the two particular [categorical majors] in the two moods where the antecedent of the conditional in one is universal affirmative and in the other particular affirmative, and two other moods from the particular affirmative categorical major with each of the two universal conditionals when the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative. As for the conclusion: if the conditional is universal and its antecedent is negative, it is universal, and its antecedent matches the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and differs from the categorical in quality, and these are eight moods: four from the universal affirmative categorical major with the two universal conditionals when its antecedent [is negative]. An example of it from the universal negative categorical major with them when the antecedent is negative, because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical major necessitates the antecedent of the minor from the second figure. And its production with the minor yields the desired from the first figure, as in our saying: Whenever, or absolutely not, if nothing of J is B, then H is Z, and all B is A, yields: Whenever nothing of J is A, then H is Z, if the minor is affirmative. And absolutely not if nothing of J is A then H is Z if [the minor] is negative, because whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of J is A, so all B is A; and whenever nothing of J is A and all B is A, then nothing of J is B, yields from the second: Whenever nothing of J is A, then nothing of J is B; and it together with the minor yields the desired from the first. And similarly if the antecedent is particular negative, as in our saying: Whenever, or absolutely not, if nothing of J is B then H is Z, and nothing of B is A, yields: Whenever all J is A then H is Z if the minor is affirmative, and absolutely not if all J is A then H is Z if [the minor] is negative, because whenever all J is A then nothing of J is B, due to what has passed; and it together with the minor yields the desired. And likewise if the antecedent is particular negative. As for when the conditional is not universal with a negative antecedent, the conclusion is a particular conditional whose antecedent matches the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and the categorical in quality, and these are eighteen moods.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the productive moods in the second figure of conditional syllogisms (qiyas al-muttasil) where the major premise is categorical (hamliyya) and the minor is conditional (muttasila). The author first states two conditions for productivity: (1) the categorical premise must be universal or particular affirmative and match the conditional's antecedent in quantity and quality while the conditional is universal; (2) the conditional must be universal or its antecedent affirmative. Considering these, there are 26 productive moods when the conditional is universal with a negative antecedent, and 18 when the conditional is not universal. The author then provides an example using symbolic letters (ج, ب, أ, هـ, ز) to illustrate the reduction of a second-figure mood to the first figure. The explanation shows how the conclusion follows from the premises and how the syllogism is transformed. The passage concludes by noting the total of 18 moods for the non-universal case.

Proof by Contradiction in Second Figure Conditional Syllogisms
Author illustrates proof by contradiction for second figure conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply proof by contradiction to validate second figure conditional syllogisms and determine how the conclusion depends on the quality of the categorical minor.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction in the second figure assumes the opposite of the conclusion to derive an impossible entailment.

In conditional syllogisms, the second figure often requires a special validation method because the conclusion does not follow directly. Classical logicians, following Ibn Sina, developed proof by contradiction (al-bayan bi al-khalf) for this purpose. This passage shows how assuming the contradictory of the conclusion leads to an impossible negation of the original entailment, thereby confirming the original conclusion. We will walk through the method and then apply it to specific examples using symbolic terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target syllogism and conclusion
Locate the second figure conditional syllogism: a conditional major premise (e.g., 'If all J is B then H is Z') and a categorical minor (e.g., 'All B is A'). The conclusion is a conditional: 'If all J is A then H is Z' (for affirmative minor) or its negation.
The definitive article 'al-bayan' indicates a standard procedure.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
Take the contradictory (naqid) of the desired conclusion as a new premise. For a universal affirmative conditional conclusion, the contradictory is a particular negative conditional, and vice versa.
The contradictory must directly oppose the conclusion's truth value.
3
Construct a new second figure syllogism
Use the contradictory as the major premise and keep the original minor premise unchanged. This new syllogism will produce a result that contradicts the original logical entailment (mulazama) between the antecedent of the conclusion and the antecedent of the minor.
The middle term remains the same (B in the example).
4
Derive the impossible consequence
The new syllogism yields 'the negation of the entailment of the antecedent of the conclusion from the antecedent of the minor' (salb mulazamat muqaddam al-natija li muqaddam al-sughra). This is impossible because the original entailment holds by definition.
Impossibility here means logical contradiction.
5
Validate the original conclusion
Since assuming the contradictory leads to an impossibility, the original conclusion must be true. The author then shows how this method works for both affirmative and negative categorical minors, with examples using J, B, A, H, Z.
The proof is general and applies to all moods (durub) of the second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيان بالخلف كما إذا كان النقيض كبرى صغرى القياس من الثاني فإنه ينتج سلب ملازمة مقدم النتيجة لمقدم الصغرى؛ وهو محال وإن كانت الحملية موجبة جزئية فالبيان هو الذي مر ذكره في الصغرى السالبة الكلية في الشكل الأول من القسم الثالث كقولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل ج ب فـ هـ ز، وكل ب أ ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز إن كانت الصغرى موجبة، وقد لا يكون إذا كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز إذا كانت سالبة لما مر. ولو قلت والصغرى بحالها لا شيء من ب أ ينتج مع الصغرى الموجبة قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هـ ز؛ ومع السالبة قد لا يكون إذا كان لا شيء من ج أ فـ هـ ز لما مر. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب. وأما إذا كانت الحملية موجبة جزئية كما في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض ج ب فـ هـ ز، وبعض ب أ ينتج مع الموجبة قد يكون إذا كان بعض ج أ فـ هـ ز؛ ومع الصغرى السالبة قد لا يكون إذا كان بعض ج أ فـ هـ ز. ثم للبيان طرق منها عام يعم الكلية والجزئية، ومنها خاص كما أشار إليه الشيخ في الكلية أن مقدم النتيجة مستلزم لمقدم الصغرى الكلية؛ وإنتاجه معها المطلوب.
And the proof by contradiction is like when the contradictory [of the conclusion] is taken as the major of the syllogism from the second [figure], then it produces the negation of the entailment of the antecedent of the conclusion from the antecedent of the minor; and that is impossible. And if the categorical is particular affirmative, the proof is that which was mentioned earlier in the universal negative minor in the first figure from the third division, as in our saying: Whenever, or absolutely not, if all J is B then H is Z, and all B is A, yields: It may be that if all J is A then H is Z if the minor is affirmative, and it may not be that if all J is A then H is Z if [the minor] is negative, due to what has passed. And if you say, with the minor as it is, nothing of B is A, then it yields with the affirmative minor: it may be that if nothing of J is A then H is Z; and with the negative [minor]: it may not be that if nothing of J is A then H is Z, due to what has passed. And similarly in the rest of the moods. And if the categorical is particular affirmative, as in our saying: Whenever, or absolutely not, if some J is B then H is Z, and some B is A, yields with the affirmative [minor]: it may be that if some J is A then H is Z; and with the negative minor: it may not be that if some J is A then H is Z. Then there are methods for the proof: some general, applying to universal and particular, and some specific, as the Master indicated in the universal, that the antecedent of the conclusion entails the antecedent of the universal minor; and producing with it the desired [conclusion].
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage explains the method of proof by contradiction (al-bayān bi al-khalf) for the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author first describes the general approach: assume the contradictory of the conclusion as a premise, then derive a negation of an entailment that is impossible. Next, specific cases are examined: for a particular affirmative categorical major, the proof uses an earlier example from the first figure. The author provides examples with symbolic terms to show how, depending on whether the minor is affirmative or negative, the conclusion may or may not follow. Finally, two types of proof methods are mentioned: a general method that works for both universal and particular premises, and a specific method noted by 'the Master' (likely Ibn Sina) for universal cases, where the antecedent of the conclusion entails the antecedent of the minor, allowing derivation of the desired conclusion.

Validating the Second Figure of Conditional Syllogisms
Author restates two conditions for productive moods in the second figure of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate whether a second-figure conditional syllogism meets the conditions for productiveness.

Core Idea
Two conditions ensure the second figure yields a valid conclusion.

The author revisits the conditions for the second figure of conditional syllogisms. These conditions determine when a combination of a categorical major and a conditional premise (major or minor) produces a necessary conclusion. Understanding them is crucial for constructing sound mixed syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure structure
In the second figure, the conditional premise (muttasila) and the categorical major (kubra hamliyya) are arranged such that the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Locate the conditional and the categorical major.
2
Apply the first condition
Check if the categorical major is universal. If not, verify that it matches the antecedent of the conditional in quality (affirmative/negative) AND the conditional itself is universal. If either fails, the syllogism is unproductive.
Quality matching means both affirmative or both negative.
3
Apply the second condition
Check if the conditional is universal. If not, verify that its antecedent is contrary in quality to the categorical major. If met, the syllogism produces a valid conclusion.
Contrary means one affirmative and one negative.
4
Determine productivity
If either condition is fully satisfied (the universal versions or the quality alternatives), the figure yields a necessary conclusion. If neither condition holds, the syllogism is invalid.

[ب] وأما الشكل الثاني منه فشرط إنتاجه على قسمين: أحدهما أن تكون الكبرى الحملية كلية أو كونها موافقة لمقدم المتصلة في الكيف مع كون المتصلة كلية. وثانيهما هو أن تكون المتصلة كلية أو كون مقدمها مخالفا للحملية في
[B] As for the second figure of it, the condition for its productiveness is in two parts: one is that the categorical major is universal or matches the antecedent of the conditional in quality while the conditional is universal. The second is that the conditional is universal or its antecedent is contrary to the categorical in [quality?].
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage restates the conditions for productive moods in the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author divides the conditions into two parts. First, the categorical major (al-kubrā al-ḥamliyya) must be either universal or match the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional in quality (kayf), provided the conditional itself is universal. Second, the conditional must be universal, or its antecedent must be contrary (mukhālif) to the categorical in quality. This mirrors earlier conditions but with a slight variation: here the emphasis is on matching or being contrary in quality rather than quantity. The passage is incomplete but provides a concise formulation of the two main conditions.

الكبرى الحملية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT sufficient for the syllogism to be productive?
A) The categorical major is universal.
B) The categorical major matches the antecedent in quality and the conditional is universal.
C) The categorical major is particular and the conditional is particular.
D) The conditional is universal.

Key Takeaway
The second figure of conditional syllogisms is productive only when either the categorical major is universal or matches the antecedent in quality, and either the conditional is universal or its antecedent is contrary in quality to the categorical.
