The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for Valid Inference in the Second Figure of Mixed Syllogisms
Author establishes conditions for valid inference in the second figure mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid second figure mixed syllogism and apply them to determine whether a given mood is productive.

Core Idea
The validity of a mixed conditional-categorical syllogism depends on quantity and quality alignment between the premises.

The second figure of conditional syllogisms is a powerful tool in Islamic logic, combining a conditional major premise with a categorical minor. But not every combination yields a valid inference. The author lays down two precise conditions that govern when a conclusion follows. This lesson will unpack those conditions and show you how to test any mood for productivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Look at the example: the major is a conditional (muttasila) and the minor is a categorical (hamliyya). Note their quantities and qualities.
2
State the first condition
The categorical major must be universal OR it must agree in quality with the antecedent of the conditional while the conditional is universal.
This ensures that the connection between the categorical and conditional is properly aligned.
3
State the second condition
The conditional must be universal OR its antecedent must be opposite in quality to the categorical minor.
This condition guarantees that the middle term is properly distributed.
4
Apply the conditions to an example
Take a specific mood (e.g., universal affirmative categorical with universal conditional) and verify that both conditions hold, confirming productivity.
For instance, 'Whenever all J is B then H-Z' (universal conditional) and 'All A is B' (universal categorical) satisfy both conditions.

So 'H-Z' if it is negative because whenever all 'J-A' then none of 'J-B' for what has passed. And that with the minor premise yields the desired conclusion. And likewise if the antecedent is particular negative. But if the conditional is not universal negative in antecedent, then the conclusion is particular conditional, its antecedent agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and with the categorical in quality, and that is eighteen moods. The proof by reductio is like when the contradictory is the major premise of the second figure of the syllogism, for it produces the negation of the consequence of the antecedent of the conclusion to the antecedent of the minor; and that is impossible. And if the categorical is affirmative particular, the proof is what was mentioned in the universal negative minor in the first figure of the third division, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z', and 'All B is A' produces: it may be that if all J is A then H-Z' if the minor is affirmative, and it may not be if all J is A then 'H-Z' if it is negative for what has passed. And if you say: and the minor remains, none of B is A, it produces with the affirmative minor: it may be that if none of J is A then 'H-Z'; and with the negative: it may not be that if none of J is A then 'H-Z' for what has passed. And likewise for the other moods. And if the categorical is affirmative particular, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if some J is B then H-Z', and some B is A, it produces with the affirmative: it may be that if some J is A then 'H-Z'; and with the negative minor: it may not be that if some J is A then 'H-Z'. Then for the proof there are methods: some general covering universal and particular, and some specific as the master indicated in the universal: that the antecedent of the conclusion entails the antecedent of the universal minor; and producing with it the desired conclusion. As for the second figure of it, the condition for its production is in two parts: one is that the categorical major be universal or that it agrees with the antecedent of the conditional in quality while the conditional is universal. The second is that the conditional be universal or that its antecedent be opposite to the categorical in quality. And by considering these two conditions, the productive moods are twenty-eight: eight from the universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in its four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal negative or particular negative; and eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in the four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, then the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent agrees with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity because the antecedent of the conclusion with the categorical major entails the antecedent of the minor in the first figure, and that is eight types: four from the universal affirmative categorical major, and four from the universal negative likewise if its antecedent is negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z', and all A is B produces: 'Whenever all J is A then H-Z' or 'not at all if all J is A then H-Z' because whenever all J is A then all H is A and all A is B, and whenever it is so, then all J is B from the first produces: 'Whenever all J is A then all J is B'. And that with the affirmative and negative minor produces the desired from the first. And likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is affirmative particular but the antecedent of the conclusion is affirmative particular. And likewise in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if none of J is B then H-Z', and none of A is B produces: 'Whenever all J is A then H-Z' or 'not at all if all J is A then H-Z' for what has passed. And likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative but the antecedent of the conclusion is affirmative particular at that time; this is if the conditional is universal agreeing with the categorical in quality. But if not, then the conclusion is particular with a negative antecedent agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity, by reductio and other methods from the third, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z'.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the second figure of mixed syllogisms (the one that combines a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise). The author begins by describing the proof (bayān) for cases where the major premise is a universal conditional with a negative antecedent. He explains that the proof proceeds by reductio (al-khalf): we assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion, which becomes a major premise in the second figure, leading to an impossibility. Then he discusses cases where the categorical minor is affirmative particular, recalling an earlier proof from the first figure. He gives examples with variables J, B, A, H, Z, showing how the conclusion (another conditional) follows in certain situations (may be or may not be) depending on the quality and quantity of the premises. After surveying various cases, he states that there are general and specific methods of proof, referencing 'the master' (al-Shaykh, i.e., Avicenna). Next, he turns to the second figure proper (al-shakl al-thāni) and lists two conditions for valid inference: (1) the categorical major must be universal or agree in quality with the antecedent of the conditional, and the conditional must be universal; (2) the conditional must be universal or its antecedent must be opposite in quality to the categorical. Considering these two conditions, the total number of productive moods is 28: 8 from universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals (4 × 2), 4 from universal affirmative with each of the two particular conditionals (2 × 2) when the conditional's antecedent is negative; similarly for universal negative categorical. He then explains the types of conclusions: when the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent matches the conditional's antecedent in quantity, resulting in 8 subtypes. He illustrates with an example: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z, and all A is B' produces 'Whenever all J is A then H-Z' (or its negation), via a chain of reasoning involving the first figure. He also covers particular cases and concludes by stating that when the conditional is not universal and agreeing in quality, the conclusion is particular with a negative antecedent, obtained by reductio or other methods from the third figure (though the example is cut off). Throughout, the author is clarifying the logical structure of these syllogisms, providing the conditions for validity and enumerating all valid moods, with careful attention to quantity and quality.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of the two conditions for a valid second figure mixed syllogism, according to the author?
A) The categorical minor must be universal.
B) The conditional must be universal or its antecedent must be opposite in quality to the categorical.
C) The categorical minor must agree with the consequent of the conditional.
D) The antecedent of the conditional must be universal.

Key Takeaway
The productivity of a second figure mixed syllogism depends on two conditions: the categorical major's universality or quality agreement with the conditional's antecedent, and the conditional's universality or quality opposition between its antecedent and the categorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following premises: Major (conditional): 'Whenever a thing is contingent, it is temporally originated' (كلما كان الشيء ممكنا فهو حادث). Minor (categorical): 'All bodies are contingent' (كل جسم ممكن). Does this mood satisfy the two conditions for the second figure? Explain why or why not.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Enumerating the 28 Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates the 28 productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 28 productive moods of the second figure mixed syllogism and explain how they arise from the two conditions.

Core Idea
The 28 moods are systematically derived from combinations of universal/particular conditionals and categoricals with specific quality requirements.

Once the two conditions are established, the author counts exactly 28 productive moods. This lesson will guide you through that enumeration, showing how each mood satisfies the conditions and why others are excluded. You will learn to recognize the pattern behind the numbers.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two conditions
Condition I: The categorical major must be universal OR agree in quality with the conditional's antecedent while the conditional is universal. Condition II: The conditional must be universal OR its antecedent opposite in quality to the categorical minor.
These conditions restrict which combinations of quantity and quality are productive.
2
Start with the universal affirmative categorical major
Take the categorical major as universal affirmative (e.g., 'كل أ ب'). With the two universal conditionals (affirmative antecedent and negative antecedent), each has 4 moods (different combinations of the conditional's antecedent and consequent quantities/qualities). That gives 8 moods.
The author says 'ثمانية من الحملية الموجبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربعة' – 8 from universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in its four moods.
3
Add universal affirmative with particular conditionals
With the two particular conditionals (each having a particular antecedent), only when the conditional's antecedent is negative (universal or particular) does it produce. That gives 4 moods (2 conditionals × 2 negative antecedent types).
Author: 'وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها سالبًا كليا أو سالبا جزئيًا'
4
Repeat for universal negative categorical major
Similarly, universal negative categorical major yields 8 moods with universal conditionals and 4 with particular conditionals (this time when the antecedent is positive).
Author: 'وثمانية من الحملية السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة، وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجباً كليا أو موجباً جزئياً'
5
Total the moods
Add all: 8 + 4 + 8 + 4 = 28 productive moods.
These are the only combinations that satisfy both conditions.

كذلك كان كل ج ب من الأول ينتج كلما كان كل (ج) أ) فكل ج ب». وإنه مع الصغرى الموجبة والسالبة ينتج المطلوب من الأول. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم المتصلة موجباً جزئياً لكن مقدم النتيجة موجب جزئي. وعلى هذا في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من «ج ب» فـ هـ ز»، ولا شيء من «أب» ينتج كلَّما كان كل ج أ» فـ هـز أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج أ» فـ «هز» لما مر . وكذلك إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبا جزئياً لكن مقدم النتيجة موجب جزئي حينئذ؛ هذا إذا كانت المتصلة كلية موافقة لمقدم الحملية في الكيف. فأما إذا لم تكن فالنتيجة جزئية مقدمها سالب موافق لمقدم المتصلة في الكم بالخلف وغيره من الثالث كقولنا كلَّما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج ب» فـ «هز»،
So 'H-Z' if it is negative because whenever all 'J-A' then none of 'J-B' for what has passed. And that with the minor premise yields the desired conclusion. And likewise if the antecedent is particular negative. But if the conditional is not universal negative in antecedent, then the conclusion is particular conditional, its antecedent agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity and with the categorical in quality, and that is eighteen moods. The proof by reductio is like when the contradictory is the major premise of the second figure of the syllogism, for it produces the negation of the consequence of the antecedent of the conclusion to the antecedent of the minor; and that is impossible. And if the categorical is affirmative particular, the proof is what was mentioned in the universal negative minor in the first figure of the third division, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z', and 'All B is A' produces: it may be that if all J is A then H-Z' if the minor is affirmative, and it may not be if all J is A then 'H-Z' if it is negative for what has passed. And if you say: and the minor remains, none of B is A, it produces with the affirmative minor: it may be that if none of J is A then 'H-Z'; and with the negative: it may not be that if none of J is A then 'H-Z' for what has passed. And likewise for the other moods. And if the categorical is affirmative particular, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if some J is B then H-Z', and some B is A, it produces with the affirmative: it may be that if some J is A then 'H-Z'; and with the negative minor: it may not be that if some J is A then 'H-Z'. Then for the proof there are methods: some general covering universal and particular, and some specific as the master indicated in the universal: that the antecedent of the conclusion entails the antecedent of the universal minor; and producing with it the desired conclusion. As for the second figure of it, the condition for its production is in two parts: one is that the categorical major be universal or that it agrees with the antecedent of the conditional in quality while the conditional is universal. The second is that the conditional be universal or that its antecedent be opposite to the categorical in quality. And by considering these two conditions, the productive moods are twenty-eight: eight from the universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in its four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal negative or particular negative; and eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in the four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, then the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent agrees with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity because the antecedent of the conclusion with the categorical major entails the antecedent of the minor in the first figure, and that is eight types: four from the universal affirmative categorical major, and four from the universal negative likewise if its antecedent is negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z', and all A is B produces: 'Whenever all J is A then H-Z' or 'not at all if all J is A then H-Z' because whenever all J is A then all H is A and all A is B, and whenever it is so, then all J is B from the first produces: 'Whenever all J is A then all J is B'. And that with the affirmative and negative minor produces the desired from the first. And likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is affirmative particular but the antecedent of the conclusion is affirmative particular. And likewise in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if none of J is B then H-Z', and none of A is B produces: 'Whenever all J is A then H-Z' or 'not at all if all J is A then H-Z' for what has passed. And likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative but the antecedent of the conclusion is affirmative particular at that time; this is if the conditional is universal agreeing with the categorical in quality. But if not, then the conclusion is particular with a negative antecedent agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity, by reductio and other methods from the third, as in our saying: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z'.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the second figure of mixed syllogisms (the one that combines a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise). The author begins by describing the proof (bayān) for cases where the major premise is a universal conditional with a negative antecedent. He explains that the proof proceeds by reductio (al-khalf): we assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion, which becomes a major premise in the second figure, leading to an impossibility. Then he discusses cases where the categorical minor is affirmative particular, recalling an earlier proof from the first figure. He gives examples with variables J, B, A, H, Z, showing how the conclusion (another conditional) follows in certain situations (may be or may not be) depending on the quality and quantity of the premises. After surveying various cases, he states that there are general and specific methods of proof, referencing 'the master' (al-Shaykh, i.e., Avicenna). Next, he turns to the second figure proper (al-shakl al-thāni) and lists two conditions for valid inference: (1) the categorical major must be universal or agree in quality with the antecedent of the conditional, and the conditional must be universal; (2) the conditional must be universal or its antecedent must be opposite in quality to the categorical. Considering these two conditions, the total number of productive moods is 28: 8 from universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals (4 × 2), 4 from universal affirmative with each of the two particular conditionals (2 × 2) when the conditional's antecedent is negative; similarly for universal negative categorical. He then explains the types of conclusions: when the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent matches the conditional's antecedent in quantity, resulting in 8 subtypes. He illustrates with an example: 'Whenever or not at all if all J is B then H-Z, and all A is B' produces 'Whenever all J is A then H-Z' (or its negation), via a chain of reasoning involving the first figure. He also covers particular cases and concludes by stating that when the conditional is not universal and agreeing in quality, the conclusion is particular with a negative antecedent, obtained by reductio or other methods from the third figure (though the example is cut off). Throughout, the author is clarifying the logical structure of these syllogisms, providing the conditions for validity and enumerating all valid moods, with careful attention to quantity and quality.

الحملية
الضروب المنتجة
ثمانية وعشرون

The Syllogism
intermediate

Conclusions and Proof Methods in Second Figure Conditional Syllogisms
Author discusses types of conclusions and proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) for a given second figure mixed syllogism and use reductio (al-khalf) to verify it.

Core Idea
The conclusion's quantity and quality are determined by the conditional's antecedent and the categorical's quality, and reductio is the key proof method.

Once we know that a mood is productive, we must also determine what conclusion follows. The author explains that if the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, the conclusion is a universal conditional; otherwise, it is particular. He also describes how to prove these conclusions using reductio ad absurdum, a method that assumes the contradictory and derives an impossibility. This lesson will show you how to construct such proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Is the major premise universal or particular? What is the quality of its antecedent?
The antecedent is the part before 'then' (فـ).
2
Check agreement with categorical
Does the antecedent of the conditional agree in quality (affirmative/negative) with the categorical minor?
Agreement means both affirmative or both negative.
3
Determine conclusion type
If the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, then the conclusion is a universal conditional with the same quantity in the antecedent as the original conditional's antecedent. Otherwise, the conclusion is a particular conditional with a negative antecedent.
The author specifies these rules after enumerating the moods.
4
Prove by reductio
Assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion. Use that as a premise with the categorical major (or minor) in the second figure to derive an impossibility (e.g., a contradiction with the original premises). This proves the original conclusion.
The author says: 'البيان بالخلف كما إذا كان النقيض كبرى صغرى القياس من الثاني فإنه ينتج سلب ملازمة...'
5
Apply to a specific example
Use the author's example: 'كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل ج ب فـ هـ ز، وكل أ ب' produces 'كلما كان كل ج أ فـ هـ ز'. Trace the reductio: assume the contradictory and show it leads to contradiction.
This example is from the text.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the second figure of mixed syllogisms (the one that combines a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise). Enumerating the 28 Productive Moods in Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates productive moods of mixed syllogisms by quantity and quality conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 28 productive moods of mixed syllogisms (categorical + conditional) and identify the conditions on quantity and quality.

Core Idea
Twenty-eight productive moods arise from combining categorical and conditional premises under specific quantity and quality conditions.

After establishing the general conditions for productive syllogisms, the author now lists exactly 28 moods that work. This enumeration is crucial for ensuring we don't waste time on invalid combinations. We'll break down the counts by categorical type (universal affirmative/negative) and conditional type (universal/particular) with conditions on the antecedent's quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions: quantity and quality
The first condition is that the categorical premise must be either universal affirmative or universal negative; the second concerns the quality of the conditional's antecedent (must be negative for affirmative categoricals, affirmative for negative categoricals).
These conditions eliminate certain combinations, yielding only 28 out of many possibilities.
2
Count moods for universal affirmative categorical
From the text: eight moods with universal conditionals (4 each from two types) and four moods with particular conditionals (when antecedent is negative). That gives 12.
The text says 'eight from the universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods' – meaning 8 total for universal conditionals, plus 4 for particular conditionals.
3
Count moods for universal negative categorical
Similarly, eight moods with universal conditionals and four with particular conditionals (when antecedent is affirmative), making 12.
4
Count moods for particular categorical
Four moods when the particular categorical major (affirmative or negative) combines with universal conditionals, given agreement of non-occurrence.
This brings the total to 12 + 12 + 4 = 28.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيف. وباعتبار هذين الشرطين فالضروب المنتجة ثمانية وعشرون ثمانية من الحملية الموجبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في ضروبها الأربعة، وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها سالبًا كليا أو سالبا جزئيًا، وثمانية من الحملية السالبة الكلية مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة، وأربعة منها مع كل واحدة من الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجباً كليا أو موجباً جزئياً. وأما النتائج فإن كانت المتّصلة كلية، ومقدمها موافق للحملية في الكيف؛ فالنتيجة متصلة كلية مقدمها موافق لمقدم المتّصلة في الكم لما أن مقدم النتيجة مع الكبرى الحملية مستلزم لمقدم الصغرى من الشكل الأول وذلك ثمانية أضرب؛ أربعة من الكبرى الحملية الموجبة الكلية، وأربعة من الكبرى السالبة كذلك إذا كان مقدمها سالبًا كما في قولنا كلَّما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج ب» فـ «هز»، وكل أب ينتج كلما كان كل (ج) أ» فـ «هز» أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج أ» فـ «هز» لأنه كلما كان كل «ج أ» فكل « هـ أ» وكل «أب»، وكلَّما كان كذلك كان كل ج ب من الأول ينتج كلما كان كل (ج) أ) فكل ج ب». وإنه مع الصغرى الموجبة والسالبة ينتج المطلوب من الأول. وكذلك إذا كان مقدم المتصلة موجباً جزئياً لكن مقدم النتيجة موجب جزئي. وعلى هذا في قولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من «ج ب» فـ هـ ز»، ولا شيء من «أب» ينتج كلَّما كان كل ج أ» فـ هـز أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج أ» فـ «هز» لما مر .وكذلك إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة سالبا جزئياً لكن مقدم النتيجة موجب جزئي حينئذ؛ هذا إذا كانت المتصلة كلية موافقة لمقدم الحملية في الكيف. فأما إذا لم تكن فالنتيجة جزئية مقدمها سالب موافق لمقدم المتصلة في الكم بالخلف وغيره من الثالث كقولنا كلَّما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان كل «ج ب» فـ «هز»، ولا شيء من «أب» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من «ج أ» فـ «هز» أو قد لا يكون إذا كان لا شيء من (ج) أ) فـ هـز) وإلا لصدق نقيضه. والصغرى معه ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان كل ج ب فلا شيء من «ج أ»؛ وإنه باطل لأنه كل ج ب» فلا شيء من «ج أ لصدق الحملية. وإنتاجها / مع المقدم تاليها من [٢١٤] الثاني ثمَّ هذه الصادقة مع الصغرى تنتج المطلوب من الثالث. وعلى هذا في سائر الضروب ولأن الكبرى الحملية الجزئية موجبة وسالبة مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين إذا كان عدم المتّصلة موافقا للحملية في الكم والكيف: وذلك أربعة أضرب ينتج متصلة جزئية مقدمها موجب جزئي بالبيان الذي مرَّ ذكره في النتيجة من الحملية الموجبة الجزئية في الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان أو ليس البتة إذا كان بعض (ج) (ب) فـ هـز ، وبعض أب ينتج قد يكون إذا كان بعض (ج) أ) فـ هـز أو قد لا يكون إذا كان بعض (ج) أ» فـ «هز» لأنه كلَّما كان كل «أج» فبعض ج ب لإنتاج مقدمها في الحملية إياه من الثالث. وإنه مع الكبرى ينتج كلما كان كل «أج» فـ هـز» أو ليس البتة؛ وكل واحد منهما مع عكس المقدم المطلوب. وعلى هذا في الضربين الآخرين، والنتيجة دائمًا نحو المتصلة في الكيف.
Quality. And considering these two conditions, the productive moods are twenty-eight: eight from the universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal negative or particular negative; and eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in the four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, then the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent agrees with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity, because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the major categorical entails the antecedent of the minor in the first figure; and that is eight moods: four from the universal affirmative major categorical, and four from the universal negative likewise if its antecedent is negative, as in our saying 'Whenever or never if every B is C, then Z, and every A is B' yields 'Whenever every A is C, then Z' or 'Never if every A is C, then Z' because whenever every A is C, then every A is B and every B is C, and whenever that is the case, then every A is C from the first figure yields 'Whenever every A is C, then every A is B'. And it together with the affirmative and negative minor yields the required from the first figure. Likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, then the antecedent of the conclusion is particular affirmative. And similarly in our saying 'Whenever or never if no B is C, then Z, and no A is B' yields 'Whenever every A is C, then Z' or 'Never if every A is C, then Z' as passed. And likewise if the antecedent of the conditional is particular negative, then the antecedent of the conclusion is particular affirmative at that time. This is when the conditional is universal agreeing with the antecedent of the categorical in quality. But if not, then the conclusion is particular with a negative antecedent agreeing with the antecedent of the conditional in quantity, by reductio and others from the third figure, as in our saying 'Whenever or never if every B is C, then Z, and no A is B' yields 'Maybe if no A is C, then Z' or 'Maybe not if no A is C, then Z' otherwise its contradictory would be true. And the minor with it yields 'Never if every B is C, then no A is C'; and it is false because 'every B is C, so no A is C' due to the truth of the categorical. And its production together with the antecedent of its consequent from the second [214], then this true one together with the minor yields the required from the third figure. And similarly in all the moods. And because the particular categorical major, affirmative and negative, with each of the two universal conditionals, if the non-occurrence of the conditional agrees with the categorical in quantity and quality: that is four moods yielding a particular conditional whose antecedent is particular affirmative by the explanation that passed concerning the conclusion from the particular affirmative categorical in the first figure, as in our saying 'Whenever or never if some B is C, then Z, and some A is B' yields 'Maybe if some A is C, then Z' or 'Maybe not if some A is C, then Z' because whenever every A is C, then some B is C, for its antecedent in the categorical produces it from the third figure. And it together with the major yields 'Whenever every A is C, then Z' or 'Never'; and each of them together with the converse of the antecedent yields the required. And similarly in the other two moods, and the conclusion always resembles the conditional in quality.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the productive moods in mixed syllogisms where one premise is a categorical (حمليه) and the other is a conditional (متصلة), focusing on the first two figures of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating two conditions regarding quantity and quality. Then he counts the total productive moods as twenty-eight, breaking them down by combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative categorical and conditional premises. He then explains how the conclusion is formed when the conditional is universal and its antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, giving examples with variables (أ، ب، ج، هـ). The author outlines eight moods for universal categoricals with universal conditionals, then discusses cases where agreement does not hold, leading to particular conclusions via reductio (الخلف) or the third figure. He further explains particular categorical majors with universal conditionals, yielding four moods with particular conditional conclusions. The commentary should follow the text's step-by-step enumeration, clarifying each combination and the reasoning behind the conclusions. For instance, when the conditional is universal and its antecedent matches the categorical in quality, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent matches the conditional's antecedent in quantity—this is demonstrated by showing that the antecedent of the conclusion together with the major categorical entail the antecedent of the minor in the first figure. The author also provides counterfactual reasoning for cases of mismatch.

The Syllogism

Deriving the Conclusion in Mixed Syllogisms: Universal vs Particular
Author enumerates productive moods of mixed syllogisms by quantity and quality conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the conclusion of a mixed syllogism (categorical + conditional) is universal or particular based on the agreement of the conditional's antecedent with the categorical in quality.

Core Idea
When the conditional's antecedent agrees with the categorical in quality, the conclusion is universal; when they disagree, it is particular.

After listing the productive moods, the author explains how to derive the conclusion. The key is whether the antecedent of the conditional matches the categorical in quality (both affirmative or both negative). If they match, the conclusion is a universal conditional; if not, the conclusion is particular and proven via reductio. Let's examine this reasoning with examples.

Guided Path
1
Check agreement of quality
Look at the categorical premise and the antecedent of the conditional. Are they both affirmative or both negative? If yes, the conclusion is universal; if no, it is particular.
2
If agreement: universal conclusion
The conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent matches the conditional's antecedent in quantity. The proof: the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical major entail the antecedent of the minor in the first figure.
Example: 'Whenever every B is C, then Z' (conditional) + 'Every A is B' (categorical) → 'Whenever every A is C, then Z'.
3
If disagreement: particular conclusion
The conclusion is a particular conditional (maybe or maybe not) with a negative antecedent. The proof uses reductio: assume the opposite, derive a contradiction, then the original follows. This is illustrated via the third figure.
Example: 'Whenever every B is C, then Z' (affirmative) + 'No A is B' (negative) → 'Maybe if no A is C, then Z' or 'Maybe not...'.
4
Apply the rule to the enumerated moods
In the 28 moods, half will have agreement and yield universal conclusions, the other half disagreement yield particular conclusions.
The text specifies which moods produce which.

Third Figure Conditions and Productive Moods
Author states conditions for the third figure and enumerates forty productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for productivity in the third figure and enumerate the first two categories of productive moods among the forty.

Core Idea
The third figure's productivity hinges on two conditions and yields exactly forty moods.

This passage presents the conditions for productive mixed syllogisms in the third figure (الشكل الثالث) and begins listing the moods. As a memorization anchor, it requires careful attention to the classification of universal and particular premises. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly constructing valid syllogisms in Kalam and fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The author mentions two conditions: first, the universal conditional must have a negative antecedent, and that antecedent must not be 'nobler' (أشرف) than the categorical premise. Second, either the categorical or the conditional must be universal, or the antecedent of the conditional must be universal.
The term 'ashraf' is rare; it likely means more universal or general.
2
Understand the 'nobler' condition
The first condition restricts when the conditional's antecedent can be more universal than the categorical premise. This is a specific restriction for the third figure to avoid invalid moods.
3
Count moods from universal categoricals with universal conditionals
The author says: 'sixteen from each of the two universal categoricals with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods.' This yields 2 (categoricals) × 2 (conditionals) × 4 (moods) = 16 moods.
Each combination produces four moods.
4
Count moods from universal categoricals with particular conditionals
There are 8 moods from each universal categorical with each particular conditional when the antecedent is universal affirmative or particular affirmative. That is 2 (categoricals) × 2 (conditionals) × 2 (antecedent types) = 8.
The antecedent must be affirmative (universal or particular).
5
Note the incomplete enumeration
The text breaks off at 'twelve from each of the two conditionals', suggesting a third category of moods. The total should be 40: 16 + 8 + 16? Or 16+8+12? Possibly the missing part explains the last 12. Be aware of the cut-off.
The original text ends abruptly on page 194.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما الشكل الثالث فشرط إنتاجه على قسمين أيضًا؛ أحدهما أن تكون المتصلة الكلية إذا كان المقدم سالبا، ولا يكون مقدمها أشرف الحملية حينئذ. وثانيهما أن تكون الحملية أو المتصلة كلية أو يكون المقدم كليا. والضروب المنتجة باعتبار هذين الشرطين أربعون؛ ستة عشر من كل واحدة من الحمليتين الكليتين مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الكليتين في الضروب الأربعة. وثمانية من كل واحدة من الحمليتين الكليتين مع كل واحدة من المتصلتين الجزئيتين إذا كان مقدمها موجباً كليا أو جزئيا. واثنا عشر من كل واحدة من المتصلتين
[T] As for the third figure, its condition for productivity is also of two types: one is that the universal conditional is when its antecedent is negative, and its antecedent is not nobler than the categorical at that time. The second is that either the categorical or the conditional is universal, or the antecedent is universal. The productive moods considering these two conditions are forty: sixteen from each of the two universal categoricals with each of the two universal conditionals in their four moods, and eight from each of the two universal categoricals with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and twelve from each of the two conditionals...
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage introduces the third figure (الشكل الثالث) of mixed syllogisms (categorical and conditional). The author states two conditions for productivity: first, the universal conditional must have a negative antecedent, and that antecedent must not be nobler (أشرف) than the categorical; second, at least one premise (categorical or conditional) must be universal, or the antecedent must be universal. Under these conditions, the total productive moods are forty. The breakdown begins: sixteen moods come from each universal categorical paired with each universal conditional in four moods; eight from each universal categorical with each particular conditional when its antecedent is universal or particular affirmative; twelve more are mentioned but the text cuts off. The lesson presents these as a memorization anchor for the student to learn the possibilities. In practice, a student of logic would need to understand how these moods produce conclusions in the third figure.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Conditions and Productive Moods of the Third Figure in Mixed Syllogisms
Author sets two conditions and enumerates forty productive moods for the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for productive moods in the third figure of mixed syllogisms, enumerate the forty productive moods, and determine the conclusion type based on the premises.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms in the third figure require two conditions and yield forty moods.

The third figure of mixed syllogisms combines categorical and conditional premises. This passage lays out the exact conditions under which such syllogisms are productive, then enumerates all forty valid moods and describes how the conclusion is formed depending on the nature of the conditional premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Conditions
Read the first sentence of the Arabic. The author states two conditions for a productive mood: (1) if the conditional is universal, its antecedent must be negative, and the antecedent must not be 'nobler' than the categorical; (2) either the categorical or the conditional must be universal, or the antecedent must be universal.
The concept of 'nobler' refers to the categorical being more general or prior in order.
2
Break Down the Counting of Moods
The author gives a numerical breakdown: 16 from two universal categoricals with two universal conditionals in four moods; 8 from universal categoricals with particular conditionals under a condition; 12 from particular conditionals with universal conditionals; 4 from particular categoricals. Validate that these sum to 40.
The breakdown presupposes the two conditions are met; the moods are those that satisfy them.
3
Understand the Conclusion Rules
The passage distinguishes two cases for the conclusion. If the conditional is universal with a negative antecedent, the conclusion is universal with an antecedent opposite in quality to the categorical. If not, the conclusion is particular with an antecedent agreeing in quality.
The author uses the phrase 'mukhālifa li-l-ḥamliyya fī al-kayf' (opposite in quality) and 'muwāfiq' (agreeing).
4
Trace the Example with Variables
The passage illustrates with 'if every B is C then H is Z' and 'every B is A'. Work through how the conclusion 'if some C is A then H is Z' is derived by reduction to the impossible using the second and third figures.
The example shows the reasoning for the case where the conditional is not universal with a negative antecedent.

As for the third figure, the condition for its production is also in two parts: one is that the universal conditional be such that its antecedent is negative, and its antecedent not be nobler than the categorical at that time. The second is that the categorical or the conditional be universal, or the antecedent be universal. The productive moods according to these two conditions are forty: sixteen from each of the two universal categoricals with each of the two universal conditionals in the four moods; eight from each of the two universal categoricals with each of the two particular conditionals when their antecedent is universal positive or particular; twelve from each of the two particular conditionals with each of the two universal conditionals in three moods—those in which the antecedent of the conditional is not universally negative—and four from each of the two particular categoricals when their antecedent is universally positive. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal with a negative antecedent, then the conclusion is universal with a universal antecedent opposite in quality to the categorical, because the antecedent of the conclusion together with the categorical entails the minor premise from the second [figure]; and its production with the minor yields the desired from the first; that is in twelve moods: eight from the universal minor, positive and negative, with the major categorical likewise, and four from the universal minor, positive and negative, with the categorical; and similarly for the particular. And so on for the rest of the moods. The demonstration for all is as previously. As for when the conditional is not universal with a negative antecedent, the conclusion is particular with a particular antecedent agreeing with the categorical in quality, by reduction to the impossible and other [methods] from the third [figure], as in our saying: 'Whenever it is the case that or not at all that if every B is C, then H is Z, and every B is A' yields it may be that if some C is A then H is Z, or it may not be that if some C is A then H is Z; otherwise the minor together with the [assumption of] truth would yield from the second [figure] 'Not at all that if every B is C then some C is A'; and that is false because whenever every B is C, then some C is A, since its antecedent together with the categorical produces its consequent from the third figure; and this true [proposition] together with the minor also produces the desired from the third figure, positively and negatively. And so on for the rest of the moods.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for productive moods in the third figure of mixed syllogisms (combining categorical and conditional premises). The author first states two conditions: (1) If the conditional premise is universal, its antecedent must be negative, and the antecedent must be lower in rank than the categorical (i.e., the categorical is more general)? (2) Either the categorical or the conditional must be universal, or the antecedent of the conditional must be universal. Then he enumerates the total productive moods: forty in all, broken down by combinations of universal/particular categoricals and universal/particular conditionals. For each combination, the number of moods is specified (16, 8, 12, 4). Then he discusses the nature of the conclusion based on whether the conditional is universal with a negative antecedent or not. When it is, the conclusion is universal with an antecedent opposite in quality to the categorical; when it is not, the conclusion is particular with an antecedent agreeing in quality. He illustrates with an example using variables (B, C, A, H, Z) and shows how the reduction to the impossible works. The commentary follows the author's enumeration and explanation, preserving the logical precision without adding external examples.

Conditions and Products of the Fourth Figure with Conditional Premises
Author details the three conditions for productive syllogisms in the fourth figure with categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid fourth-figure syllogism with a conditional major and categorical minor, and derive the conclusion using the given rules.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires specific conditions to produce a valid conclusion from a conditional major and categorical minor.

This lesson examines the fourth figure of syllogism when the major premise is a conditional (muttasila) and the minor is categorical (hamliyya). The author spells out three conditions that must be satisfied for the syllogism to be productive, and then enumerates the resulting moods. Understanding these conditions is essential for applying syllogistic logic in Islamic sciences, particularly in legal reasoning where conditional arguments appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Find the three conditions listed in the passage. First: neither the categorical nor the antecedent of a particular conditional can be particular negative. Second: if the conditional is particular, its antecedent must be universal affirmative or opposite in quality to the universal categorical. Third: if the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, the categorical must be universal.
These conditions ensure that the syllogism avoids certain invalid combinations.
2
Verify the count of productive moods
The author states 32 productive moods based on the conditions. Break down the enumeration: e.g., 8 moods from universal affirmative categorical with each universal conditional in the four figures, etc. Check that the count matches the conditions.
The count includes variations in quantity and quality of the conditional and categorical.
3
Analyze the conclusion rules
When the conditional is universal with a universal negative antecedent and the categorical is universal negative, the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is determined by conversion and entailment. Similarly for other combinations.
The conclusion often involves reversing or modifying the antecedent via the categorical.
4
Work through an example
Follow the author's first example: 'Whenever if no B is C then H is Z, and no A is B' yields 'Whenever every C is A then H is Z'. See how intermediate steps use conversion and syllogistic figures.
The example uses abstract terms (أ, ب, ج, هـ, ز) to illustrate the logical flow.

As for the fourth figure, the condition for its productivity is divided into three parts: First, that neither the categorical nor the antecedent of the particular conditional be particular negative. Second, that its antecedent be universal affirmative or opposite in quality to the universal categorical if the conditional is particular. Third, that the categorical be universal if the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative. Considering these conditions, the productive moods are thirty-two: eight from the universal affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in the four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or universal negative. Eight from the universal negative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in the four moods, and four from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative or particular. Six from the particular affirmative categorical with each of the two universal conditionals in three moods, which are those where the antecedent is not universal negative. And two from it with each of the two particular conditionals if its antecedent is universal affirmative. As for the conclusions: if the conditional is universal and its antecedent is universal negative, and the categorical is universal negative, or the antecedent is particular affirmative or particular negative, and the categorical is universal affirmative, then the conclusion is universal, its antecedent universal when the antecedent is negated and opposite to the antecedent when the categorical is negated, and universal and particular when it is affirmed, and agreeing with the categorical, because the antecedent of the conclusion with the categorical major produces the antecedent of the minor or its converse or what is entailed by it from the four figures. That is ten moods: four from the first condition, four from the second, and six from the third—as in our statement: 'Whenever it is the case, or never is it the case, that if no B is C then H is Z, and no A is B' produces 'Whenever it is the case that every C is A then H is Z'; or 'Never is it the case that if every C is A then H is Z' because it is true that 'Whenever every C is A then every C is A and no A is B', and 'Whenever it is so, then no C is B' produces 'Whenever every C is A then no C is B', and 'Whenever no C is B then no B is C' produces 'Whenever every C is A then no B is C'; and that with the minor produces the desired. Similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is the case, or never is it the case, that if some B is C then H is Z, and every A is B' produces 'Whenever some C is A then H is Z' or 'Never is it the case that if some C is A then H is Z' as before. Similarly in our statement: 'Whenever it is the case, or never is it the case, that if not some'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage discusses the valid moods of the fourth figure when the major premise is a conditional (mutasila) and the minor is categorical (hamliya). The author first states the three conditions that must hold for the syllogism to produce a conclusion: (1) Neither the categorical minor nor the antecedent of a particular conditional can be particular negative. (2) If the conditional is particular, its antecedent must be universal affirmative or opposite in quality to the universal categorical. (3) If the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, the categorical must be universal. Given these conditions, the number of productive moods is 32. The author then enumerates them based on the quantity and quality of the categorical and conditional premises. Next, the conclusions are described: when the conditional is universal with a universal negative antecedent and the categorical is universal negative (or the antecedent is particular affirmative/negative with universal affirmative categorical), the conclusion is a universal conditional whose antecedent is determined by rules of conversion and entailment. The author provides two detailed examples: first with universal negative premises, and second with particular affirmative premises, showing how the conclusion is derived via intermediate syllogisms. The examples use abstract terms (أ, ب, ج, هـ, ز) but illustrate the logical flow. Throughout, the text demonstrates the systematic enumeration of moods and the reliance on conversion and syllogistic figures to justify conclusions.
